Conditions for the Martyr Exempt from Ghusl
Definition and conditions of the shahid exempt from ghusl with intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the three conditions for a shahid to be exempt from ghusl according to the Hanafi school and explain the intra-Hanafi disagreement regarding the weapon condition.

Core Idea
A martyr is not washed only if killed unjustly by a cutting weapon with no direct monetary compensation due.

The term 'shahid' in Hanafi fiqh refers specifically to a Muslim killed in battle under certain conditions. Not every battlefield death qualifies for the exemption from ritual washing. This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'aya explains the three conditions derived from the matn and highlights a significant internal disagreement between Abu Hanifa and his two companions regarding the type of weapon that triggers the martyrdom ruling. Understanding these conditions is crucial for correctly applying the funeral rites in cases of battlefield deaths.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Three Conditions
Extract from the matn the three conditions: killed with a hadidah (iron weapon), killed unjustly (not as hadd or qisas), and no monetary compensation is due for the killing itself.
The third condition is nuanced: it excludes cases where the killing directly obligates payment, but not cases where payment is a substitute for dropped qisas (like a father killing his son).
2
Analyze the Intra-Hanafi Dispute on Hadidah
Compare Abu Hanifa's view (only hadidah qualifies) with the two companions' view (any weapon qualifies). Understand the underlying reasoning: Abu Hanifa distinguishes between 'amd (intentional) with a cutting tool and shibh amd (quasi-intentional) with a heavy object; the companions consider all intentional killing as 'amd.
This dispute has practical consequences: a person killed with a large stone is a shahid according to the companions but not according to Abu Hanifa.
3
Understand the Exclusion of Hadd and Qisas
The second condition excludes those executed as a hadd punishment (e.g., for zina) or as qisas. Such persons are not considered martyrs and must be washed and shrouded.
This ensures that only those killed unjustly, without a legal basis, qualify.
4
Examine the Third Condition – No Monetary Compensation
The third condition excludes killings that directly obligate payment. The father-son exception illustrates that if qisas is dropped due to a textual exception, the payment does not nullify martyrdom because the obligation did not arise from the killing itself.
The hadith of Hanzalah (who was in janabah and washed by angels) supports that martyrdom prevents new obligations but does not remove existing ones.
5
Apply the Proof: Hadith of Hanzalah
Analyze the dalil: the hadith shows that Hanzalah, a martyr, was in janabah and needed washing. This indicates that martyrdom prevents the obligation of ghusl from arising for those not already obligated, but does not remove an existing obligation. This supports the third condition (no monetary compensation due by the killing itself, i.e., no pre-existing obligation).
The hadith is narrated by al-Tabarani, Ibn Hibban, and al-Hakim; its thubut is zanni.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وبالحديدة : احتراز عن القتل بالمثقل". وظلماً : احتراز عن القتل حداً، أو قصاصاً. ولم يجب به مال : احتراز عن قتل وجب به مال ، والمراد أنَّ المال يجب بنفس القتل، فإن الأب إذا قَتَلَ ابنه بحديدة ظلماً يكون الابن شهيداً ; لأن المال وإن وجب، فإنَّه لم يجب بنفس هذا القتل. وله : أنَّ الشَّهادة مانعة عن وجوب غسل الميت لا رافعة لما وجب قبله، ويشهد له ما روي «أنَّ حنظلة استشهد في غزوة أحد وكان جنباً فغسلته الملائكة»، أخرجه الطبراني في «معجمه»، وابن حبان والحاكم. [١] قوله : عن القتل بالمثقل ; مفعول من التثقيل ; أي شيء ثقيل يقع به القتل غالباً كالحجر الكبير، ومثله يسمّى شبه العمد، ولا يجب به القصاص بل المال، وإنّما يجب القصاص في القتل العمد، وهو ما إذا تعمد ضربه بسلاح كالسيف وما يجري مجراه في تفريق الأجزاء كالمحدد من الخشب أو الحجر، وهو المراد بالحديدة، هذا كله عنده ، وعندهما في الكلِّ القصاص، فلا يقيَّدُ تعريف الشهيد عندهما بحديدة. [٢]قوله : عن القتل حداً ; فمَن قُتِلَ في حد الزنا أو قُتِل قصاصاً عوضاً عن لرجل فليس بشهيد فيُغسلُ ويُكفِّن. قتله [٣) قوله : عن قتل.... الخ ; كالقتل بالحجر الصغير ونحوه مما لا يقتل به غالباً، وكالقتل الخطأ كان رمى الصيد فأصابَ إنساناً وقتله، فإنَّ الواجب في هذه الصور الدية لا القصاص، وتفصيلُه في «الفرائض السراجية» (۲) ، وشرحها «الشريفي»(۳)، وغيرهما. [٤ قوله : فإن لم تجب بنفس هذا القتل ; لأنَّ القتل بالحديدة عمداً ظلماً موجبه القصاص في الأصل، وإنّما سقط بورودِ نص دال على أنَّ الوالد لا يقتل بولده تكريماً له، فيجب المال حذراً عن بطلان دم المقتول بالكلية.
And 'by an iron weapon': a precaution against killing with a heavy object. And 'unjustly': a precaution against killing as a hadd punishment or qisas. And 'no monetary compensation is obligatory for it': a precaution against killing for which monetary compensation is obligatory. The meaning is that the monetary compensation is obligatory by the killing itself. For if a father kills his son with an iron weapon unjustly, the son is a martyr, because even though compensation is obligatory, it is not obligatory by virtue of this killing itself. And for him (Abu Hanifa): that martyrdom prevents the obligation of washing the deceased, it does not remove what was obligatory before it. This is supported by what is narrated: 'Hanzalah was martyred in the Battle of Uhud while he was in a state of janabah, so the angels washed him.' It was narrated by al-Tabarani in his Mu'jam, Ibn Hibban, and al-Hakim. [1] His statement: 'against killing with a heavy object' – a verbal noun from tathqil, i.e., any heavy object by which killing mostly occurs, like a large stone. The like of this is called shibh amd (quasi-intentional), and neither qisas nor monetary compensation is obligatory for it. Rather, qisas is obligatory only in intentional killing, which is when he intentionally strikes him with a weapon like a sword or what runs in its course in separating limbs, like a pointed piece of wood or stone. This is what is meant by 'hadidah.' All this according to him (Abu Hanifa). According to the two companions, qisas is obligatory in all cases, so the definition of martyr is not restricted to iron weapon according to them. [2] His statement: 'against killing as a hadd punishment' – so whoever is killed in the hadd punishment for zina or killed as qisas in place of a man is not a martyr, so he is washed and shrouded. [3] His statement: 'against killing... etc.' – like killing with a small stone and similar that does not usually kill, and like accidental killing, such as shooting at game but hitting a person and killing him. In these cases, the obligatory (compensation) is blood money, not qisas. Its detail is in 'al-Fara'id al-Sirajiyyah' and its commentary 'al-Sharifi' and others. [4] His statement: 'for it (monetary compensation) is not obligatory by virtue of this killing itself' – because killing with an iron weapon intentionally and unjustly, its original consequence is qisas. It is only dropped due to the arrival of a text indicating that a father is not killed for his son as an honor for him. So monetary compensation becomes obligatory as a precaution against the complete nullification of the blood of the killed person.
Source pages p. 374

Commentary
This passage is a commentary (sharh) on a matn that defines the conditions for a martyr (shahid) who is exempt from the ritual washing (ghusl) of the deceased. The matn states three conditions: the killing must be with a hadidah (iron weapon), it must be unjust (dhulman), and no monetary compensation (mal) is due for the killing. The commentator expands each condition. The first condition (hadidah) excludes killing with a heavy object (muthaqqal), which is considered shibh amd (quasi-intentional) by Abu Hanifa and does not carry qisas. Abu Hanifa restricts the definition of shahid to those killed with a cutting iron weapon. However, the two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani) hold that qisas is due in all intentional killings, so they do not restrict the martyr to an iron weapon. The second condition (unjustly) excludes those killed as a hadd punishment or qisas, as they are not martyrs. The third condition (no monetary compensation due) excludes cases where the killing itself obligates a payment, such as when a father kills his son: the father does not face qisas (due to a textual exception) but must pay compensation; however, this compensation is not by virtue of the killing itself (since qisas was dropped), so the son is still a martyr. The text then cites the hadith of Hanzalah, who was in janabah when martyred and was washed by angels, proving that martyrdom prevents the obligation of ghusl from arising but does not remove an existing obligation. This supports the condition that if a monetary obligation already existed (like janabah ghusl), it remains. The subsequent commentary numbers ([1] to [4]) further elaborate on each point, including the distinction between intentional and quasi-intentional killing, the definition of hadidah, and the reasoning behind the father-son exception.

Key Terms
شهيد (shahid): One who is killed unjustly in battle by enemy forces; according to the text, with specific conditions: killed with an iron weapon (hadidah) according to Abu Hanifa, or any weapon according to the companions, and not in qisas or hadd punishment, and no monetary compensation owed.
حديدة (hadidah): An iron weapon that cuts, such as a sword, or any sharp object that separates body parts, including sharp wood or stone. This is the condition for shahid according to Abu Hanifa.
المثقل (al-muthaqqal): A heavy object that causes death by its weight, like a large stone. This is excluded from shahid by Abu Hanifa because such killing is shibh amd (quasi-intentional) and requires diyah, not qisas.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the consequence if a father unjustly kills his son with an iron weapon?
A) The father is executed as qisas, and the son is not a shahid.
B) The father pays blood money (diyah), and the son is a shahid.
C) The son is not a shahid because monetary compensation is due.
D) The father is not punished, and the son is not a shahid.

Key Takeaway
A Muslim killed unjustly in battle is not washed if the killing meets strict conditions: iron weapon, unjust, no direct monetary compensation; the weapon condition is disputed among Hanafi imams.

Martyrdom of the Wounded in Battle
Explaining that a wounded body found in battle is a shahid regardless of weapon

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the conditions for a person found dead in battle to be considered a shahid and explain the Hanafi proof from the Sunnah.

Core Idea
A wound on a body found in battle is evidence of martyrdom, regardless of the weapon used.

The status of a person found dead in the battlefield with wounds raises a question: is he automatically a martyr? In this passage, the Hanafi school clarifies that the presence of a wound is key—it proves the enemy killed him, not natural causes. This status has practical effects, such as exemption from ritual washing. Let's examine the ruling and its evidence.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Act and Ruling
The act is finding a dead body with a wound in the battlefield. The ruling is that this person is a shahid (martyr) and thus not to be washed.
The condition is explicit: the wound must be present.
2
Locate the Dalil
The author cites the practice of the Prophet (peace be upon him) regarding the martyrs of Uhud: he buried them with their blood and did not wash them.
This hadith is reported in Sahih al-Bukhari and the four Sunan.
3
Trace the Wajh al-Dalalah
The Prophet's action shows that wounds are not a reason for washing; the presence of wounds indicates martyrdom. The fact that the Uhud martyrs were killed by various means (stones, sticks, etc.) proves that any weapon qualifies.
The proof also covers indirect killing (trampling, burning).
4
Note the Condition's Purpose
The wound is required only to distinguish being killed from natural death (hatf anfihi). If no wound is found, the default is natural death, and ghusl is required.
The wound can be internal, inferred from blood from orifices.
5
Evaluate the Strength of the Proof
The hadith is authentic (mutawatir in meaning), though the exact ruling is zanni in dalalah. The Hanafi school relies on this practice, and there is no significant disagreement among the major schools on this point.
This is a muttafaq alayh ruling.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقوله : أو وُجِدَ ميتاً : فإِنَّ مَن وُجِدَ ميتاً جريحاً في المعركة، فهو شهيد ; لأنَّ الظَّاهِرَ أنَّ أهلَ الحرب ،قتلوه، ومقتولهم شهيد بأي شيءٍ قتلوه"، وإنما شُرِطَ الجراحة" فيمن وُجد في المعركة ; ليدل على أنه قتيل لا ميت حنف أنفه. (۱) قوله : ومقتولهم ; مباشرة أو تسبيباً حتى لو أوطؤوا دوابهم مسلماً ، أو رموه من السُّور، أو ألقوا عليه حائطاً، أو أحرقوه بنار ، أو ما أشبه ذلك فمات كان شهيداً. كذا في «شرح الكنز» للزيلعي [٢]قوله : بأي شيءٍ قتلوه ; سواء كان جارحة، أو حجراً صغيراً، أو كبيراً، أو خشباً، أو نحو ذلك ; وذلك لأنه ورد في حـق شـهـداء أحد : «زملُـوهـم بكـلــومـهـم ودمائهم، أخرجه أحمد. وثبت في «صحيح البُخَارِيّ والسُنَنِ الأربعة : «إِنَّ النَّبي صلى الله عليه وسلم دفنهم بدمائهم، ولم يغسلهم»(۳)، ومن المعلوم أن كلهم لم يكن قتيل السيف والسلاح، بل منهم من دفع بالحجر ، ومنهم مَن قُتِلَ بالعصا، ونحو ذلك. كذا في «البناية»(4). [٣]قوله : الجراحة ; سواء كانت ظاهرة أو باطنة، ويستدل عليها بوجود الأثر، كما إذا سالَ الدَّمُ من عينه وإذنه أو حلقه. كذا في «الكافي». [٤]قوله : ليدل ; فإنّ وجود الجراحة دليل على أنه مات بقتل غيره لا بموت نفسه. [0] قوله : حتف أنفه ; الحتف : بالفتح : الهلاك، وكان أهل الجاهلية يعتقدون أنَّ عند الموت يخرج من الأنف ، فقالوا لمن مات من غير سبب ظاهر : مات حتف أنفه ; أي بموت نفسه.
And His statement: 'Or if he is found dead': For whoever is found dead wounded in the battlefield, he is a martyr, because the apparent (presumption) is that the people of war killed him, and their killed one is a martyr by whatever they killed him. And the condition of a wound is required for one found in the battlefield only to indicate that he is a killed person, not one who dies a natural death (hatf anfihi). (1) His statement: 'their killed one' – whether directly or by causation, even if they trample him with their animals, or throw him from a wall, or collapse a wall on him, or burn him with fire, or the like, and he dies, he is a martyr. This is in Sharh al-Kanz by al-Zayla'i. [2] His statement: 'by whatever they killed him' – whether with a weapon, a small stone, a large stone, wood, or the like; because it is narrated concerning the martyrs of Uhud: 'Wrap them with their wounds and blood,' narrated by Ahmad. And it is established in Sahih al-Bukhari and the four Sunan that 'the Prophet (peace be upon him) buried them with their blood and did not wash them.' It is well known that not all of them were killed by sword and weapon, but among them were those struck with a stone and those killed with a stick and the like. This is in al-Binayah. [3] His statement: 'a wound' – whether apparent or internal, and it is inferred by the presence of a trace, such as blood flowing from his eye, ear, or throat. This is in al-Kafi. [4] His statement: 'to indicate' – for the presence of a wound is evidence that he died by being killed by another, not by his own death. [0] His statement: 'hatf anfihi' – al-hatf (with fatha): destruction. The people of Jahiliyyah used to believe that at death, the soul exits from the nose. So they said of one who dies without apparent cause: 'He died hatf anfihi,' i.e., by his own death.
Source pages p. 375

Commentary
This passage continues the commentary on the definition of a shahid. It addresses the case of a person found dead in the battlefield with a wound. The text explains that such a person is presumed to have been killed by the enemy, and therefore is a martyr, regardless of the specific weapon used. The requirement of a wound is to distinguish being killed from dying naturally (hatf anfihi). The commentary expands by stating that the enemy can kill directly or by causation (e.g., trampling, throwing, burning). The proof is the practice of the Prophet (peace be upon him) regarding the martyrs of Uhud, who were buried with their wounds and blood without being washed, even though they had been killed by various means, not just swords. The wound may be apparent or internal, as indicated by blood from orifices. The term 'hatf anfihi' is explained as a natural death without apparent cause, rooted in pre-Islamic Arabian belief.

Key Terms
جراحة (jarahah): Wound, whether visible or internal, indicated by blood from eyes, ears, throat, etc.
حتف أنفه (hatf anfihi): A natural death without apparent cause, literally 'death from the nose,' i.e., dying of natural causes.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why is the presence of a wound required for a person found dead in the battlefield to be considered a shahid?
A) To ensure the person was killed by a weapon specifically.
B) To distinguish being killed by the enemy from dying naturally.
C) To prove that the person was a soldier.
D) To determine the exact cause of death for legal purposes.

Key Takeaway
A person found dead in battle with a wound is presumed to be a shahid, and ghusl is not required, based on the Prophet's practice with the Uhud martyrs.

Definition and Conditions of the Martyr (Shahid) in Hanafi Law
Defining the shahid and specifying its conditions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi definition of a shahid and identify the five conditions that determine whether a person is considered a shahid for the rulings of janazah (no washing, burial with blood).

Core Idea
A shahid is a Muslim adult killed unjustly without blood money due—not only in battle but by any fatal means.

The passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah defines the legal martyr (shahid) for funeral rulings. It refines the common battlefield image, establishing a broader definition based on hadith and qiyas. This lesson unpacks the preferred definition from al-Mukhtasar and its conditions.

Guided Path
1
Extract the preferred definition
Find the author's concise definition from al-Mukhtasar: 'He is a Muslim, pure, adult, killed unjustly, and no blood money is obligatory for him.' This is the core legal formula.
The definition excludes non-Muslims, children, those killed by right (e.g., qisas), and cases where diyah is due.
2
Examine each condition
Break down each condition: Muslim (believer), pure (not in a state of janabah? actually pure means free from major impurity at death, but the text interprets it as 'pure' from any disqualifying state, often taken as 'mukallaf'? The passage says 'what I said in al-Mukhtasar: Muslim, pure, adult'. So pure may refer to being free from impurity? Or it means 'tahir' i.e., not in a state that precludes shahada? Clarify: The condition 'pure' (طاهر) is often explained as 'tahir' meaning free from major ritual impurity (janabah) at the time of death, but the text does not elaborate; it is derived from the martyrs of Uhud who were in a state of purity. For our purposes, note it is a condition. Adult: legally responsible (mukallaf). Killed unjustly: not in lawful execution, self-defense, etc. No blood money due: no diyah on the killer or his ʿāqila.
The condition 'no blood money due' distinguishes shahid from accidental killing (where diyah is due).
3
Identify the primary proof
The hadith of the martyrs of Uhud: the Prophet buried them with their blood and did not wash them. This hadith is used as the foundation for the shahid ruling. The passage also notes that the wound (جراحة) is evidence of killing, not natural death.
The hadith is narrated in Sahih al-Bukhari and the Sunan. It is a hadith ahad (zanni al-thubut) but its meaning is decisive (qati' al-dalalah) according to the Hanafi school.
4
Apply the definition to various scenarios
The passage expands the definition to include those killed indirectly (by trampling, collapsing wall, fire, etc.) as long as it is unjust. The condition of 'no blood money' excludes cases where diyah is wajib. Practice applying the definition to: being killed by a robber with a stone, being killed in a car accident (not unjust, no killer), being executed by qisas.
Modern mapping: A Muslim adult killed unjustly in a terrorist attack (by explosion, gunfire) is a shahid if no diyah is claimed or due (the killer is unknown or dead). But if the killer is caught and diyah imposed, the victim is not a shahid in the worldly ruling.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقوله : أو وُجِدَ ميتاً : فإِنَّ مَن وُجِدَ ميتاً جريحاً في المعركة، فهو شهيد ؛ لأنَّ الظَّاهِرَ أنَّ أهلَ الحرب قتلوه، ومقتولهم شهيد بأي شيءٍ قتلوه، وإنما شُرِطَ الجراحة فيمن وُجِد في المعركة ؛ ليدل على أنه قتيل لا ميت حتف أنفه. ومقتولهم مباشرة أو تسبيباً حتى لو أوطؤوا دوابهم مسلماً، أو رموه من السُّور، أو ألقوا عليه حائطاً، أو أحرقوه بنار، أو ما أشبه ذلك فمات كان شهيداً. بأي شيءٍ قتلوه سواء كان جارحة، أو حجراً صغيراً، أو كبيراً، أو خشباً، أو نحو ذلك وذلك لأنه ورد في حـق شـهـداء أحد : «زملـوهـم بكـلـومـهـم ودمائهم»، أخرجه أحمد. وثبت في «صحيح البُخَارِيّ والسُنَنِ الأربعة : «إِنَّ النَّبي صلى الله عليه وسلم دفنهم بدمائهم، ولم يغسلهم»، ومن المعلوم أن كلهم لم يكن قتيل السيف والسلاح، بل منهم من دفع بالحجر، ومنهم مَن قُتِلَ بالعصا، ونحو ذلك. الجراحة سواء كانت ظاهرة أو باطنة، ويستدل عليها بوجود الأثر، كما إذا سالَ الدَّمُ من عينه وإذنه أو حلقه. ليدل فإنّ وجود الجراحة دليل على أنه مات بقتل غيره لا بموت نفسه. حتف أنفه الحتف بالفتح الهلاك وكان أهل الجاهلية يعتقدون أن عند الموت يخرج من الأنف فقالوا لمن مات من غير سبب ظاهر مات حتف أنفه أي بموت نفسه. فالحاصل أن الشهيد من قتل بحديدة ظلماً ولم يجب به مال أو مَن وُجِد ميتاً جريحاً في المعركة سواء قُتِلَ بحديدة أم لا. لكن في هذا التعريف نظر وهو أنه لا يشمل ما إذا قتله المشركون أو أهل البغي أو قطاع الطريق بغير الحديدة فإنَّ قتيلهم شهيد بأي آلة قتلوه. فالتعريف الحَسَنُ الموجز ما قُلْتُ في «المختصر » وهو مسلم طاهر بالغ قتل ظلماً ولم يجب به مال. كونَه شهيداً في حكم الدُّنيا وهو عدم الغسل والدفن بالدماء ونحو ذلك ثابت بأحاديث وردت في حق شهداء أحد وغيرهم والتحق به المقتول ظلماً بحديدة بقتل لم يجب به مال لأنه في معناه ومماثله وزيد قيد المسلم والطاهر والمكلّف لأن ما ورد فيه النَّص كان موصوفاً بهذه الصفات فلا يتعدى حكمهم إلى غيرهم. ما إذا قتله المشركون أي في غير المعركة ظلماً. أو أهل البغي هم الخارجون عن طاعة الإمام الحق. أو قطاع الطريق بضم القاف وتشديد الطاء المهملة جمع القاطع يعني الذين يقطعون طريق المسافرين ويأخذون أموالهم ظلماً. بغير الحديدة فإن كان بها فهو داخل في قول المصنف قتِلَ بحديدة. قتل ظلماً فإنه إذا قتل بحق رجم أو قصاص أو ببغي أو بقطع طريق فإنه يُغسل وكذا إذا قُتِلَ بشيء لا يوصف بالظلم كما إذا افترسَهُ السَّبع أو عليه البناء أو غرق ونحو ذلك. ولم يجب به قال البِرْجَنْدي أي لم يجب بقتله مال لا على القاتل ولا على غيره فخرج المقتول خطأ أو جار مجرى الخطأ فإن كل قتيل يتعلق به وجوب القصاص على قاتله فالمقتول شهيد وذلك إذا قتل بحديدة صغيرة أو كبيرة جرحت أو لا كذا إذا أحرقَهُ بالنَّار أو جرحه بالقصب أو بما في معنى الحديدة وأما المقتول بالنّقل فعنده يجب به المال فيغسل وعندهما يجب القصاص فلا يغسل. مال أي دية ولا يضر الدية الواجبة بالصلح أو بصيانة الدم عن الهدر.
His statement: 'or if found dead': So whoever is found dead with a wound in the battlefield is a martyr because the apparent [evidence] is that the people of war killed him, and their killed one is a martyr by whatever they killed him. And the condition of the wound is stipulated for one found in the battlefield to indicate that he is killed and not one who died by his own death. Their killed one [is made] directly or indirectly, even if they tread upon him with their mounts, or throw him from the wall, or collapse a wall on him, or burn him with fire, or similar, and he dies, he is a martyr. By whatever they killed him, whether a weapon or a small stone or a large one or wood or similar, and that is because it is reported concerning the martyrs of Uhud: 'Wrap them with their wounds and their blood' recorded by Ahmad. And it is established in Sahih al-Bukhari and the four Sunan: 'The Prophet (peace be upon him) buried them with their blood and did not wash them.' And it is known that not all of them were killed by sword and weapon, but among them were those struck with stone and those killed with a stick and similar. The wound whether apparent or internal, and it is inferred by the presence of the trace, such as if blood flows from his eye or ear or throat. 'To indicate'—the existence of the wound is evidence that he died by the killing of another, not by his own death. 'His own death'—al-hatf: by fath means perdition, and the people of Jahiliyyah believed that at death something comes out of the nose, so they said of one who died without apparent cause: 'He died by his own death' i.e. by his own self. So the conclusion is that the martyr is one killed by an iron [weapon] unjustly and no blood money is obligatory for him, or one found dead with a wound in the battlefield whether killed by an iron or not. But in this definition there is consideration: it does not include when the polytheists or rebels or highway robbers kill him without iron, for their killed one is a martyr by whatever instrument they kill him. So the good concise definition is what I said in al-Mukhtasar: 'He is a Muslim, pure, adult, killed unjustly, and no blood money is obligatory for him.' His being a martyr in the worldly ruling—that is, not washing and burial with blood and similar—is established by ahadith reported concerning the martyrs of Uhud and others, and analogically attached to it is the one killed unjustly by iron with a killing for which no blood money is obligatory, because he is in its meaning and similar, and the conditions of Muslim, pure, and legally responsible are added because the text that came was described with these attributes, so their ruling is not extended to others. 'When the polytheists kill him'—i.e. outside the battlefield unjustly. 'Or the rebels'—they are those who go out from obedience to the true Imam. 'Or highway robbers'—with damma on qaf and tashdid on the important ta', plural of qati': meaning those who cut the road of travelers and take their property unjustly. 'Without iron'—if with it, then it is included in the author's statement: 'killed by iron'. 'Killed unjustly'—if he is killed by right, such as stoning, qisas, rebellion, or highway robbery, then he is washed; similarly if he is killed by something not described as unjust, such as if a beast seizes him or a building falls on him or he drowns and similar. 'And no money is obligatory for him'—al-Birjandi said: meaning no blood money is obligatory by his killing, neither on the killer nor on another; so excluded is the one killed by mistake or in the course of mistake, for every killed person is attached to it the obligation of qisas on his killer; so the killed one is a martyr, and that is when he is killed by a small or large iron which wounds or not, similarly if he burns him with fire or wounds him with a reed or with something in the meaning of iron. As for the one killed by an arrow [with a blunt head?]—according to him (Abu Hanifa) blood money is obligatory, so he is washed; according to them both (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) qisas is obligatory, so he is not washed. 'Blood money'—i.e. diyah, and the diyah obligatory by settlement or for protection of blood from being nullified does not harm.
Source pages p. 375

Commentary
This passage is a definitional ruling from a Hanafi commentary on the chapter of martyrdom. It defines who is considered a shahid (martyr) in Islamic law and the conditions for the legal rulings of not being washed and being buried with blood. The author begins by explaining the statement from the matn: 'or found dead' in the battlefield with a wound. The reason is that the apparent evidence indicates the enemy killed him, and those killed by the enemy are martyrs regardless of the weapon. Even indirect killing, such as trampling or collapsing a wall, qualifies. The condition of a wound is to distinguish being killed from dying naturally. The author then cites the hadith of the martyrs of Uhud, where the Prophet buried them with their wounds and blood without washing, and notes that not all were killed by swords but by stones and sticks, so all are martyrs. The wound can be external or internal, indicated by blood flow. Next, the passage gives a comprehensive definition: a martyr is one killed unjustly by an iron weapon (hadidah) with no blood money due, or found dead with a wound in the battlefield. However, the author notes that this definition overlooks cases where polytheists, rebels, or highway robbers kill without iron—such killed ones are also martyrs. Therefore, the author's preferred concise definition from al-Mukhtasar is: 'A Muslim, pure, adult killed unjustly, and no blood money is obligatory for him.' The commentary then explains that the worldly ruling of martyrdom (no washing, burial with blood) is proven by hadith and is extended analogically to anyone killed unjustly with iron where blood money is not due, provided the person is Muslim, pure, and legally responsible. Excluded are those killed by right (e.g., in qisas) or by natural causes. The passage ends with an intra-Hanafi dispute: according to Abu Hanifa, killing with a blunt arrow (nathl) requires blood money, so the victim is washed; according to Abu Yusuf and Muhammad, qisas is required, so the victim is not washed. This shows that the category of shahid depends on whether blood money is mandatory or not.

Key Terms
شهيد (shahid): A person who is killed unjustly, does not die from natural causes, and for whom no blood money is obligatory, subject to conditions of being Muslim, pure, and adult.
الحديدة (al-hadidah): Iron weapon or instrument that can cause fatal injury; includes swords, arrows, bullets, etc.
المقتول ظلماً (al-maqtul zulman): One killed without legal justification (e.g., not in qisas, jihad, or capital punishment).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the preferred definition in the passage, which of the following persons is considered a shahid (martyr) who is not washed?
A) A Muslim man killed in a car accident
B) A Muslim adult killed unjustly by a stone, with no blood money due
C) A non-Muslim killed while fighting against Muslims
D) A Muslim killed in a legitimate qisas execution

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi shahid is defined by five conditions: Muslim, pure, adult, killed unjustly, and no blood money due; only then is the body not washed and buried with blood.

The Intra-Hanafi Dispute on the Blunt Arrow (Nathl) and the Shahid Ruling
Intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf on the shahid ruling for a blunt arrow victim.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the disagreement between Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn on whether a person killed by a blunt arrow (nathl) is washed, and apply the thamarah al-khilaf (practical consequence) to a real scenario.

Core Idea
The nathl dispute turns on whether blood money (diyah) or qisas is due – if diyah, the victim is washed; if qisas, he is a shahid.

The passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah presents a classic intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf regarding the definition of shahid. The dispute centers on a specific case: a person killed by a blunt arrow (nathl) that does not wound but kills. This lesson analyzes the positions, proofs, and practical outcome.

Guided Path
1
Identify the contested case
The case is killing by nathl (نَثْل) – an arrow with a blunt head that kills without wounding. This is distinct from a sharp arrow that wounds.
The text uses 'المقتول بالنّقل' (al-maqtul bi al-naql) which is a variant reading; the correct term is nathl.
2
State Abu Hanifa's position and reasoning
Abu Hanifa holds that blood money (diyah) is obligatory for killing by nathl. Therefore, the condition 'no blood money due' is violated, so the victim is not a shahid and must be washed.
The reasoning: since the arrow does not wound, it does not cause bleeding, so it is not analogous to killing by iron that wounds. The absence of qisas (retaliation) indicates diyah.
3
State the Sahibayn's position and reasoning
Abu Yusuf and Muhammad hold that qisas is obligatory for killing by nathl. Therefore, the killing is considered deliberate unjust killing, and since qisas is due (not diyah), the victim is a shahid (not washed).
They argue that the arrow is a weapon capable of causing death, and deliberate use makes qisas applicable, just like any other weapon.
4
Examine the basis of the disagreement
The disagreement stems from the illa (effective cause) for the shahid ruling. Abu Hanifa ties it to the absence of any financial liability (diyah), while the Sahibayn tie it to the absence of blood money specifically (since qisas is not money). The hadith of Uhud does not explicitly cover nathl, so qiyas is used.
This is a classic example of ikhtilaf in tahqiq al-manat (determining the precise legal cause).
5
Determine the practical consequence
The thamarah al-khilaf: In Abu Hanifa's view, the body is washed and the usual janazah performed. In the Sahibayn's view, the body is not washed and is buried with blood, as a shahid.
Although the passage does not specify the mufta bihi, many later Hanafi fatwas tend to follow the Sahibayn, but caution is needed.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقوله : أو وُجِدَ ميتاً : فإِنَّ مَن وُجِدَ ميتاً جريحاً في المعركة، فهو شهيد ؛ لأنَّ الظَّاهِرَ أنَّ أهلَ الحرب قتلوه، ومقتولهم شهيد بأي شيءٍ قتلوه، وإنما شُرِطَ الجراحة فيمن وُجِد في المعركة ؛ ليدل على أنه قتيل لا ميت حتف أنفه. ومقتولهم مباشرة أو تسبيباً حتى لو أوطؤوا دوابهم مسلماً، أو رموه من السُّور، أو ألقوا عليه حائطاً، أو أحرقوه بنار، أو ما أشبه ذلك فمات كان شهيداً. بأي شيءٍ قتلوه سواء كان جارحة، أو حجراً صغيراً، أو كبيراً، أو خشباً، أو نحو ذلك وذلك لأنه ورد في حـق شـهـداء أحد : «زملـوهـم بكـلـومـهـم ودمائهم»، أخرجه أحمد. وثبت في «صحيح البُخَارِيّ والسُنَنِ الأربعة : «إِنَّ النَّبي صلى الله عليه وسلم دفنهم بدمائهم، ولم يغسلهم»، ومن المعلوم أن كلهم لم يكن قتيل السيف والسلاح، بل منهم من دفع بالحجر، ومنهم مَن قُتِلَ بالعصا، ونحو ذلك. الجراحة سواء كانت ظاهرة أو باطنة، ويستدل عليها بوجود Commentary
This passage is a definitional ruling from a Hanafi commentary on the chapter of martyrdom. It defines who is considered a shahid (martyr) in Islamic law and the conditions for the legal rulings of not being washed and being buried with blood. The author begins by explaining the statement from the matn: 'or found dead' in the battlefield with a wound. The reason is that the apparent evidence indicates the enemy killed him, and those killed by the enemy are martyrs regardless of the weapon. Even indirect killing, such as trampling or collapsing a wall, qualifies. The condition of a wound is to distinguish being killed from dying naturally. The author then cites the hadith of the martyrs of Uhud, where the Prophet buried them with their wounds and blood without washing, and notes that not all were killed by swords but by stones and sticks, so all are martyrs. The wound can be external or internal, indicated by blood flow. However, the author notes that this definition overlooks cases where polytheists, rebels, or highway robbers kill without iron—such killed ones are also martyrs. This shows that the category of shahid depends on whether blood money is mandatory or not.

Key Terms
النَّثْل (al-nathl): An arrow with a blunt head that does not wound but can kill by blunt force.
الصاحبان (al-Sahibayn): Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani, the two main disciples of Abu Hanifa.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
The intra-Hanafi dispute on nathl turns on whether qisas or diyah is due: if qisas, the victim is a shahid (not washed); if diyah, he is washed.

The Hanafi Definition of a Martyr (Shahid) and Its Conditions
Defining the shahid with commentary on conditions and intra-Hanafi dispute

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi definition of a shahid (martyr) in worldly rulings, identify its five conditions, and explain the intra-Hanafi dispute regarding a Muslim killed by a dhimmi.

Core Idea
A shahid is a Muslim, pure, adult, killed unjustly, with no blood money due.

In this passage, the commentator refines the definition of a shahid, rejecting a narrow formulation that required an iron weapon and battlefield presence. Instead, he presents a concise set of conditions derived from the Prophetic practice concerning the martyrs of Uhud. He also addresses a key difference within the Hanafi school: whether a Muslim killed by a dhimmi with an iron weapon qualifies as a shahid. This lesson unpacks the definition, conditions, and the debated case.

Guided Path
1
Identify the initial definition and its critique
The commentator begins by presenting a definition: a shahid is one killed unjustly with an iron weapon, no blood money due, and not surviving the injury, or found dead in battle. He criticizes this for excluding cases where the killer is a polytheist, rebel, or highway robber using a non-iron weapon.
This critique shows that the earlier definition is too narrow.
2
Extract the five conditions of a shahid
From the commentator's concise definition in al-Mukhtasar, derive the conditions: Muslim, pure (tahir), adult (baligh), killed unjustly, and no blood money due for his killing.
These conditions are based on the attributes of the martyrs of Uhud.
3
Analyze the condition 'no blood money due'
The commentator explains that 'no blood money' means no diyah is liable on the killer or others. This excludes killing by mistake (khata) or cases where qisas is due according to the Sahibayn.
The presence of blood money or qisas nullifies the martyrdom ruling for worldly purposes.
4
Understand the exclusion of irtithath
Irtithath refers to a wounded person carried from the battlefield with signs of life. Such a person is not a shahid in worldly rulings and must be washed, because he survived the injury temporarily.
This condition ensures that only those who die directly from the injury without lingering are included.
5
Examine the intra-Hanafi dispute on the dhimmi case
The text presents a dispute: if a Muslim is killed unjustly by a dhimmi with an iron weapon, Abu Hanifa rules he is a shahid (no ghusl), while Abu Yusuf and Muhammad hold that qisas is due, so the victim must be washed. The relied-upon (mufta bihi) position is Abu Hanifa's view.
This dispute highlights the difference in interpreting 'no blood money' when qisas is possible.

The conclusion is that the shahid is one who is killed unjustly with an iron weapon, and no blood money is due for him, and he is not alive after the injury, or one who is found dead with a wound in the battle, whether killed with an iron weapon or not. But there is a critique of this definition: it does not include when the polytheists, or rebels, or highway robbers kill him without an iron weapon, for their slain is a shahid, with any tool they killed him. So the good concise definition is what I said in 'al-Mukhtasar': He is a Muslim, pure, adult, killed unjustly, and no blood money is due for him. [0] [1] His statement: 'or one who is found' – being a shahid in the worldly ruling, which is not being washed and burial with blood and the like, which will be mentioned, is established by ahadith narrated concerning the martyrs of Uhud and others. And joined to him is one killed unjustly with a weapon by a killing that does not require blood money, because he is in its meaning and similarity. And the conditions of being Muslim, pure, and legally responsible were added because what is narrated in the text was described with these attributes, so their ruling is not extended to others. [2] His statement: 'when the polytheists kill him,' i.e., outside the battle, unjustly. 'Or rebels' – they are those who go out of obedience to the rightful Imam. 'Or highway robbers' – with a dammah on the qaf, and tashdid on the ta, plural of qati'; meaning those who cut off the road of travelers and take their wealth unjustly. 'Without the iron weapon' – if it was with one, then he is included in the author's statement 'killed with an iron weapon'. [3] His statement: 'killed unjustly' – if he is killed justly, by stoning, or qisas, or rebellion, or highway robbery, then he is washed. Similarly, if he is killed by something not described as unjust, like if a beast of prey attacks him, or a building falls on him, or he drowns, and the like. So in Sharh al-Mukhtasar for al-Birjandi. [4] His statement: 'and no blood money is due for him' – al-Birjandi said: i.e., no wealth is due by his killing, neither on the killer nor on others. So excluded is one killed by mistake, or similar to mistake, for every slain one to whom qisas is due on his killer is a shahid. That is if he was killed with a small or large iron weapon whether it wounded or not. Similarly, if he burned him with fire, or wounded him with a reed, or with something like an iron weapon. As for one killed by a heavy stone, according to Abu Hanifa (عنده) blood money is due so he is washed, while according to the two (عندهما) qisas is due so he is not washed. Thus in al-Khulasah. Saqt. al-Hadr. [0] His statement: 'mal' – i.e., blood money. The blood money due through reconciliation or to protect one's blood from being wasted does not affect it. Book of Salah, Chapter of the Martyr. [1] (1) 'And he is not alive after the injury' (11). [2] Without mentioning the iron weapon and being found in the battlefield, so it includes the slain of the polytheists, rebels, and highway robbers, with any tool they killed him, and it includes the one found dead with a wound in the battlefield because he is a Muslim killed unjustly, and no blood money is due for his killing. As for the slain other than these – a Muslim killed by a Muslim who is not a rebel or highway robber, and a Muslim killed by a dhimmi – then he is only a shahid according to Abu Hanifa if he is killed with an iron weapon unjustly. [4] 11   His statement: 'and he is not alive after the injury' – irtithath is that something of the facilities of life afflicts him after the wound before death. He said in al-Mughrib (2): 'irtathth al-jarih' when he is carried from the battlefield with some life remaining; derived from 'rathth al-mata' (shabby goods) for its dropping and corruption, or from 'rabbat al-nas' (frail people) ; because he is weak or cast aside like shabby goods. [2] His statement: 'without' – related to his statement 'I said': i.e., I did not mention in al-Mukhtasar (3) the condition of the iron weapon nor being found in the battlefield. [3] His statement: 'and highway robbers' – in al-Masr, those who commit robbery are like road cutters. Thus in Sharh al-Majma'. And it is mentioned in al-Bahr (4) that he added in al-Muhit a fourth cause: one who is killed defending even if defending a dhimmi, then he is a shahid with any tool he is killed, even if he is not among those killed by a rebel, a harbi, or a highway robber. And he said in al-Nahr: his being a shahid even if killed without something sharp is very problematic because blood money is due for his killing. And in al-Mujtaba: if two Muslim detachments meet and each thinks the other are polytheists, and they are about the dead of the two groups, Muhammad said: no blood money or expiation is due on anyone because they defended themselves, and he did not mention washing. And they should be washed because their killer did not wrong them. [4] His statement: 'dhimmi' – with kasr of the dhal and tashdid of the mim: it is the disbeliever who pays the jizyah and obeys the Muslims.
Source pages p. 376

Commentary
This passage is a commentary (sharh) on the definition of a shahid (martyr) in Hanafi fiqh. The author first presents an earlier definition: a shahid is one killed unjustly with an iron weapon, with no blood money due, and not having survived the injury, or one found dead with a wound in battle. He criticizes this definition for excluding those killed by polytheists, rebels, or highway robbers without an iron weapon, because they are all martyrs regardless of the instrument. He then offers his own concise definition from his work 'al-Mukhtasar': a shahid is a Muslim, pure (tahir), adult (baligh), killed unjustly, and no blood money (mal) is due for his killing. He adds the conditions 'Muslim, pure, adult' because the hadith of Uhud and other texts describe martyrs with these qualities, so the ruling (no washing, burial with blood) does not extend to non-Muslims, impure persons, or children. He then explains each phrase: 'found in the battlefield' indicates that the martyrdom ruling is worldly (no washing, burial with blood) based on authentic hadith. 'Killed unjustly' excludes those killed by legal punishment, qisas, or by accidents like being mauled by a beast or drowning; such persons are washed. 'No blood money due' means no diyah is required of the killer; this excludes killing by mistake (khata), where blood money is due and the slain is not a shahid. The text also notes an intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf: if a Muslim is killed by a dhimmi with an iron weapon, Abu Hanifa holds he is a shahid (no ghusl), while Abu Yusuf and Muhammad hold that qisas is due, so he is not a shahid (must be washed). The relied-upon (mufta bihi) position in the Hanafi school is Abu Hanifa's view as stated in the zahir al-riwayah. Additionally, the definition excludes the condition of 'being found alive after injury' (irtithath), which is clarified: if a wounded person is carried from the battlefield with life signs, he is not considered a shahid for worldly rulings (he is washed). The commentary also discusses various subcases, such as one killed defending property or a dhimmi, and the situation of two Muslim forces mistakenly fighting each other.

Key Terms
شهيد (shahid): A person who dies a martyr's death in the worldly ruling: not washed, buried in his bloodied state, and no funeral prayer according to some views.
ارتثاث (irtithath): The state of a wounded person who is carried from the battlefield with some signs of life remaining; such a person is considered 'mutaraththith' and is not treated as a shahid (must be washed).
بغي (baghy): Rebellion; those who rebel against the rightful Imam (i.e., legitimate ruler) and fight without justification.
قطاع الطريق (qutta' al-tariq): Highway robbers; those who cut off roads and take travelers' wealth by force unjustly.
ذمي (dhimmi): A non-Muslim subject of the Islamic state who pays jizyah and is under the protection of the Muslim community.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi definition presented, which of the following persons would NOT be considered a shahid (martyr) in the worldly sense?
A) A Muslim adult killed unjustly by a highway robber with a stone
B) A Muslim child killed by a dhimmi with an iron weapon
C) A Muslim adult killed intentionally by a Muslim with a sword without any legal justification
D) A Muslim found dead with wounds in the battlefield after a battle

Key Takeaway
A shahid in Hanafi fiqh is a Muslim, pure, adult, killed unjustly with no blood money due; the weapon and location are irrelevant for polytheists, rebels, and highway robbers, but for a dhimmi killer, an iron weapon is required per the relied-upon position.

Shrouding the Martyr and the Weapon-Type Dispute
Explains the garments removed from the martyr and the intra-Hanafi dispute on weapon type.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state which garments are removed from the martyr's body and explain the intra-Hanafi dispute regarding whether retaliation depends on the type of weapon used.

Core Idea
A martyr is buried in his blood-stained clothes, but only those suitable as a shroud; weapons and luxury items are removed.

This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'āya explains the practical rulings for shrouding the martyr (shahīd). It begins by specifying which items are stripped from his body and which are left, then delves into an internal Hanafi disagreement over whether the killer of a martyr always faces retaliation (qiṣāṣ) or only when an iron weapon was used. Understanding these details is essential for correctly applying the sunna in burial and for distinguishing between the views of Imām Abū Ḥanīfa and the Ṣāḥibayn.

Guided Path
1
Identify the legal act and its ruling
The act is shrouding the martyr; the ruling is wājib to bury him in his blood-stained clothes with adjustments to conform to the sunna number of garments.
The ruling is based on sunna and ijmāʿ.
2
List the items removed from the martyr
Items not of the type of shroud are removed: fur coats, padded garments, caps, weapons, and boots. Garments that serve as shroud (e.g., izār, qamīṣ) are left on.
The principle: anything that is not part of the standard kafan of a non-martyr is removed.
3
Understand the principle of adjusting the shroud
If the martyr has more than three garments, excess is removed; if less, additional cloth is added to make it three. This ensures the shroud conforms to the sunna number.
‘Completing the shroud’ means making it conform to the prescribed number, not necessarily adding material.
4
Analyze the intra-Hanafi dispute on weapon type
Imām Abū Ḥanīfa: Qiṣāṣ is only due if the killer intentionally used an iron tool (e.g., sword). If a blunt tool was used, diya (blood money) is due. The Ṣāḥibayn: Any intentional killing, regardless of weapon, imposes qiṣāṣ.
The dispute affects criminal liability, not the martyr status.
5
Identify the practical consequence (thamarah al-khilāf)
Under Abū Ḥanīfa’s view, a killer using a blunt object pays diya; under the Ṣāḥibayn, the killer is executed. The muftā bihī (relied upon) position in later Ḥanafī practice is that of the Ṣāḥibayn.
The martyr is still buried as a martyr in both views.

Source Text

فيُنْزَعُ عنه غير ثوبه ) : أي غير ثوب " يختص بالميت كالفرو" والحشو، والقلنسوة ) ، والسلاح ، والخفّ ، ويُزادُ ويُنَقَصُ لِيَتَم كفنه )[١] قوله : لوجبَ المالُ عنده ؛ لأنَّ وجوب القصاص عنده مختص بما إذا تعمد ضربه بحديدة، فإن لم يتعمد بأن قتل خطأ أو تعمَّدَ ضربه بغير حديدة سواء كان مما يقتلُ به غالباً أو لا فالواجب الدية.[٢) قوله : فينزع ؛ شروع في كيفية تكفينه.[٣] قوله : أي غير ثوب.... الخ ؛ إشارة إلى أنَّ الضمير في قوله : «ثوبُهُ» إلى «الشهيد» لا من حيث أنه شهيد، بل من حيث أنه ميت والاختصاص مفهوم من الإضافة، وحاصله : أنه يخرج عن الشَّهِيدِ الثّياب التي ليست من جنس كفن الميت وغيرها كالسلاح. [٦] قوله : ويزاد وينقص ; الظاهر المطابق لتصريح كثير من الفقهاء أن معناه أنه يزادُ إن نقص عن كفن السنَّة ، وينقص إن زاد عليه، مثلاً إن كان عليه ثلاثة أردية ينزعُ واحد منها ، وإن كان عليه رداء واحد يزاد الآخر.[٧]قوله : ليتم كفنه ; أوردَ عليه بأنَّ الزِّيادة في الكفن لإتمامه أمر مقبول، فإنه إذا كان ناقصاً وأريد إتمامه زيد، وأما النقصانُ للإتمام فغير معقول، فإنَّ الإتمام عبارة عن تكميل الناقص لا تنقيص الزَّوائد وأجيب عنه : بأن معنى إتمام الكفن هو جعله منطبقاً على العدد المسنون فيصح ذلك في الصورتين. ١ ] قوله : ولو كان معه ... الخ ; هذا التفسير للجملة الثانية ظاهر الفساد، فإنَّ الظاهر أنّ المراد بالنقصان نقصان ما هو من جنس الكفن، فإن نقصان ما ليس من جنسه قد مر ذكره ، إلا أن يقال : المراد منه ما ليس من جنس الكفن المسنون بمعنى عدم موافقته معه لزيادته عليه، وإن كان مما يجانسه ظاهراً.
He is stripped of other than his garment: meaning other than the garment that is specific to the deceased, like a fur, a padded garment, a cap, weapons, and boots. And [extra] is added and [excess] is reduced to complete his shroud. [1] His statement: 'wealth would be obligatory according to him'; because the obligation of retaliation according to him is specific to when he intentionally strikes him with an iron tool. If he did not intend, i.e., killed by mistake, or intentionally struck him with a non-iron tool, whether it usually kills or not, then the blood money is obligatory. [2] His statement: 'he is stripped'; beginning of the explanation of how he is shrouded. [3] His statement: 'other than his garment'; indication that the pronoun in 'his garment' refers to 'the martyr' not in that he is a martyr, but in that he is a deceased person, and the specificity is understood from the possessive. The gist: from the martyr are removed the garments that are not of the type of the shroud of a deceased person, and others like weapons. (4) His statement: 'like a fur'; with fatha, in Persian: yūstīn, a type of garment worn to repel cold. [0] His statement: 'and the padded garment'; with fatha, intended is the garment padded with cotton, as in al-Bināya. [6] His statement: 'and it is added and reduced'; the apparent meaning, corresponding to many jurists, is that it is added if it is less than the sunna shroud, and reduced if it exceeds it. For example, if he has three robes, one is removed; if he has one robe, another is added. [7] His statement: 'to complete his shroud'; it was objected that the addition to the shroud for its completion is acceptable, because if it is deficient and one wants to complete it, it is added; but reduction for completion is unintelligible, because completion means making the deficient complete, not reducing the excess. The answer: the meaning of completing the shroud is to make it conform to the prescribed number, so it is valid in both cases. [1] His statement: 'and if he has with him...'; this interpretation of the second sentence is apparently corrupt, because the apparent meaning is that reduction means reduction of what is of the type of shroud, for the reduction of what is not of its type has already been mentioned. Unless it is said that what is meant is what is not of the type of the sunna shroud, meaning it does not agree with it due to its excess, even if it is apparently of its type.
Source pages p. 378

Commentary
This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'āya comments on the Shrḥ al-Wiqāya regarding the martyr (shahīd). It begins by stating the ruling: from the martyr are removed only those items that are not part of the standard burial shroud, such as fur coats, padded garments, caps, weapons, and boots. The garments that are part of the shroud (like a loincloth or shirt) are left on him. Then it discusses the intra-Hanafi dispute between Imām Abū Ḥanīfa and the two Ṣāḥibayn (Abū Yūsuf and Muḥammad) concerning the type of weapon that makes one a martyr. According to Abū Ḥanīfa, a martyr is one killed intentionally with an iron weapon (like a sword). If killed with a blunt instrument, the killer owes blood money (diya), not retaliation. According to the Ṣāḥibayn, the martyr is one killed by any means, and retaliation is due for intentional killing regardless of the weapon. The commentary then explains the process of adjusting the shroud: if the martyr has more than the prescribed three garments, excess is removed; if less, additional cloth is added. Finally, it clarifies the meaning of 'completing the shroud' as making it conform to the sunna number, not necessarily adding material.

Key Terms
شهيد (shahīd): A martyr who dies in battle against disbelievers and is buried without washing, in his blood-stained clothes.
كفن (kafan): The burial shroud, which for a martyr consists of the clothes he was wearing, except non-shroud items.
حديدة (hadīdah): An iron weapon (e.g., sword, spear) that typically kills when used intentionally.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the text, what item is NOT removed from the martyr?
A) A fur coat
B) A padded garment
C) A sword
D) An undergarment (like an izār)

Key Takeaway
The martyr's shroud consists of his worn clothes adjusted to the sunna number, and the Hanafi school differs on whether the killer's weapon type affects the obligation of qisas.

The Martyr's Prayer: Hanafi vs Shafi'i
Inter-madhhab dispute on praying over the martyr

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on praying over the martyr, explain the Shafi'i counter-argument, and apply the principle of al-muthbit muqaddam ʿalā al-nāfī to resolve the conflict.

Core Idea
The Hanafi school prays over the martyr because affirming narrators outrank negating ones — the Prophet did pray at Uhud.

This passage from ʿUmdat al-Riʿāyah tackles a famous inter-madhhab dispute: Should the funeral prayer be performed over the martyr? The text first establishes the agreed-upon rule that the martyr is not washed, then confronts the contradictory narration from Ṣaḥīḥ al-Bukhārī. By applying the Ḥanafī uṣūl principle that a positive report (muthbit) takes precedence over a negative one (nāfī), the Ḥanafīs uphold the obligation of prayer.

Guided Path
1
Identify the agreed ruling
Begin with the point of consensus: the martyr is not washed and is buried in his blood, based on the Prophet's practice at Uḥud.
2
Locate the disputed issue
The dispute is specifically about the funeral prayer (ṣalāt al-janāzah) over the martyr: is it offered or not?
The washing is not in dispute; only the prayer is contested.
3
Examine the Hanafi evidence
The Ḥanafīs cite multiple aḥādīth (e.g., from ʿUqba ibn ʿĀmir, Ibn ʿAbbās) that the Prophet prayed over the martyrs of Uḥud.
These reports are recorded by Ibn Mājah, al-Ḥākim, Aḥmad, and others.
4
Examine the Shafi'i evidence
Imām al-Shāfiʿī relies on a narration in Ṣaḥīḥ al-Bukhārī (from Jābir) that the Prophet did not pray over the martyrs and buried them without prayer.
This is a single, albeit sahih, report.
5
Apply the tarjih principle
The Ḥanafīs invoke the rule: al-muthbit muqaddam ʿalā al-nāfī — the affirming narrator has additional knowledge. Since the affirming reports are multi-chained and the negating is solitary, precedence goes to affirmation.
This principle is well-established in Ḥanafī uṣūl al-fiqh.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ولا يغسل؛ الأصلُ في دفنِ الشَّهيدِ بدمه، وعدم غسله ما ثبت عن النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم في شهداء أحد (١) كما مر ذكره. (۱) وأما في الصلاة عليهم فقد ثبت أنه له صلى عليهم (٢)، أخرجه ابن ماجه والحاكم وأحمدَ والدَّارَقُطْنِي والطبراني والبيهقي وأبو داود وغيرهم، كما بسطَهُ الزَّيْلَعِيُّ في «تخريج أحاديث الهداية» وغيره ، وورد في «صحيح البخاري»: «إنّه لم يـصـل ، وبه أخذ الشَّافعي الله ، ومن المعلوم أنَّ المثبت مقدَّم على النافي).
His statement: 'and he is not washed'; the basis for burying the martyr in his blood and not washing him is what is established from the Prophet ﷺ regarding the martyrs of Uhud, as mentioned previously. As for praying over them, it is established that heﷺ prayed over them, recorded by Ibn Mājah, al-Ḥākim, Aḥmad, al-Dāraquṭnī, al-Ṭabarānī, al-Bayhaqī, Abū Dāwūd, and others, as expanded by al-Zaylaʿī in Takhrīj Aḥādīth al-Hidāyah and others. And in Ṣaḥīḥ al-Bukhārī it is reported: 'He did not pray over them,' and al-Shāfiʿī took this view. And it is known that the one who affirms is given precedence over the one who negates.
Source pages p. 379

Commentary
This passage addresses the rulings on washing and praying over the martyr. The author states the agreed-upon principle that the martyr is not washed and is buried with his blood and clothes, based on the practice of the Prophet at Uhud. Then he discusses the dispute with Imām al-Shāfiʿī concerning the funeral prayer (ṣalāt al-janāzah) for the martyr. The Ḥanafī position is that prayer over the martyr is obligatory (wājib), as there are authentic narrations that the Prophet prayed over the martyrs of Uhud (e.g., the ḥadīth of ʿUqba ibn ʿĀmir and others). Imām al-Shāfiʿī holds that the Prophet did not pray over them, citing a narration in Ṣaḥīḥ al-Bukhārī. The Ḥanafī response is that the affirming reports (muthbit) take precedence over the negating ones (nāfī), according to the principle of uṣūl al-fiqh that the one who affirms has additional knowledge. Therefore, the Ḥanafīs consider the prayer obligatory.

Key Terms
المثبّت مقدّم على النافي (al-muthbit muqaddam ʿalā al-nāfī): A principle of uṣūl al-fiqh: when there are conflicting reports, the one that affirms an act (positive narration) is given precedence over one that negates it.
أحد (Uḥud): Refers to the Battle of Uḥud where many companions were martyred; their treatment is the basis for the rulings on martyrs.

Key Takeaway
The Ḥanafī school holds that the funeral prayer over the martyr is wājib, relying on the principle that an affirming narrator is preferred over a negating one.

Ruling on Washing and Praying Over the Martyr
States the ruling for a martyr and discusses proof and dispute

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the Hanafi school holds that the funeral prayer is performed over the martyr and evaluate the Shafi'i counter-argument.

Core Idea
The martyr is not washed but the funeral prayer is wajib upon him, despite the Shafi'i disagreement.

This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah presents the Hanafi ruling on the treatment of the martyr's body and the dispute with Imam al-Shafi'i regarding the funeral prayer. The text first clarifies the shrouding rules, then focuses on the proof for prayer and the response to the opposing evidence.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Identify the ruling on washing, shrouding, and prayer for the martyr as presented in the first part of the passage.
The passage explicitly states: 'ولا يُغسل ويُصَلِّى عليه، ويُدفن بدمه' (He is not washed, prayer is offered over him, and he is buried with his blood).
2
Identify the opponent and their view
Recognize that Imam al-Shafi'i disagrees on the prayer, relying on a hadith in Sahih al-Bukhari that the Prophet did not pray over the martyrs of Uhud.
The text refers to the Shafi'i position: 'وورد في «صحيح البخاري»: «إنّه لم يـصـل، وبه أخذ الشَّافعي»'
3
Follow the opponent's proof
Examine the hadith that negates the prayer and understand its basis for the Shafi'i stance.
The hadith of Jabir is cited: 'لم يصل عليهم', meaning the Prophet did not pray over them.
4
Analyze the Hanafi response
Understand why the Hanafis favor the affirming hadith over the negating one, citing the principle that the affirmative takes precedence, especially when consistent with the general obligation of prayer.
The text states: 'المثبت مقدَّم على النافي' and explains that the prayer is obligatory with certainty, so it cannot be dropped by a zanni report.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
Identify the practical consequence: in the Hanafi school, the funeral prayer is performed for the martyr; in the Shafi'i school, it is not.
This difference affects the actual practice of the Muslim community.

Book of Prayer, Chapter of the Martyr. And he is not washed, and prayer is offered over him, and he is buried with his blood. That is, if he does not have with him what is of the same type as the shroud: like an izār and similar, it is added; and if what is not of its type exists, it is decreased. And he is not washed, and prayer is offered over him, and he is buried with his blood. Completing the deficient, not decreasing the surplus. And it was answered: that the meaning of completing the shroud is making it conform to the prescribed number, so it is valid in both cases. [1] His statement: 'And if he has with him... etc.' This interpretation of the second sentence is apparently corrupt, for the apparent meaning is that the decrease refers to what is of the same type as the shroud, because the decrease of what is not of its type has already been mentioned. Unless it is said: what is meant is what is not of the same type as the prescribed shroud, meaning it does not conform with it due to its increase over it, even if it outwardly resembles it. [2] His statement: 'And he is not washed' – the basis for burying the martyr with his blood and not washing him is what is established from the Prophet ﷺ regarding the martyrs of Uhud, as previously mentioned. As for praying over them, it is established that he ﷺ prayed over them, narrated by Ibn Mājah, al-Ḥākim, Aḥmad, al-Dāraquṭnī, al-Ṭabarānī, al-Bayhaqī, Abū Dāwūd, and others, as detailed by al-Zayla‘ī in Takhrīj Aḥādīth al-Hidāyah and elsewhere. And it is reported in Ṣaḥīḥ al-Bukhārī: 'He did not pray over them', and al-Shāfi‘ī follows that. And it is known that the affirming [proof] takes precedence over the negating. (1) From Ibn ‘Abbās: 'The Messenger of Allāh ﷺ commanded concerning the slain of Uhud that the iron and leather be removed from them, and that they be buried with their blood and clothes.' In Sunan Abī Dāwūd (212), Sunan Ibn Mājah (1:485), Musnad Aḥmad (247), and our shaykh al-Arnā’ūṭ said: Ḥasan for other than it. (2) From ‘Uqbah ibn ‘Āmir: 'The Prophet ﷺ prayed over the slain of Uhud after eight years, like bidding farewell to the living and the dead.' In Sunan Abī Dāwūd (235), Musnad Aḥmad (4:154), Ṣaḥīḥ Ibn Ḥibbān (7:474), al-Mustadrak (1:520), and others. And from Ibn ‘Abbās: 'The Messenger of Allāh ﷺ ordered Ḥamzah on the day of Uhud, so he was positioned facing the qiblah, then he said takbīr over him seven times, then he gathered the martyrs to him until he prayed seventy prayers over him.' In Sunan al-Bayhaqī al-Kabīr (4:116) and others. (3) From Jābir: 'The Messenger of Allāh ﷺ would join two men from the slain of Uhud in one garment, then say: 'Which of them has more knowledge of the Qur’an?' So when one was pointed out to him, he would put him first in the grave, and said: 'I am a witness over these.' And he ordered them to be buried with their blood, and he did not pray over them and did not wash them.' In Ṣaḥīḥ al-Bukhārī (1:452) and Sunan al-Bayhaqī al-Kabīr (4:34) and others. (4) That is, the affirmation of the ḥadīth of Jābir regarding prayer over the martyr is rejected because the narration of the affirmer is consistent with principles, so it takes precedence over the narration of the negator due to its opposition to them; and because prayer is obligatory upon us with certainty, so it is not dropped by a zannī [report] opposed by the like or examples of it. See Fatḥ Bāb al-‘Ināyah (1:463).
Source pages p. 379

Commentary
This passage is an inter-madhhab dispute concerning the burial of a martyr. The Hanafi position is that a martyr is not washed but is prayed over and buried with his blood and clothes. The text first clarifies the ruling on the shroud: if the martyr has something of the same type as the shroud, it is added to complete the prescribed number; if he has something of a different type, it is removed to avoid exceeding the number. The commentary then addresses the issue of prayer: there is a conflict between hadith reports. The Hanafis rely on multiple reports that the Prophet prayed over the martyrs of Uhud, while al-Shāfi‘ī relies on a report in Ṣaḥīḥ al-Bukhārī that he did not. The Hanafi response is that the affirming reports take precedence because they are supported by the principle that prayer is obligatory and cannot be dropped by a doubtful report. They also note that the narration of Jābir that denies prayer is contrary to established principles, so it is outweighed.

Key Terms
شهيد (shahīd): One who is killed unjustly in battle against non-Muslims, while fighting in the way of Allah.
غسل (ghusl): Washing the body of the deceased with water and sidr (lotus leaves).
صلاة الجنازة (ṣalāt al-janāzah): The funeral prayer, consisting of four takbirs and supplications.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
The martyr is not washed but the funeral prayer is wajib, based on affirming hadith that take precedence over negating reports.

Exceptions to Washing the Martyr: Who Must Be Washed Despite Being Killed Unjustly
Lists categories of people who must be washed even if killed unjustly.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the five categories of people who must be washed when killed unjustly and explain the reason for each exception.

Core Idea
Not everyone killed unjustly is a martyr; children, menstruating women, post-partum women, junub, and unknown victims must be washed.

In Hanafi law, a martyr (shahid) who dies in battle is not washed. However, there are exceptions. This lesson examines five categories who, even if killed unjustly, must be given a full ritual bath (ghusl). Understanding these exceptions ensures proper funeral rites.

Guided Path
1
Recall the general rule for martyrs
Start by remembering that a martyr killed in battle is not washed. This is the baseline from which exceptions are made.
The general rule is based on hadith: the martyrs of Uhud were not washed.
2
List the five exceptions from the text
From the passage, identify: child, menstruating woman, post-partum woman, junub, and one found killed in a town with unknown killer.
The first four are excluded because they are not in the 'meaning' of the text on martyrdom.
3
Understand the rationale for each exception
For each, ask: why is this person not considered a martyr? The child lacks intention; women in menstruation/post-partum are in a state of impurity; junub is impure; the unknown victim may not be a fighter.
The key is that they do not meet the conditions for martyrdom: adult, pure, Muslim, fighting intentionally.
4
Note the condition for the 'found killed' case
The person found killed must be in an inhabited area (town or village) where qasamah and diyah apply. If found in a desert, he is not washed.
This is because the community's responsibility (qasamah) triggers the ruling; in a desert, no such responsibility exists.
5
Recognize the practical difference
The ruling determines whether ghusl is wajib or not. If wajib, the body must be washed before funeral prayer; if not, it is buried without washing.
This has significant practical implications for funeral arrangements.

And washed: a child, a menstruating woman, a post-partum woman, a junub, and one found killed in a town whose killer is unknown. So he is washed. And a child, a menstruating woman, a post-partum woman, a junub, and one found killed in a town whose killer is unknown – indeed, if his killer is unknown, he is washed, whether it is known that his killing occurred with an iron weapon or with a large stick, or a small one, because what is obligatory is the blood-money and the qasamah. Thus it is mentioned in al-Dhakhīrah, and he did not mention whether he is found in a place where qasamah is obligatory or not. [1] His statement: 'And washed: a child' – the child, menstruating woman, post-partum woman, and junub, even if they are killed unjustly with an iron weapon, are washed because they are not within the meaning of what is mentioned in the text, so they are not joined to it unless another evidence indicates that washing is dropped from them, and it does not exist. [2] His statement: 'And one found... etc.' – meaning, he is washed if he is found killed in a town and his killer is not known, even if he is an adult, pure, Muslim. And by 'town' he means general, such that it includes a village. So if he is found killed in a desert that is not a inhabited village, he is not washed because qasamah and blood-money are not obligatory. Thus in Ma‘rāj al-Dirāyah. [3] His statement: 'with an iron weapon' – meaning a pointed tool, i.e., having a sharp edge, whether it is a weapon like a knife and sword or other like pointed wood, a sharp piece of reed, a pointed stone, etc. [4] His statement: 'or with a large stick' – and similar things that usually kill, and such killing if intentional is shibh al-‘amd according to him, and ‘amd according to them. [5] His statement: 'or a small one' – and similar things that usually do not cause death. [6] His statement: 'the blood-money' – with kasr of the dāl and fatḥ of the yā’, lightly pronounced: a name for the financial compensation obligatory in place of the blood of the killed, which is one hundred camels, or one thousand dinars of gold, or ten thousand dirhams. [7] His statement: 'and the qasamah' – with fatḥ: an expression for oaths taken by the people of a district or house in which a dead person is found with a wound or mark of beating or strangulation, and his killer is not known. Then fifty men from the people of the district swear, each saying: 'By Allah, I did not kill him, nor do I know his killer.' So when they swear, judgment is given that the people of the district must pay the blood-money of the killed. Its detail will come in its place, Allah willing.
Source pages p. 380

Commentary
This passage is a ruling stating the exceptions to the rule that one killed unjustly is a martyr and not washed. The text lists five categories who must be washed: a child (sabi), a menstruating woman (ha'id), a post-partum woman (nufasa'), a person in a state of major ritual impurity (junub), and a person found killed in a town where the killer is unknown. The commentary explains that these individuals are not covered by the general ruling that the martyr is not washed, because they are not within the meaning of the text (i.e., they do not meet the description of a martyr as an adult, pure, Muslim fighter). Even if killed with an iron weapon, they are washed. The commentary also clarifies the conditions for the one found killed: it must be in an inhabited place (town or village) where qasamah (oath-taking by the community) and diyah (blood-money) are obligatory; if found in an uninhabited desert, he is not washed. The definitions of 'iron weapon', 'large stick', 'small stick', and the terms diyah and qasamah are provided.

Key Terms
صبي (ṣabī): A minor child who has not reached puberty.
حائض (ḥā’iḍ): A woman in the state of menstruation.
نفساء (nufasā’): A woman in the post-partum period (during nifas).
جنب (junub): A person in a state of major ritual impurity requiring ghusl.
دية (diyah): Blood-money, a financial compensation for unlawful killing.
قسامة (qasāmah): Oaths taken by the community when a dead person is found and the killer is unknown.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why is a child who is killed unjustly with a sword washed, unlike an adult martyr?
A) Because a child cannot be a martyr as he is not obligated to fight.
B) Because the sword invalidates the martyrdom.
C) Because the child is impure.
D) Because the child did not intend to fight.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school mandates washing for five distinct groups even when killed unjustly, upholding ritual purity over the presumption of martyrdom.
