Washing a Found Dead Person: Weapon and Martyr Status in Hanafi Fiqh
Intra-Hanafi dispute on washing based on weapon and location

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi ruling to determine whether a found dead person is a martyr and must be washed, based on weapon type and location, and state the intra-Hanafi dispute on the large stick.

Core Idea
A martyr is killed by iron; a large-stick killing requires washing per Abu Hanifa, contrary to the Sahibayn.

When a dead body is found, Muslim jurists must decide whether the person is a martyr and thus exempt from washing and funeral prayer. This lesson unpacks the Hanafi criteria, focusing on the weapon used and the location where the body is found. We explore the classic disagreement between Abu Hanifa and his two disciples over the large stick, and how location (qasamah) affects the ruling.

Guided Path
1
Identify the location type
Determine if the body was found in a place where qasamah (oath and blood-money) is obligatory (e.g., a residential area) or not (e.g., a public street or mosque). This affects the baseline obligation but the weapon ruling remains primary.
Qasamah is wajib in a private locality, not in public thoroughfares.
2
Classify the weapon
Identify the weapon that caused death: iron (al-hadidah), large stick (al-asa al-kabir), or small stick (al-asa al-saghir). Each triggers a different ruling on martyrdom and washing.
Iron includes swords and knives; large sticks are heavy clubs (muthaqqal); small sticks are light.
3
Apply Abu Hanifa's ruling
For iron: the victim is a martyr – not washed. For large stick: not a martyr – must be washed. For small stick: not a martyr – must be washed by consensus.
The large stick case is the only point of ikhtilaf between Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn.
4
Note the Sahibayn's disagreement
Abu Yusuf and Muhammad consider the large stick equivalent to iron; both require qisas, so the victim is a martyr and not washed. Abu Hanifa distinguishes: large stick does not require qisas, only blood-money, so the victim is not a martyr.
Thamarah al-khilaf: whether to wash the body matters in funeral rites.
5
Determine the mufta bihi
The relied-upon position in the madhhab is Abu Hanifa's: a person killed with a large stick is washed. This is the operative ruling for fatwa.
Later Hanafi texts (e.g., Al-Hidayah) uphold this view.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أقولُ : إن المراد به إن وُجِدَ في موضع تَجِبُ القسامة. أمَّا إِذا وُجِدَ في موضع لا تجب القسامة كالشارع، والجامع :
فإن عُلِمَ أَنه قتل بالحديدةِ لا يُغْسَل ؛ لأنه شهيد(4) وإن علم أنه قُتِلَ بالعصا الكبير، ينبغي أن يُغْسَلَ عند أبي حنيفة له، إذ ليس شهيدا عنده، خلافاً لهما.
وإن عُلِمَ أنه قُتِلَ بالعصا الصَّغير ينبغي أن يُغْسَلَ اتفاقا ؛ لأنَّ نفس القتل أوجب الدية ، فعدم وجوبها بعارض جَهْل القاتل لا يَجعلُه شهيداً. [١] قوله : إن المراد ؛ لأنه ذكر أن الواجب في القَسَامة وهي لا تجب في كلِّ موضع، بل في محلة أو دار، أو نحو ذلك، فإن وجد القتيل في المسجد الجامع أو الطريقِ الأعظم، أو غيرهما ما فيه حقُّ العامة فلا قسامةً فيه، بل الدية في بيت المال. كذا في «الهداية).
[۲] قوله : كالشارع ؛ هو الطريقُ الذي يشرعُ فيه النَّاسُ عامة على الإسنادِ المجازي أو هو من شَرَعَ الطريق أي تبين كذا في «المغرب» (٢).
[٣] قوله : إنه قتل ... الخ ؛ قال البِرْجَنْدِيّ في شرح النقاية»: هذا التفصيلُ يجري فيما إذا وجد في موضع تجب فيه القسامة بلا فرق، إلا أن الدية هاهنا على أهل المحلة، وهناك في بيت المال، فالظاهرُ أنَّ هذا الفرق غير مؤثر.
[٤] قوله : لأنه شهيد ؛ لكونه مقتولاً ظلماً بحديدة.
[0]قوله : عنده ؛ لأنَّ المقتول بالمثقل لا يجب فيه القصاص عنده ، بل الدية ، وعندهما هو ومقتول المحدد سيان.
[٦] قوله : اتفاقاً : أي بين أئمتنا الثلاثة، لأنَّ مثل هذا القتل يوجبُ الدِّيةَ عندهم جميعاً، والقتل الذي يجب به عوض مالي لا يكون المقتول به شهيداً كما مر ، ولَمَّا كان يرد هاهنا أنَّ الدِّيةَ ليست بواجبة في هذه الصور لعدم علم القاتل، أجاب عنه بقوله : «فعدم وجوبها.... الخ، وحاصله : أنَّ نفس القتل موجب للدية فيكون مانعاً من أحكام الشهادة، وعدم وجوبها هاهنا لعارض، وهو عدم العلم بالقاتل فلا يؤثر ذلك.
أما إذا عُلِمَ القاتل"" :
فإن علم أن القتل بالحديدة لم يُغْسَل؛ لأنه شهيد". وإن علم أنه قُتِلَ بالعصا الكبيرة ينبغي أن يُغْسَلَ عند أبي حنيفة له خلافاً لهما.
وإن عُلِمَ أنه قُتِلَ بالعصا الصغيرة يُغْسَلُ اتفاقاً . وقد قال في «الهداية ) : مَن وُجِدَ قتيلاً في المصر غُسل ؛ لأنَّ الواجب فيه الدِّيةُ والقسامة ، فخَفَّ أثر الظلم
I say: What is meant is that he is found in a place where qasamah is obligatory. As for if he is found in a place where qasamah is not obligatory, like the street and the mosque: if it is known that he was killed with iron, he is not washed because he is a martyr. If it is known that he was killed with a large stick, it is appropriate that he be washed according to Abu Hanifa, for he is not a martyr in his view, contrary to the two [Abu Yusuf and Muhammad]. If it is known that he was killed with a small stick, it is appropriate that he be washed by agreement, because the killing itself necessitates blood-money, and its non-necessitation due to the accidental ignorance of the killer does not make him a martyr. ... As for when the killer is known: if it is known that the killing was with iron, he is not washed because he is a martyr. If it is known that he was killed with a large stick, it is appropriate that he be washed according to Abu Hanifa, contrary to the two. If it is known that he was killed with a small stick, he is washed by agreement. And he said in Al-Hidayah: Whoever is found killed in the city is washed because the obligation [upon the killer] is blood-money and qasamah, so the effect of oppression is lightened.
Source pages p. 381

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute about the ruling of washing a body found dead, specifically regarding martyr status. The commentator begins by clarifying that the ruling depends on the location where qasamah is obligatory. If the body is found in a place where qasamah is not required, like a public street or a large mosque, then the type of weapon matters. If killed with an iron weapon (like a sword), the victim is considered a martyr and not washed. If killed with a large stick, Abu Hanifa says to wash because no qisas is due; the two imams (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) consider it martyrdom. If killed with a small stick, all agree to wash because blood-money is due. The commentator then addresses the case when the killer is known, applying the same weapon distinctions. A quote from Al-Hidayah states that anyone found killed in a city (where qasamah applies) is washed because the obligation of blood-money lessens the oppression. The passage highlights the legal reasoning: martyr status requires that no monetary compensation be due for the killing; if blood-money is involved, the victim is not a martyr.

Key Terms
شهيد (shahid): A person killed unjustly with certain weapons, who is not washed and not prayed upon, as a sign of honor.
قسامة (qasamah): An oath taken by the people of a locality when a dead person is found and the killer is unknown; it imposes blood-money on the locality.
الحديدة (al-hadidah): Iron weapon, such as a sword or knife, that cuts and typically causes qisas.
العصا الكبير (al-asa al-kabir): Large stick or club, considered a heavy object (muthaqqal) that does not necessitate qisas but only blood-money.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A body is found in a public street, killed with a large stick, and the killer is unknown. According to Abu Hanifa, should the body be washed?
A) No, because he is a martyr.
B) Yes, because he is killed with a large stick and is not a martyr.
C) Only if the killer is known.
D) Yes, because qasamah is not obligatory in the street.

Key Takeaway
In the Hanafi school, martyr status for a found dead person depends on weapon type: iron = martyr (no wash); large stick = not martyr (wash per Abu Hanifa); small stick = wash by consensus; location (qasamah) modifies blood-money but not the core weapon rule.

Washing the Martyr: Known vs Unknown Killer and Weapon Type
Intra-Hanafi dispute and Hidayah ruling on ghusl for martyrs

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on washing a martyr based on whether the killer is known and the type of weapon used, and explain the intra-Hanafi disagreement regarding a large stick.

Core Idea
A martyr is not washed only if killed by an iron weapon and the killer is known; otherwise, ghusl is wajib.

The janazah rulings for martyrs are unique: they are not washed, preserving their blood as testimony. But not every person killed unjustly qualifies. This lesson unpacks the conditions set by the Hanafi school, focusing on the crucial distinction between known and unknown killers and the type of weapon involved.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three scenarios when killer is known
List the three weapon types (iron, large stick, small stick) and note the ruling for each. Observe the disagreement on the large stick.
2
Understand the rationale for the iron weapon ruling
Iron weapon necessitates qisas, so the victim is a true shahid – not washed. Know the difference between qisas (retaliation) and diyah (blood money).
3
Analyze the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf on the large stick
Abu Hanifa holds that ghusl is wajib because the stick is not a typical lethal weapon; Sahibayn consider it analogous to iron, so no ghusl. Examine their proofs.
4
Examine the Hidayah ruling when killer is unknown
When the killer is unknown, the victim is washed regardless of weapon because only diyah and qasamah apply. Understand why this does not contradict the previous rules.
5
Resolve the apparent contradiction between Al-Hidayah and Al-Dhakhirah
The Hidayah ruling applies only when killer unknown. If killer unknown but killed with iron, still washed because qisas cannot be enforced. This resolves the commentator's confusion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أما إذا عُلِمَ القاتل: فإن علم أن القتل بالحديدة لم يُغْسَل؛ لأنه شهيد. وإن علم أنه قُتِلَ بالعصا الكبيرة ينبغي أن يُغْسَلَ عند أبي حنيفة له خلافاً لهما. وإن عُلِمَ أنه قُتِلَ بالعصا الصغيرة يُغْسَلُ اتفاقاً. وقد قال في «الهداية»: من وُجِدَ قتيلاً في المصر غُسل؛ لأنَّ الواجب فيه الدِّيةُ والقسامة، فخَفَّ أثر الظلم. أقول: هذه الرواية مخالفة لما ذُكِرَ في «الذخيرة»؛ لأن رواية «الهداية» فيما إذا لم يُعْلَمْ قاتله؛ لأنَّه علل بوجوب القسامة، ولا قسامة إلا إذا لم يعلم القاتل، ففي صورة عدم العلم بالقاتل إذا عُلِمَ أن القتل بالحديدة، ففي رواية «الهداية» لا يُغسل؛ لأنَّ نفس هذا القتل أوجب القصاص. وأما وجوب الدِّيةِ والقسامة؛ فلعارض العجز عن إقامة القصاص، فلا يخرجه هذا العارض عن أن يكون شهيدا. [قوله: إلا إذا علم أنه قتل بحديدة؛ تمامه: «بأنَّ الواجب فيه القصاص، وهو عقوبة، والقاتل لا يتخلص عنها إما في الدنيا وإما في العقبى». انتهى. وهذا التعليل صريح في أنَّ المراد بقوله: إلا إذا علم أنه قتل بحديدة، ما إذا علم قاتله، وبه صرّح شراح «الهداية» كابن الهمام والسغناقي والعَيْني وغيرهم، وبهذا يسقط ما فهمه الشارح وبنى عليه كونه مخالفا لما في «الذخيرة»]
As for if the killer is known: If it is known that the killing was with an iron implement, he is not washed because he is a martyr. And if it is known that he was killed with a large stick, he should be washed according to Abu Hanifa, differing from the two companions. And if it is known that he was killed with a small stick, he is washed by agreement. And it is stated in Al-Hidayah: Whoever is found killed in the town is washed because the obligation is diyah and qasamah, so the effect of oppression becomes light. I say: This narration contradicts what is mentioned in Al-Dhakhirah; because the narration of Al-Hidayah concerns when his killer is not known; because he justified with the obligation of qasamah, and there is no qasamah except when the killer is not known. So in the case of not knowing the killer, if it is known that the killing was with an iron implement, then according to the narration of Al-Hidayah, he is not washed, because this killing itself necessitates qisas. As for the obligation of diyah and qasamah, it is due to the incapacity to establish qisas, so this incapacity does not take him out of being a martyr. [His statement: 'Except if it is known that he was killed with an iron implement'; its completion: 'Because what is obligatory is qisas, which is a punishment, and the killer does not escape from it either in this world or the next.' This reasoning makes clear that the meaning of his statement 'except if it is known that he was killed with an iron implement' is when his killer is known, and the commentators of Al-Hidayah such as Ibn al-Humam, al-Sighnaqi, al-Ayni and others explicitly stated this, and thereby what the commentator understood and built upon as contradicting Al-Dhakhirah falls away.]
Source pages p. 382

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute and a discussion of the ruling from Al-Hidayah regarding ghusl (washing) of a martyr. The author first presents the ruling when the killer is known: if the victim is killed with an iron weapon (hadidah), he is not washed because he is a martyr (shahid) — qisas is obligatory, so he is a true martyr. If killed with a large stick (asā kabīrah), according to Abu Hanifa he should be washed, differing from the two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) who consider him a martyr not to be washed. If killed with a small stick (asā saghīrah), all agree he is washed because only diyah (blood money) is obligatory. The text then cites Al-Hidayah: whoever is found dead in a town (misr) is washed because the obligation is diyah and qasamah (oath), so the effect of oppression is lightened. The author initially perceives a contradiction with Al-Dhakhirah, but then clarifies: the Hidayah ruling applies only when the killer is unknown (since qasamah is only when the killer is unknown). In that case, even if killed with an iron weapon, the victim is washed because qisas cannot be enforced, but the general rule is that if the killer is known and killed with iron, he is not washed. The commentators of Al-Hidayah explain that the exception 'except if known to be killed with iron' refers to when the killer is known, and the apparent contradiction is resolved.

Key Terms
شهيد (shahīd): A person killed unjustly (zulman) in a way that qisas is obligatory, making him a martyr who is not washed.
حديدة (hadīdah): An iron weapon (e.g., sword, knife) that causes death by cutting and necessitates qisas.
قسامة (qasāmah): An oath taken by fifty men from the victim's clan when the killer is unknown, to establish liability for diyah.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, under what condition is a person killed with an iron weapon still washed?
A) When the killer is known.
B) When the killer is unknown.
C) When killed with a large stick.
D) When killed with a small stick.

Key Takeaway
A martyr is not washed only if killed by an iron weapon and the killer is known; if the killer is unknown or a non-iron weapon is used (except large stick dispute), ghusl is wajib.

Ruling on Washing a Martyr Killed by an Iron Weapon
Reconciling two Hanafi narrations on ghusl for a martyr killed by iron.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on washing a martyr killed by an iron weapon and explain how knowledge of the killer affects the ruling.

Core Idea
A martyr killed by iron is washed if the killer is unknown, not washed if known — because diya benefits the deceased.

This passage reconciles two Hanafi narrations regarding ghusl for a martyr killed by an iron weapon. The apparent meaning of al-Hidayah seems to prohibit washing in all such cases, but later commentators clarify that this applies only when the killer is known. Understanding the basis in qisas versus qasama/diya is key to the ruling.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two narrations
Recognize that al-Hidayah appears to say no ghusl for any iron-caused martyr, while al-Dhakhira explicitly says ghusl is required when the killer is unknown.
The core of the dispute is whether the exception in al-Hidayah is conditional on knowing the killer.
2
Determine the apparent conflict
Note that if taken literally, al-Hidayah would forbid ghusl even when the killer is unknown, contradicting al-Dhakhira.
The commentator initially saw a contradiction, but later exonerates al-Hidayah.
3
Analyze the reasoning of al-Hidayah
Observe that al-Hidayah's wording 'except when it is known he was killed by iron' is followed by 'because what is obligatory is qisas.' Qisas requires a known killer; therefore the exception implies knowledge of the killer.
The obligation of qasama/diya only arises when the killer is unknown, which is the case where ghusl is performed.
4
Follow the later commentary
Study how later scholars (Ibn al-Humam, al-Saghanaki, al-Ayni) interpret al-Hidayah consistently with al-Dhakhira, introducing the condition of known killer.
These commentators are the source of the mufta bihi position.
5
Apply the mufta bihi ruling
Conclude that the operative ruling differentiates: if the killer is known, no ghusl (qisas applies); if unknown, ghusl is performed (qasama/diya benefit the deceased).
This resolves the apparent contradiction and is the relied-upon fatwa.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الصلاة باب الشهيد

إلا إذا عُلِمَ أنه قتل بحديدة ظلماً .

أقولُ : هذه الرواية مخالفة لما ذُكِرَ في «الدخيرة»؛ لأن رواية «الهداية» فيما إذا لم يُعْلَمْ قاتله؛ لأنَّه علل بوجوب القسامة، ولا قسامة إلا إذا لم يعلم القاتل، ففي صورة عدم العلم بالقاتل إذا عُلِمَ أن القتل بالحديدة، ففي رواية «الهداية» لا يُغسل؛ لأنَّ نفس هذا القتل أوجب القصاص. وأما وجوب الدِّيةِ والقسامة؛ فلعارض العجز عن إقامة القصاص، فلا يخرجه هذا العارض عن أن يكون شهيدا.

[١] قوله : إلا إذا علم أنه قتل بحديدة؛ تمامه : «بأنَّ الواجب فيه القصاص، وهو عقوبة، والقاتل لا يتخلص عنها إما في الدنيا وإما في العقبى». انتهى. وهذا التعليل صريح في أنَّ المراد بقوله : إلا إذا علم أنه قتل بحديدة، ما إذا علم قاتله، و به صرّح شراح «الهداية» كابن الهمام والسغناقي والعَيْني وغيرهم، وبهذا يسقط ما فهمه الشارح وبنى عليه كونه مخالفا لما في «الذخيرة».

[٢] قوله : هذه الرواية... الخ؛ حاصله بناءً على ما فيه أنَّ كلام «الهداية» إنما هو فيمن لم يعلم قاتله بدليل أنه أوجب فيه القسامة، ولا دية ولا قسامة إذا علم القاتل، وقوله : إلا إذا علم ... الخ؛ استثناء من قوله السابق، فيعلم منه أنه إذا وجد قتيل لم يعلم قاتله وعلم أنه قتل بحديدة ظلماً لا يغسل.

[٣] قوله : وأما وجوب... الخ؛ دفع لما يتوهَّمُ أنه كيف يجبُ القسامة والدِّيةُ مع أنه موجب للقصاص.

[٤] قوله : فلا يخرجه... الخ؛ قال الشارح الهروي في شرح قول المصنف له السابق : لأنه تجب فيها القسامة فكأنَّ النَّفس باقية ببقاء عوضه، حتى لو وجد في مفازة
(۱) انتهى من الهداية (۱) : (۹۵)، وتمام كلامه : لأن الواجب فيه القصاص.... (۲) هذا ما يدل عليه ظاهر عبارة «الهداية)، وهو ما ذهب إليه صدر الشريعة، لكن شراح الهداية» كما بينا قالوا : لا يغسل إذا كان القاتل معيّناً، ووجهوا كلام صاحب («الهداية) بناء على ذلك كما سبق ذكره. (۳) من «الهداية» (۲) : ١٤٩). (٤) قال ابن الهمام في «الفتح» (۱ : ۱۰۹) : أي ويعلم قاتله عيناً، أما مجرد وجدانه مذبوحاً لا يمنع غسله، وقد يستفاد هذا من قوله؛ لأن الواجب فيه القصاص؛ لأن وجوبه إنما يتحقق على القاتل المعين...»، ومثله في العناية» (۱): (۱۰۹)، و«الكفاية» (۱ : ۱۰۹)، وغيرها.

وأما على رواية «الذخيرة» فيُغْسَل، وعبارة «الذخيرة) هذه : وإن حصل القتل بحديدة، فإن لم يعلم قاتله تجب الدِّيةُ والقسامة على أهل المحلة فيُغْسَل، وإن علم القاتل لم يُغْسَل عندنا. ففي «الذخيرة» لم يُعتبر نفس القتل، فوجوب الدِّية وإن كان بالعارض أخرجَهُ عن الشهادة. وفي «المتن» أخذ بهذه الرواية، هذا إذا عُلِمَ أنه بأي آلة قتل ليس بقربها، عمران، إن لم يغسل، وكذا إن علم قاتله فإن الواجب حينئذٍ القصاص، عقوبة يرجع نفعها إلى ولي القتيل وسائر المسلمين أيضاً لا إلى المقتول فقط، فهو في وهو حكم شهداء أحد، بخلاف الدية. وفي «الهداية» و«الخلاصة» : إلا إذا علم أنه قتل بحديدة، ولكن لم يعلم قاتله يغسل، لما أن الواجب هناك القسامة، ونفعها يعود إلى الميت حتى يقضي دينه وينفذ وصاياه في الدية. وفي «النهاية»: هذا القيد، أعني إذا علم قاتله، يستفاد من «الهداية» من قوله : لأن الواجب فيه القصاص، إنما يكون على القاتل المعلوم. وفي «الكرماني»: في الكتاب إشارة إليه لأنه إنما يكون ظلمـاً لـو كـان القاتل معلوماً، حتى لو لم يعلم جاز أن يكون متعدياً فلا يكون القتل ظلماً، فعليك أن لا تحمل عبارة «الهداية» على ظاهرها كما حمل في الصدرية واعترض عليه. بشيء.

[١] قوله : بالعارض؛ وهو عدم العلم بالقاتل عيناً. [۲] قوله : بهذه الرواية؛ أي برواية «الذخيرة» حيث أطلق كلامه ولم يقيده (۳) قوله : هذا؛ أي التفصيلُ المذكور سابقاً من «الهداية» و«الذخيرة».
Book of Prayer, Section on the Martyr. 'Except when it is known that he was killed by an iron weapon unjustly.' I say: This narration contradicts what is mentioned in al-Dhakhira, because the narration of al-Hidayah applies when his killer is not known, for it gave the reason for the obligation of qasama, and qasama is only when the killer is not known. So in the case of not knowing the killer, if it is known that the killing was by iron, according to the narration of al-Hidayah, he is not washed, because this very killing made qisas obligatory. As for the obligation of diya and qasama, it is due to the impediment of inability to establish qisas, so this impediment does not exclude him from being a martyr. [1] His saying: 'Except when it is known that he was killed by iron', its completion: 'Because what is obligatory in it is qisas, and it is a punishment, and the killer does not escape it either in this world or the Hereafter.' End. This reasoning is explicit that what is meant by his saying 'except when it is known that he was killed by iron' is when his killer is known, and the commentators of al-Hidayah like Ibn al-Humam, al-Saghanaki, al-Ayni, and others stated this, and thus falls what the commentator understood and built upon it as being contradictory to what is in al-Dhakhira. [2] His saying: 'This narration...' etc., its summary based on what is in it is that the statement of al-Hidayah is only concerning one whose killer is not known, as evidenced by it making qasama obligatory, and there is no diya or qasama when the killer is known. And his saying 'except when it is known...' is an exception from his previous statement, so it is known from it that if a slain person is found whose killer is not known and it is known that he was killed by an iron weapon unjustly, he is not washed. [3] His saying: 'As for the obligation...' etc., is a rebuttal of the misconception of how can qasama and diya be obligatory while it necessitates qisas. [4] His saying: 'So it does not remove him...' etc. The commentator al-Harawi said in his commentary on the author's previous statement: 'Because qasama becomes obligatory in it, so as if the life remains by the survival of its compensation, even if found in a wilderness...' (from al-Hidayah 1:95, and the rest of his statement: 'because what is obligatory is qisas...'). This is what the apparent wording of al-Hidayah indicates, which is what Sadr al-Shari'ah held, but the commentators of al-Hidayah, as we explained, said: He is not washed if the killer is specified, and they interpreted the words of the author of al-Hidayah based on that as previously mentioned. (From al-Hidayah 2:149). Ibn al-Humam said in al-Fath (1:109): 'Meaning, and he knows his killer specifically, as for merely finding him slaughtered it does not prevent washing him, and this can be derived from his statement: because what is obligatory is qisas; because its obligation is only realized against a specific killer...' and similar is in al-Inayah (1:109), al-Kifayah (1:109), etc. As for according to the narration of al-Dhakhira, he is washed. The wording of al-Dhakhira is: 'If the killing occurred by an iron weapon, if his killer is not known, diya and qasama are obligatory upon the people of the quarter, so he is washed; and if the killer is known, he is not washed according to us.' In al-Dhakhira, the killing itself is not considered, so the obligation of diya, even if due to an impediment, takes him out of martyrdom. In al-Matn, this narration is followed; this is when it is known by which tool he was killed, not close to it, 'Imran [?], if he is not washed. Similarly, if his killer is known, then the obligation is qisas, a punishment whose benefit returns to the guardian of the slain and the rest of the Muslims also, not only to the slain; so it is the ruling of the martyrs of Uhud, unlike diya. In al-Hidayah and al-Khulasah: 'Except when it is known that he was killed by an iron weapon, but his killer is not known, then he is washed, because what is obligatory there is qasama, and its benefit returns to the deceased so that his debts may be paid and his bequests executed from the diya.' In al-Nihayah: 'This qualification, meaning if his killer is known, is derived from al-Hidayah from his statement: because what is obligatory is qisas, which is only against a known killer.' In al-Kirmani: 'In the book there is an indication of it, because it is only unjust if the killer is known, otherwise it might be that he [the killer] was acting unlawfully [without being a specific aggressor?], so the killing would not be unjust. So you must not interpret the wording of al-Hidayah by its apparent meaning as was done in al-Sadriyyah and objected to.' [1] His saying: 'due to the impediment' i.e., not knowing the killer specifically. [2] His saying: 'by this narration' i.e., the narration of al-Dhakhira where he gave a general statement without qualification. [3] His saying: 'this' i.e., the above-mentioned detail from al-Hidayah and al-Dhakhira.
Source pages p. 383

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute and critical refinement regarding the ruling on washing a martyr (ghusl al-shahid) killed by an iron weapon. The core issue is whether the obligation of qisas or diya/qasama determines the need for ghusl. The text reconciles two narrations: one from al-Hidayah (apparently saying no ghusl) and one from al-Dhakhira (saying ghusl if killer unknown). The commentary by later authorities clarifies that the Hidayah's exception ('except when known to be killed by iron') actually applies only when the killer is known, because only then qisas is obligatory. When the killer is unknown, qasama and diya take effect, and the benefit of the diya goes to the deceased's estate (for debts and bequests), so he is not considered a martyr in the sense of being unwashed. Thus the final, relied-upon position is: if the killer is known, no ghusl (due to qisas, a punishment); if unknown, ghusl is performed (due to diya benefiting the deceased). The passage also compares to the martyrs of Uhud, who were not washed, because their killers were known and qisas applied.

Key Terms
شهيد (shahīd): A person killed unjustly; in this context, a martyr who may or may not be washed based on the circumstances of his death.
قسامة (qasāma): An oath taken by fifty people from the victim's community to establish liability for murder when the killer is unknown, leading to diya.
دية (diya): Blood money paid to the victim's heirs as compensation for homicide when qisas is not applicable.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a slain person is found with a wound from an iron weapon but the killer is unknown, what is the Hanafi ruling on washing him?
A) He is not washed because the killing was by iron.
B) He is washed because the killer is unknown and diya/qasama apply.
C) He is not washed regardless of the killer's identity.
D) He is washed only if the iron weapon was not the cause of death.

Key Takeaway
A martyr killed by an iron weapon is washed if the killer is unknown, due to the benefit of diya; otherwise, if known, no ghusl due to qisas.

The Hanafi Conditions for Washing the Martyr (Shahid)
Explaining when a person killed is exempt from washing, with detailed intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi conditions that determine whether a person killed in battle is washed or not, and distinguish between cases of known/unknown killer and post-injury worldly activities.

Core Idea
If the killing is unjust and death immediate without worldly care, the martyr is not washed; otherwise washing is wajib.

The status of a martyr (shahid) carries special rulings: his body is not washed, and he is buried in his bloodied clothes. However, not every person killed in battle qualifies for this status. The Hanafi school carefully examines the circumstances of death, including whether the killing was unjust, whether the killer is known, and whether the victim engaged in worldly activities after injury. This lesson unpacks these conditions from the classical commentary 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah.

Guided Path
1
Identify the default ruling
Start with the principle: washing the deceased is wajib (obligatory). The exemption to not wash is only for the true martyr. Establish that we are looking for conditions that lift the obligation.
The default is washing; martyrdom is a dispensation.
2
Distinguish just vs. unjust killing
If the killing is just (e.g., hadd punishment or qisas), the person is not a martyr and must be washed. Only unjust killing (zulm) can confer martyrdom.
Just killing is not considered oppression.
3
Check if the killer is known
If the killer is known, the obligation is qisas (retaliation), which benefits the guardian and community, not the deceased. Therefore, the dead is not a martyr. If the killer is unknown, the obligation is diyah (blood-money), which benefits the deceased (e.g., to pay debts), so washing is required.
This is a key distinction: qisas vs. diyah.
4
Check for post-injury worldly activities (irtath)
If the wounded person is carried from the battlefield alive and then sleeps, eats, drinks, receives treatment, is sheltered, or transported, he loses martyrdom status and must be washed, because he engaged in worldly life after injury.
Any engagement with worldly affairs after injury breaks the martyrdom.
5
Apply the combined ruling
Only if the killing is unjust, immediate, and without any post-injury worldly care, the person is not washed. In all other scenarios (just killing, unknown killer, irtath, unsure cause), washing is wajib.
The Hanafi position is that washing is the default; martyrdom is a narrow exception.

وأما على رواية «الذخيرة» فيُغْسَل ، وعبارة «الذخيرة) هذه : وإن حصل القتل بحديدة، فإن لم يعلم قاتله تجب الدِّيةُ والقسامة على أهل المحلة فيُغْسَل، وإن علم القاتل لم يُغْسَل عندنا. ففي «الذخيرة» لم يُعتبر نفس القتل ، فوجوب الدِّية وإن كان بالعارض أخرجَهُ عن الشهادة. وفي «المتن» أخذ بهذه الرواية هذا إذا عُلِمَ أنه بأي آلة قتل ليس بقربها ،عمران، إن لم يغسل ، وكذا إن علم قاتله فإن الواجب حينئذٍ القصاص ، عقوبة يرجع نفعها إلى ولي القتيل وسائر المسلمين أيضاً لا إلى المقتول فقط ، فهو في وهو حكم شهداء أحد ، بخلاف الدية. وفي «الهداية» و«الخلاصة» : إلا إذا علم أنه قتل بحديدة، ولكن لم يعلم قاتله يغسل، لما أن الواجب هناك القسامة، ونفعها يعود إلى الميت حتى يقضي دينه وينفذ وصاياه في الدية. وفي «النهاية»: هذا القيد، أعني إذا علم قاتله ، يستفاد من «الهداية» من قوله : لأن الواجب فيه القصاص، إنما يكون على القاتل المعلوم. وفي «الكرماني»: في الكتاب إشارة إليه لأنه إنما يكون ظلمـاً لـو كـان القاتل معلوماً، حتى لو لم يعلم جاز أن يكون متعدياً فلا يكون القتل ظلماً ، فعليك أن لا تحمل عبارة «الهداية» على ظاهرها كما حمل في الصدرية واعترض عليه.
أو قتل بحد أو قصاص أو جُرحَ وارتت بأن نام ، أو أكل أو شرب، أو عولج، أو آواه خيمة ، أو نُقل من المعركة حياً وأما إذا لم يُعْلَم ، فأقول : يَجِبُ أن يُغْسَل ؛ لأنَّه لم يُعْلَمْ أَنَّ موجب نفسِ هذا القتل ما هو ، فلم يُمكن اعتباره ، فلا بُدَّ أن يُعتبر ما هو الواجب في مثل هذا القتل سواء كان أصلياً ، أو عارضياً فالواجب الدية ، فلا يكون شهيداً . (أو قتل بحد أو قصاص) ؛ لأنَّ هذا القتل ليس بظلم ، أو جُرحَ وارتث) بأن نام ، أو أكل، أو شرب ، أو عُولج ، أو آواه ، خيمة ، أو نُقل من المعركة حيا [١] قوله : إن موجب ؛ بفتح الجيم : أي ما يوجبه القتل من القصاص والدية. [٢] قوله : فلم يمكن اعتباره ؛ حاصله : أنه إذا لم يعلم ما قتل به أنه حديدة ، حتى يكون موجباً للقصاص ، أو غير حديدة حتى يكون موجباً للدية ، لم يمكن اعتبار موجب القتل في باب إثبات الشهادة وعدمه.فينبغي أن يعتبر ما يكون واجباً في مثل هذا القتل سواء كان واجباً أصلياً أو غير أصلي، وهو الدية فإنّها في بعض الصور وجوبها أصلي، وفي بعضها عارضي بعارض عدم العلم بآلة القتل، وإذا ثبت وجوبُ الدِّية ارتفعت أحكامُ الشَّهادة. [٣] قوله : أو قتل بحد ؛ داخل تحت قوله : «غسل» ؛ يعني يغسلُ مَن يُقتل حداً كما في الزنا أو قصاصاً ؛ لأن مثل هذا القتل ليس بظلم، بل جزاء ومعاوضة، فلا يكون شهادة في معنى شهادة شهداء أحد. [٤] قوله : وارتت ؛ قال الهَرَويّ : الرَّتْ الشَّيءُ البالي، وجمعه رثاث، وارتت فلان على وزن افتعل، على بناء ما لم يسم فاعله : أي حمل من المعركة رثيثاً : أى جريحاً، وفي الشَّرع : عبارة عمّن صارَ خلقاً في حكم الشهادة لنيل منافع الحياة. [0] قوله : أو آواه ؛ بمد الهمزة وقصرها، والإيواء بالفارسية : جاى دادن ، ، والمراد به هاهنا ما إذا أضربت الخيمة عليه، وهو في مكانه وإلا فهي مسألة النقل من المعركة. [٦] قوله : أو نقل ؛ أي ذلك المجروح بشرط أن يكون يعقل، فلو لم يعقل لا يغسل، وإن زاد على يوم وليلة. كذا في «البحر» (۷) قوله : حياً ؛ أي حال كونه حياً وقت النقل، سواءً وصل حياً أو مات على الأيدي .
As for the narration of al-Dhakhira, he should be washed. The statement of al-Dhakhira is: If the killing occurs with an iron instrument, and his killer is not known, blood-money and qasamah are obligatory upon the people of the locality, so he should be washed. If the killer is known, he should not be washed according to us. In al-Dhakhira, the killing itself is not considered; the obligation of blood-money, even if by accident, removed him from martyrdom. And in the Matn, this narration was adopted. This if it is known with what instrument he was killed... Similarly, if his killer is known, then the obligation is retaliation, a punishment whose benefit returns to the guardian of the slain and all Muslims, not to the slain only. This is the ruling of the martyrs of Uhud, unlike blood-money. In al-Hidayah and al-Khulasa: Unless it is known that he was killed with an iron instrument, but his killer is not known, he should be washed, because the obligation there is qasamah, and its benefit returns to the deceased so that his debt may be paid and his will executed from the blood-money. In al-Nihayah: This restriction, meaning if his killer is known, is derived from al-Hidayah from his saying: Because the obligation there is retaliation, which only applies to the known killer. In al-Kirmani: In the book there is an indication to it, because it would be unjust only if the killer were known; if not known, it is possible that he was a wrongdoer, so the killing is not unjust. So you should not take the statement of al-Hidayah at face value, as was done in al-Sadriyyah and objected to. Or one who is killed by a fixed punishment or retaliation, or is wounded and then carried away (irtath) such that he sleeps, eats, drinks, is treated, is sheltered in a tent, or is moved from the battlefield alive. And as for if it is not known, I say: He must be washed, because it is not known what the consequence of this killing is, so it cannot be considered. So consideration must be given to what is obligatory in such a killing, whether original or incidental. The obligation is blood-money, so he is not a martyr. (Or killed by a fixed punishment or retaliation) because this killing is not unjust. Or wounded and then carried away, such that he sleeps, eats, drinks, is treated, is sheltered in a tent, or is moved from the battlefield alive. [1] His saying: 'What necessitates' — with a fatha on the jim: meaning what the killing necessitates of retaliation and blood-money. [2] His saying: 'So it cannot be considered' — the gist: if it is not known with what he was killed, whether iron (necessitating retaliation) or not iron (necessitating blood-money), the consequence of the killing cannot be considered in the matter of establishing martyrdom or not. So one should consider what is obligatory in such a killing, whether original or incidental, and that is blood-money, for in some cases its obligation is original, in others incidental due to the accident of not knowing the instrument of killing. When blood-money is established, the rulings of martyrdom are lifted. [3] His saying: 'Or killed by a fixed punishment' — falls under his statement: 'washed'; meaning one who is killed by a fixed punishment (e.g., for adultery) or by retaliation should be washed, because this killing is not unjust, but rather a recompense and exchange, so it is not martyrdom in the sense of the martyrs of Uhud. [4] His saying: 'and he was carried away (irtath)' — al-Harawi said: al-ratt is the worn-out thing, its plural ruthath; 'irtath' follows the pattern of 'ifta'ala' in the passive voice: meaning carried from the battlefield worn out, i.e., wounded. In Sacred Law, it is an expression for one who becomes weak in the ruling of martyrdom by attaining the benefits of life. [5] His saying: 'or gave him shelter' — with a long or short hamza; in Persian 'jai dadan'. What is meant here is if a tent was set up over him while he was in his place; otherwise, it is the matter of moving from the battlefield. [6] His saying: 'or moved' — i.e., that wounded person on condition that he is conscious; if unconscious, he should not be washed, even if more than a day and night passes. Thus in al-Bahr. [7] His saying: 'alive' — i.e., in the state of being alive at the time of moving, whether he arrives alive or dies in the hands.
Source pages p. 384

Commentary
This passage, from the commentary 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah on Sharh al-Wiqayah, examines the rulings on washing the body of a person killed (shahid) in various scenarios. It begins by discussing two narrations: the common narration from al-Dhakhira states that if a person is killed with an iron weapon and the killer is unknown, blood-money (diyah) and oaths (qasamah) are obligatory on the locality, and the dead person is to be washed; if the killer is known, he is not washed. This is because when the killer is unknown, the obligation of blood-money (even if incidental) removes the status of martyrdom. The author then notes that the matn (main text) adopts this narration. When the killer is known, retaliation (qisas) is obligatory, which benefits the guardian and the community, not the deceased, hence the dead is not washed (like martyrs of Uhud). Al-Hidayah and al-Khulasa add a detail: if killed with iron but killer unknown, washing is required because the qasamah obligation benefits the deceased (e.g., to pay debts from the blood-money). Al-Nihayah clarifies that the condition 'killer known' is derived from al-Hidayah's statement that retaliation is only on a known killer. Al-Kirmani warns against taking al-Hidayah literally: if the killer is unknown, the killing might be by a wrongdoer and thus not 'unjust', so the deceased does not gain martyrdom. The passage then lists cases where washing is mandatory: death by fixed punishment (hadd) or retaliation, because such killings are just punishments, not oppression. Also, if a wounded person from battle is carried away alive and then sleeps, eats, drinks, receives treatment, is sheltered, or moved—these actions show he survived the battle and thus is not a martyr; he must be washed. Finally, if the cause of death is unknown, the default is that blood-money is due, so he is not a martyr and must be washed. The commentary then explains key terms: 'muujib' (what death necessitates), 'irtath' (carried wounded), 'awahu' (sheltered), and 'nuqila' (moved alive). It emphasizes that the ruling hinges on whether the killing was unjust (zulm) and whether the deceased engaged in worldly activities after injury.

Key Terms
شهيد (shahid): One killed unjustly in battle while fighting for Islam, who is not washed and buried in his bloodied clothes.
دية (diyah): Blood-money; financial compensation for homicide or bodily harm, paid to the heirs of the victim.
قصاص (qisas): Retaliation; punishment of taking life for life in cases of intentional murder.
قسامة (qasamah): Oath-taking by fifty men from the locality to establish liability for an unknown killer, leading to blood-money.
ارتث (irtath): To be carried from the battlefield wounded and alive, after which he engages in worldly activities.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the ruling on washing a person who is killed by a fixed punishment (hadd) or retaliation (qisas)?
A) He is not washed because it is unjust.
B) He must be washed because the killing is not unjust; it is a just punishment.
C) He is not washed if the killer is known.
D) He is washed only if he survives more than a day.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school strictly limits martyrdom to those killed unjustly in battle with immediate death and no post-injury worldly care; any other situation requires washing.

Ruling on Irtithath: Signs That Negate Martyrdom and Require Ghusl
Defining irtithath and exceptions to martyr status affecting ghusl

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the signs of irtithath and state the ruling on ghusl for a wounded person who exhibits them.

Core Idea
A wounded fighter who eats, sleeps, or is moved alive loses martyr status and must be washed.

In Hanafi fiqh, the general rule from the martyrs of Uhud is that martyrs are not washed. However, this rule is restricted to those who die immediately without any signs of life after being wounded. This lesson explains the concept of irtithath – the state of showing signs of life after the wound – which negates the worldly ruling of martyrdom and makes ghusl obligatory. We will explore the specific acts that constitute irtithath and the reasoning behind them.

Guided Path
1
Identify the legal definition of irtithath
Understand that irtithath literally means 'worn out' and in Shari'ah refers to a wounded person who shows signs of benefiting from life after being wounded, thus losing the worldly status of a martyr.
The term is derived from 'ratt' (worn-out garment) – the person becomes like a corpse in terms of martyrdom rulings.
2
List the signs of irtithath mentioned in the text
From the passage: sleeping, eating, drinking, receiving medical treatment, being sheltered in a tent, being moved from the battlefield alive, remaining conscious at prayer time, and making a bequest. Each of these indicates the person benefited from life after the wound.
These signs are considered only if they occur after the end of the war; during active fighting, they do not count as irtithath.
3
Understand the exceptions where ghusl is not required
The text excludes those killed by prescribed punishment (hadd) or retaliation (qisas) because such killings are not oppression, so these individuals are not considered martyrs at all and must be washed. Also, if the cause of death is unknown (uncertain weapon), the default is blood-money (diya), which negates martyrdom and requires ghusl.
The principle: if the killing is not oppression, the victim is not a martyr in the worldly sense.
4
Examine the intra-Hanafi dispute on bequest
Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf consider any bequest (wasiyyah) as a sign of irtithath. Muhammad al-Shaybani distinguishes between bequests for worldly matters (irtithath) and bequests for afterlife matters (not irtithath). The relied-upon position is Muhammad's view.
The dispute is mentioned in the commentary, and the preferred view is that bequests for the afterlife do not negate martyrdom.
5
Apply the rule to a scenario
Given a wounded fighter who drinks water and then dies, identify that drinking is a sign of irtithath, so he is not a worldly martyr and ghusl is wajib.

[٣] قوله : أو قتل بحد ؛ داخل تحت قوله : «غسل» ؛ يعني يغسلُ مَن يُقتل حداً كما في الزنا أو قصاصاً ؛ لأن مثل هذا القتل ليس بظلم، بل جزاء ومعاوضة، فلا يكون شهادة في معنى شهادة شهداء أحد. [٤] قوله : وارتت ؛ قال الهَرَويّ : الرَّتْ الشَّيءُ البالي، وجمعه رثاث، وارتت فلان على وزن افتعل، على بناء ما لم يسم فاعله : أي حمل من المعركة رثيثاً : أى جريحاً، وفي الشَّرع : عبارة عمّن صارَ خلقاً في حكم الشهادة لنيل منافع الحياة. (۲) اختلفوا في قول أبي يوسف ومحمد في اعتبار الايصاء : فقال صاحب «البحر»(١ : ٢١٤): الأظهر أنه لا خلاف، فجواب أبي يوسف بأن يكون مرتباً فيما إذا كان بأمور الدنيا، وجواب محمد بعدمه فيما إذا كان بأمور الآخرة، وقال صاحب «الدر المختار»(١ : ٦١٠) : إن أوصى بأمور الآخرة لا يصير مرتنا عند محمد وهو الأصح. ونقل في «البرهان» عن كل من أبي يوسف ومحمد قولين، فقال: ويطرد أبو يوسف الارتثاث في الوصية بأمور الدنيا فقط، أو مطلقاً، وخالفه محمد في وصية الآخرة، فلم يجعله مرتثا، أو مطلقاً. كذا في «غنية ذوي الأحكام»(۱ : ۱۷۰). وينظر : «التبيين» (١) : ٢٤٩).
Book of Prayer, Chapter on the Martyr.
Or if killed by a prescribed punishment or retaliation, or wounded and became a corpse-like (irtath) such that he slept, ate, drank, or was treated, or a tent sheltered him, or he was moved from the battlefield alive. But if it is not known, I say: It is obligatory to wash him, because it is not known what the cause of his death is, so it cannot be considered. Therefore, one must consider what is obligatory in such a killing, whether original or accidental: the obligatory (compensation) is blood-money, so he is not a martyr. (Or killed by prescribed punishment or retaliation) because such killing is not oppression. Or wounded and became a corpse-like (irtath) such that he slept, ate, drank, was treated, or a tent sheltered him, or was moved from the battlefield alive. [1] His saying: 'the cause' – with fathah on the jim: meaning what the killing necessitates of retaliation or blood-money. [2] His saying: 'so it cannot be considered' – the gist: if it is not known with what he was killed, whether it was an iron implement so that retaliation is necessitated, or not an iron implement so that blood-money is necessitated, then the cause of the killing cannot be considered in establishing martyrdom or its absence. Therefore, one must consider what is obligatory in such a killing, whether the obligation is original or accidental, which is blood-money, for in some cases its obligation is original, and in some cases accidental due to lack of knowledge of the instrument of killing, and when blood-money is established, the rulings of martyrdom are lifted. [3] His saying: 'or killed by prescribed punishment' – included under his 'ghusl; meaning one who is killed by a prescribed punishment as in zina or retaliation is washed, because such killing is not oppression, but rather retribution and compensation, so he is not a martyr in the sense of the martyrs of Uhud. [4] His saying: 'irtath' – al-Harawi said: al-ratt means worn-out thing, its plural is rithath, and irtatta 'ala wazn ifta'ala, based on unstated subject: meaning he was carried from the battlefield wounded. In Shari'ah, it refers to one who has become worn out (like a corpse) in the ruling of martyrdom due to attaining benefits of life. [5] His saying: 'or sheltered' – with madd or qasr of the hamzah, al-iwa in Persian: jay dadan (giving place). What is meant here is when the tent is pitched over him, and he is in his place; otherwise, it is the issue of being moved from the battlefield. [6] His saying: 'or moved' – i.e., that wounded man, provided he is conscious; if he is not conscious, he is not washed even if it exceeds a day and night. This is from 'al-Bahr'. [7] His saying: 'alive' – meaning at the time of being moved, whether he arrives alive or dies upon the hands.
Or he remained conscious at the time of a prayer, or bequeathed something, and they prayed over them. [1] His saying: 'or bequeathed' – similarly if he spoke much speech. [2] His saying: 'and they prayed over them' – i.e., over those mentioned: the child, the junub, the slain for which qasama is due, the wounded (murtath), and the one killed by prescribed punishment or retaliation. [3] His saying: 'something of the benefits' – i.e., the benefits of life; so if he benefited and a ruling of the living was established for him, the ruling of the martyrs of Uhud does not apply to him, because he is not in their category. Hence it has been narrated that 'Umar and other Companions who were martyred were washed. [4] His saying: 'at the time of a prayer' – the intended meaning is the amount (of time) that obligates prayer upon him, and it becomes a debt upon him. This is from 'al-Mujtaba'. [5] His saying: 'contrary to Muhammad' – its apparent meaning is that the dispute is regarding whether bequest constitutes irtithath. Al-Sadr al-Shahid mentioned in 'Sharh al-Jami' al-Saghir' that the dispute is about when he bequeathed something of worldly matters, it is irtithath by consensus. (1) All this is when it occurs after the end of war; if before, he is not considered irtath by any of these actions. See: al-Tabyeen (1:249). (2) They differed regarding the statement of Abu Yusuf and Muhammad concerning the consideration of bequest: The author of 'al-Bahr' (1:214) said: the most apparent is that there is no dispute, so Abu Yusuf's answer is that it is irtath when concerning worldly matters, and Muhammad's answer is that it is not concerning afterlife matters. The author of 'al-Durr al-Mukhtar' (1:610) said: if he bequeaths for afterlife matters, he does not become murtath according to Muhammad, and that is the correct view. It is transmitted in 'al-Burhan' from both Abu Yusuf and Muhammad two opinions: he said: Abu Yusuf consistently considers irtithath for bequest only of worldly matters, or absolutely; and Muhammad opposed him regarding bequest for afterlife matters, so he does not consider him murtath, or absolutely. This is from 'Ghunyat Dhawi al-Ahkam' (1:170). See also: al-Tabyeen (1:249).
Source pages p. 385

Commentary
This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah is a commentary on the chapter of the martyr in the Book of Prayer. The topic is the legal definition of a martyr (shahid) in terms of rulings that apply in this world, specifically whether the body is to be washed (ghusl) before burial or not. The author explains that the general rule from the martyr of Uhud is that martyrs are not washed, but this ruling is restricted to those who were killed unlawfully in battle and died immediately without any interval of consciousness or worldly activity. The passage lists several cases where a wounded fighter becomes 'murtath' (literally, ‘worn out’ like a corpse, but legally meaning he has shown signs of life after being wounded) and thus falls under the obligation of washing, because he is not considered a martyr in the worldly sense. These signs include sleeping, eating, drinking, receiving medical treatment, being sheltered, being moved from the battlefield alive, remaining conscious at prayer time, or making a bequest. The reasoning is that such actions prove that the person benefited from life after the wound, so he is treated as a living person who later died; therefore, the rulings of the living (washing, shrouding, prayer) apply to him. The commentary also addresses a case of uncertainty about the cause of death: if it is unknown whether the weapon was iron (requiring retaliation) or not (requiring blood-money), then the default is to consider blood-money, which negates martyrdom and necessitates washing. Additionally, there is an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the effect of a bequest (wasiyyah): Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf consider it a sign of irtithath, while Muhammad al-Shaybani distinguishes between bequests for worldly matters (which cause irtithath) and bequests for afterlife matters (which do not). The relied-upon position (mufta bihi) according to al-Durr al-Mukhtar is that bequest for afterlife matters is not irtithath according to Muhammad, and that is the preferred view. Finally, it is noted that these rulings apply only after the end of the war; during active fighting, any such actions do not make one murtath.

Key Terms
ارتث (irtath): Literally, 'became worn out'; legally, a wounded person who shows signs of life after being wounded, such as eating, drinking, sleeping, etc., thus losing the worldly status of a martyr.
شهيد (shahid): A martyr; in this context, one who is killed in battle against unbelievers with the intention of raising Allah's word. Only those who die immediately without worldly benefits are martyrs for both this world and the Hereafter.
غسل (ghusl): Ritual washing of the deceased body; required for all Muslims except certain categories of martyrs.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A wounded fighter is killed by a prescribed punishment (hadd) for zina after being wounded. According to the Hanafi school, what is the ruling on washing his body?
A) He is a martyr and not washed.
B) He must be washed because killing by hadd negates martyrdom.
C) He is washed only if he showed signs of irtithath.
D) He is not washed because he was already wounded.

Key Takeaway
A wounded fighter who shows any sign of life after being wounded (sleep, eat, drink, treatment, shelter, movement, prayer consciousness, bequest) loses worldly martyrdom and requires ghusl.

Irtithath and the Dispute over Wasiyyah
Intra-Hanafi dispute on wasiyyah as irtithath

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi definition of irtithath and apply the ruling on wasiyyah in the intra-Hanafi dispute.

Core Idea
A will for worldly matters is irtithath by consensus; for Hereafter, only Muhammad's view is preferred.

This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah clarifies when a battlefield survivor becomes irtithath—losing the martyr's treatment. The key condition disputed is making a will. Understanding this dispute is crucial for knowing when ghusl and janazah are wajib versus when they are not.

Guided Path
1
Define irtithath legally
From the source: irtithath means the wounded person benefits from life's necessities or any legal ruling of the living applies. This removes the shahid status.
Classical definition from Hanafi texts.
2
Identify the conditions of irtithath
The passage lists: remaining sane at prayer time, making a will, speaking a lot, moving oneself (except due to fear). Any one of these triggers irtithath.
Conditions are cumulative or alternative.
3
Locate the intra-Hanafi dispute on wasiyyah
Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf: any will (worldly or otherworldly) is irtithath. Muhammad: only a worldly will is irtithath; a will for the Hereafter is not.
The dispute is only among Hanafi imams.
4
Determine the mufta bihi position
The text states that Muhammad's view is 'al-ashah' (most correct) per al-Durr al-Mukhtar. Thus, if a wounded soldier makes a will for charity, he does not become irtithath and remains a shahid.
Tarjih by later Hanafi authorities.

Source Text

Or he remained sane at the time of prayer, or he made a will for something, and prayer was performed over them. The one brought from battle with life remaining is defined as: he who is carried from the battlefield with some life in him. Irtithath in Shari'ah is: that he benefits from some of the benefits of life, or a ruling of the rulings of the living is established for him. If he remained sane at the time of prayer, prayer becomes obligatory upon him, and this is from the rulings of the living. Making a will is considered irtithath according to Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf, may Allah have mercy on them, contrary to Muhammad. It is mentioned in al-Kafi, al-Manba' Sharh al-Majma', al-Ghurar, and others: that movement, and likewise the wounded person moving himself from one place to another, is only considered irtithath if it is not due to fear of being trampled by horses or the like. [1] His statement: Or he made a will; likewise if he spoke a lot. [2] His statement: And prayer was performed over them; meaning over those mentioned: the child, the junub, the slain for whom qasama is required, the one brought with life, and the one killed by hadd or qisas. (3) His statement: with something of the benefits; meaning the benefits of life. So if he benefits and a ruling of the living is established for him, the ruling of the martyrs of Uhud does not apply to him because he is not in their meaning. Thus it is reported that 'Umar and others of the Companions who were martyred were washed. [4] His statement: time of prayer; meaning the amount of time that makes prayer obligatory upon him, and it becomes a debt in his liability. As in al-Mujtaba. [0] His statement: contrary to Muhammad; its apparent meaning is that the disagreement is regarding whether the will is irtithath. Al-Sadr al-Shahid mentioned in Sharh al-Jami' al-Saghir that the disagreement is when he makes a will for worldly matters; it is irtithath by consensus. (1) All this is if it occurs after the war ends. As for before its end, he does not become one who is brought with life by any of what is mentioned. See: al-Tabyin (1:249). (2) They disagreed regarding the statement of Abu Yusuf and Muhammad concerning the consideration of the will. The author of al-Bahr (1:214) said: The more apparent is that there is no disagreement. So the response of Abu Yusuf is that it is irtithath if it is concerning worldly matters, and the response of Muhammad is that it is not if it is concerning matters of the Hereafter. The author of al-Durr al-Mukhtar (1:610) said: If he makes a will concerning matters of the Hereafter, he does not become one brought with life according to Muhammad, and that is the most correct. It is transmitted in al-Burhan from both Abu Yusuf and Muhammad two statements each. He said: Abu Yusuf extends irtithath in the will concerning worldly matters only, or absolutely; and Muhammad opposed him in the will concerning the Hereafter, so he did not consider it irtithath, or absolutely. As in Ghunyat Dhawi al-Ahkam (1:170). See: al-Tabyin (1:249). (3) Ghurar al-Ahkam (1:170).
Source pages p. 386

Commentary
This passage is an expansion probe into the concept of irtithath, which determines whether a battlefield survivor is treated as a living person or a martyr. The commentator explains that irtithath occurs when the injured person benefits from life's necessities or when any legal ruling of the living applies. Two key conditions are highlighted: remaining sane at the time of an obligatory prayer (indicating consciousness and obligation) and making a will (wasiyyah). The ruling on wasiyyah involves an intra-Hanafi dispute: Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf consider it irtithath, while Muhammad al-Shaybani disagrees. The commentary further notes that the dispute is narrowed: if the will concerns worldly matters, all agree it is irtithath; if it concerns matters of the Hereafter, Muhammad does not consider it irtithath, and this is the preferred position (al-ashah). Additional points: speaking a lot or moving oneself (except due to fear) also constitutes irtithath. The commentary explicitly states that those who show these signs must be washed and prayed over, unlike the true martyrs of Uhud who were not washed. This is a critical refinement: the definition and conditions are derived from the matn and clarified through inter-madhhab and intra-Hanafi discussions.

Key Terms
الارتثاث (al-irtithath): The state of a wounded soldier carried from the battlefield with some life remaining, showing signs of benefit from life or establishment of a legal ruling.
الوصية (al-wasiyyah): A bequest or will made by the wounded person, which indicates consciousness and thus removes shahid status.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the relied-upon position (al-ashah), if a wounded soldier makes a will concerning matters of the Hereafter (e.g., charity), is he considered irtithath?
A) Yes, according to all Hanafi imams.
B) No, according to Imam Muhammad, and this is the preferred view.
C) Yes, only according to Abu Hanifa.
D) No, according to all imams.

Key Takeaway
Irtithath is determined by actions indicating life; a will for worldly matters always triggers it, but a will for the Hereafter does not, per the preferred Hanafi view.

Washing and Prayer for Rebels and Highway Robbers
States ruling on ghusl and janazah prayer for rebels and highway robbers

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on ghusl and janazah prayer for those killed for rebellion or highway robbery and explain the rationale of deterrence.

Core Idea
Rebels and highway robbers are not martyrs: they are washed but denied janazah prayer as a deterrent.

In this brief passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah, the author provides a clear ruling on two categories: those killed for rebellion (baghi) and highway robbery (qati' al-tariq). While they are to be washed like any deceased Muslim, the janazah prayer is withheld as a form of societal deterrence and policy. This lesson unpacks the ruling and its rationale.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two categories
Recognize that the passage covers two distinct groups: the rebel (al-baghi) and the highway robber (qati' al-tariq). The phrase 'wa nahwihi' extends the ruling to similar offenders.
Both are considered Muslims but not martyrs.
2
State the ruling for each
For both categories, the body is washed (ghusl) but the janazah prayer is not performed. This is the core legal prescription.
Washing is wajib; prayer is prohibited (haram in this context).
3
Understand the rationale
The commentator explains that prayer is omitted as a deterrent (zajran), a policy matter (siyasatan), and a lesson (ibratan). They are not martyrs, so washing is required.
The rationale distinguishes them from true martyrs who are neither washed nor prayed over.
4
Note the extension
The phrase 'wa nahwihi' indicates that the ruling applies to others similar in criminality, such as apostates or armed bandits, by analogy.
This prevents narrow interpretation.
5
Apply to a modern scenario
Consider a modern armed robber killed in the act. The ruling still applies: wash the body, do not perform janazah prayer to deter crime.
The illa (effective cause) is criminal aggression against society.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإن قتل لبغي، أو قطع طريق غُسل ولا يُصلى عليه. [1]قوله: إن قتل؛ الحاصلُ أنَّ الباغي إذا قتل وقاطعُ الطَّرِيقِ ونحوهُ إذا قتل يغسل؛ لأنه ليس بشهيد ولا يُصلّى عليه زجراً أو سياسة وعبرة.

[1] His statement: if killed; the summary is that the rebel if killed, and the highway robber and similar if killed, he is washed because he is not a martyr, and prayer is not performed over him as a deterrent or policy and lesson.

Commentary
This is a brief ruling statement. The matn states that one who is killed for rebellion (baghi) or highway robbery (qati' al-tariq) is to be washed (ghusl) but the janazah prayer is not performed over him. The commentator explains the rationale: he is not a martyr, so washing is required to remove impurity, but prayer is omitted as a deterrent (zajran) and as a matter of policy (siyasatan) and as a lesson (ibratan). The phrase 'wa nahwihi' indicates that others like apostates or bandits may fall under the same ruling. This is a straightforward ruling without intra-Hanafi dispute in this text.

Key Terms
الباغي (al-baghi): A rebel who fights against the legitimate Muslim ruler with a ta'wil (interpretation).
قاطع الطريق (qati' al-tariq): A highway robber or bandit who attacks travelers.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
Rebels and highway robbers are washed but denied janazah prayer as a deterrent and because they are not martyrs.

Ruling on Washing a Rebel or Highway Robber and Not Praying over Them
Ruling with rationale for washing and omitting prayer

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on washing a rebel/highway robber and the prohibition of funeral prayer, and explain the rationale of deterrence.

Core Idea
A rebel/highway robber is washed but denied funeral prayer as a deterrent – not a martyr.

This lesson addresses a specific fiqh ruling from the chapter on martyrs. It clarifies that rebels and highway robbers are not treated as martyrs, even if killed. The Hanafi school prescribes washing them like ordinary Muslims but omitting the funeral prayer as a disciplinary measure. Understanding this ruling requires distinguishing between the general obligation of washing the dead and the special policy of withholding prayer as a deterrent.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
Determine what the passage is ruling upon: the treatment of a rebel (baghi) or highway robber (qati' tariq) when killed. The ruling has two parts: they are washed (ghusl), and no funeral prayer (salah al-janazah) is performed over them.
Washing is the default for any Muslim; the exception is the martyr.
2
Locate the rationale
The passage gives the reason: 'because he is not a martyr' and 'as a deterrent or policy and lesson'. The first part confirms he does not have the special status, the second part explains the legal wisdom for withholding the prayer.
The rationale is policy (siyasah) and deterrence (zajr), not a textual proof.
3
Contrast with the martyr's treatment
Compare this ruling with the standard martyr's ruling: a shahid is not washed and is buried in his blood-stained clothes, and the funeral prayer is performed. The rebel/robber lacks the conditions of a true martyr: fighting for Allah's cause with the right intention.
The martyr's exemption from washing is a concession; the rebel does not deserve that concession.
4
Note the Hanafi specificity
This ruling is specific to the Hanafi school. Other schools may treat combatants in internal conflicts differently. The Hanafi emphasis on deterrence reflects the school's concern for public order and discouraging rebellion.
The passage does not mention other schools; this step adds cross-madhhab context.

Source Text

Book of Prayer, Chapter of the Martyr. And if he is killed for rebellion or highway robbery, he is washed and no prayer is performed over him. He says: 'if he is killed'; the gist is that the rebel when killed and the highway robber and similar when killed are washed, because he is not a martyr, and no prayer is performed over him as a deterrent or policy and lesson.
Source pages p. 387

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with its rationale from the chapter on the martyr (shahid). It addresses two types of individuals: a rebel (baghi) who fights against the legitimate ruler, and a highway robber (qati' tariq) who commits banditry. The ruling is that when they are killed, they are to be washed (ghusl) like any other Muslim, but the funeral prayer (janazah) is not performed over them. The reason provided is twofold: first, they do not possess the legal status of a martyr (shahid), which would entitle them to be buried without washing and with prayer. Second, withholding the funeral prayer serves as a deterrent (zajr) and a matter of policy (siyasah) to discourage others from following their path. This ruling is specific to the Hanafi school and is derived from the principle that such individuals are considered sinful aggressors.

Key Terms
شهيد (shahid): A person killed in battle for Islam, entitled to special burial rulings.
باغي (baghi): A rebel who fights against the legitimate imam.
قاطع طريق (qati' tariq): A highway robber who terrorizes travelers.

Key Terms from Source Passage

C) He is washed but funeral prayer is not performed.
D) He is not washed but funeral prayer is performed.

Key Takeaway
A rebel or highway robber is washed like any Muslim but denied funeral prayer as a deterrent, because they lack the status of a martyr.

Validity of Prayer Inside the Ka'bah: Hanafi Position and a Misattribution Corrected
Ruling and corrected dispute on prayer inside Ka'bah

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on performing fard prayer inside the Ka'bah and explain the evidence from the Prophet's action and qiyas.

Core Idea
Prayer inside Ka'bah is valid for fard and nafl; no evidence excludes fard.

Many assume that only voluntary prayer can be performed inside the Ka'bah, but classical Hanafi texts affirm both obligatory and voluntary prayers. This lesson explores the hadith evidence, the qiyas extending the ruling to fard, and a common misattribution to Imam al-Shafi'i that the commentator clarifies. You will learn the precise condition for validity and see how later Hanafi scholars defended their position by citing Shafi'i sources.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Identify the matn's claim: both fard and nafl are valid inside the Ka'bah. Note that the Hanafi school holds this view with a condition.
The matn says 'صح فيها الفرض والنفل' (obligatory and voluntary are valid in it).
2
Locate the primary dalil
Find the hadith of the Prophet ﷺ entering the Ka'bah and praying two rak'ahs of nafl. Understand its epistemological weight (zanni al-thubut).
The hadith is in Bukhari and Muslim.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
Explain how the Prophet's action indicates permissibility. Then apply qiyas: since there is no difference between fard and nafl in facing the Ka'bah, fard is also permissible.
The commentator argues: 'من المعلوم أنه لا فرق بين الفرض والنفل في اشتراط استقبال الكعبة' (It is known that there is no difference between fard and nafl in the condition of facing the Ka'bah).
4
Recognize the misattribution
Identify the phrase that might suggest al-Shafi'i disallows prayer inside. Read the commentator's correction citing Shafi'i works.
Al-Saghnāqī clarifies this was a slip; Shafi'i sources confirm permissibility.
5
Identify the condition
Extract the condition stated at the end: the worshipper must face one of the four walls. If facing the door, it must be closed.
Condition ensures proper orientation to the structure.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب الصلاة في الكعبة
صح فيها الفرضُ والنفل
قوله : باب الصلاة في الكعبة ؛ أي هذا باب في أحكام الصلاة في بيت الله الذي يوجه إليه في الصَّلاة، ويطافُ به ، سمي الكعبة ؛ لتربعه. 
[٢]قوله : صح؛ لما ثبت أنَّ النبي دخل الكعبة يوم الفتح وصلّى في داخلها ركعتين نفلاً (٢) ، أخرجه البخاري ومسلم وغيرهما ، ومن المعلوم أنه لا فرق بين الفرضِ والنفل في اشتراط استقبال الكعبة، إلا ما دلَّ الدَّليلُ عليه، ولا دليل هاهنا يدلّ على الفرق فيجوزُ الفرضُ أيضا بلا شبهة.
(٣) قوله : فيها ؛ أي في الفرض والنفل كليهما، فلا يجوز داخلها شيء منهما عنده، قال السغناقي في «النهاية»: كأن هذا اللفظ وقع سهواً من الكاتب، فإنَّ الشَّافعي يرى جواز الصلاة في الكعبة فرضها ونفلها كذا أورده أصحابه في كتبهم من «الوجيز»، و«الخلاصة» و«الذخيرة وغيرها ، ولم يورد أحدٌ من علمائنا أيضاً الخلاف ، فيما عندي من الكتب كـ «المبسوط»، و«الأسرار»، و«الإيضاح»، و«المحيط»، وشروح «الجامع الصغير» وغيرها.

[0]قوله : الجواز ؛ يعني تجوزُ الصَّلاة مطلقاً داخل الكعبة بشرط أن يتوجه إلى جدارٍ من جدر الكعبة الأربعة، فلو توجّه إلى باب الكعبة فإن كان مغلقاً جازت
Chapter of Prayer in the Ka'bah. Obligatory and voluntary prayers are valid in it. He says: Chapter of Prayer in the Ka'bah; meaning this is a chapter on the rulings of prayer in the House of Allah which one faces in prayer and around which one circumambulates; it is called Ka'bah because of its square shape. He says: 'valid'; because it is established that the Prophet entered the Ka'bah on the day of conquest and prayed two rak'ahs of nafl inside, as recorded by Bukhari and Muslim and others. And it is known that there is no difference between obligatory and voluntary regarding the requirement of facing the Ka'bah, except what evidence indicates; and there is no evidence here to indicate a difference, so obligatory is also permissible without doubt. (3) He says: 'in it'; meaning in both obligatory and voluntary, nothing of them is valid inside it according to him. Al-Saghnāqī said in al-Nihāya: 'This word seems to have occurred inadvertently from the writer, for al-Shafi'i holds the permissibility of prayer in the Ka'bah, both obligatory and voluntary, as his followers have recorded in their books like al-Wajīz, al-Khulāṣa, al-Dhakhīra, and others. And none of our scholars have also mentioned a difference, according to what I have in books like al-Mabsūṭ, al-Asrār, al-Īḍāḥ, al-Muḥīṭ, and commentaries on al-Jāmi' al-Ṣaghīr, etc.' (4) He says: 'and the mentioned...'; the purpose of it is to refute the author of al-Hidāyah that it contradicts (what is in Shafi'i books), as in Shafi'i books, and they are more knowledgeable of their Imam's school. (0) He says: 'permissibility'; meaning prayer is absolutely permissible inside the Ka'bah on condition that one faces one of the four walls of the Ka'bah; if one faces the door of the Ka'bah, then if it is closed it is permissible.
Source pages p. 388

Commentary
This passage deals with the validity of prayer inside the Ka'bah. It begins with the matn stating that both obligatory (fard) and voluntary (nafl) prayers are valid. The commentator then explains the heading and provides the evidence: the Prophet ﷺ entered the Ka'bah during the conquest of Makkah and prayed two rak'ahs of nafl inside. From this, the commentator reasons that since there is no distinction between fard and nafl in terms of facing the qibla, and no specific evidence excludes fard, the ruling extends to fard as well. He then addresses a potential misunderstanding: the phrase 'inside it' might be interpreted as meaning that nothing is valid inside. However, the commentator clarifies that this was a slip by a writer, as Imam al-Shafi'i actually allows both fard and nafl inside the Ka'bah, as recorded in his school's standard works. The commentator refutes the author of al-Hidāyah for wrongly attributing a different view to al-Shafi'i. Finally, he states the condition: the prayer is valid inside the Ka'bah provided that the worshipper faces one of its walls. If facing the door, the door must be closed for the prayer to be valid. This condition is because the door opening would break the continuity of the wall and thus not fulfill the requirement of facing the structure.

Key Terms
كعبة (ka'bah): The sacred House of Allah in Makkah, qibla for prayer.
فرض (fard): Obligatory prayer (fard 'ayn or kifayah).
نفل (nafl): Voluntary, supererogatory prayer.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what condition is required for prayer inside the Ka'bah to be valid?
A) Facing the door of the Ka'bah
B) Facing one of its walls
C) Facing the Black Stone
D) Facing any direction

Key Takeaway
Both fard and nafl prayers are valid inside the Ka'bah, facing a wall, and the misattributed Shafi'i position is corrected.

Prayer Inside the Ka'bah: Hanafi Ruling and Proof
Ruling with proof and inter-madhhab critique

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling that both fard and nafl prayers are valid inside the Ka'bah, provide the primary hadith proof, and explain the Hanafi response to the Shafi'i conditional position.

Core Idea
The Prophet's prayer inside the Ka'bah proves both fard and nafl are valid; no distinction exists in facing the qibla.

This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah lays out the Hanafi position on prayer inside the Ka'bah, a topic of practical significance for pilgrims. The Hanafi school holds that both obligatory and supererogatory prayers are valid inside the sacred structure, based on the action of the Prophet (peace be upon him). The commentary also addresses and corrects a misattribution to Imam al-Shafi'i, and critiques the Shafi'i school's conditional approach, particularly the inconsistent ruling regarding a hypothetical collapse of the Ka'bah.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and ruling
What is the act? Prayer inside the Ka'bah. What is the ruling according to the Hanafi school? Both fard and nafl are sahih (valid).
2
Locate the dalil
What proof does the author cite? The hadith that the Prophet entered the Ka'bah on the day of conquest and prayed two rak'ahs inside, reported by Bukhari and Muslim.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
How does this hadith prove fard is also valid? The Prophet prayed nafl, but there is no evidence differentiating fard from nafl in the condition of facing the Ka'bah. By analogy (qiyas), the ruling extends to fard.
4
Analyse the ikhtilaf
What are the Shafi'i conditions? They require facing one of the four walls; if facing the door, it must be closed or the threshold high enough. The commentator also criticizes their ruling on the collapsed Ka'bah as inconsistent.
The commentator corrects a misattribution that Shafi'i disallows fard; he shows Shafi'i books permit with conditions.
