The Ruling on Siwak for a Fasting Person: Hanafi vs Shafi'i
Inter-madhhab dispute on permissibility of siwak in the evening

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on siwak, kohl, and oil for a fasting person and evaluate the Shafi'i counter-argument regarding siwak in the evening.

Core Idea
Siwak is recommended for a fasting person at any time; the khuluf does not prohibit its use.

This lesson examines a classic inter-madhhab dispute: the use of siwak, kohl, and oil by a fasting person. The Hanafi school permits all three without dislike, even in the afternoon. The Shafi'i school, however, considers siwak after noon as disliked because it removes the khuluf (the pleasant smell) from the fasting person's mouth. The passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah presents the Hanafi position and rebuts the Shafi'i evidence, relying on authentic hadith and companion practice.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi Position
Identify the ruling on siwak, kohl, and oil for a fasting person according to the Hanafi school. Note that there is no dislike (karahah) for any of them, even in the evening (after noon).
The passage explicitly says 'لا الكحل، ودهن الشارب، والسواك ولو عَشِياً' – no dislike for kohl, oil, and siwak, even in the evening.
2
Identify the Shafi'i Position and Its Basis
The Shafi'i school holds that siwak is disliked after noon because it removes the khuluf. Understand the basis: the hadith that the khuluf of the fasting person is more fragrant to Allah than musk.
The Shafi'i concern is that removing this 'fragrance' reduces the spiritual reward or virtue of fasting.
3
Analyze the Hanafi Response
The Hanafi school responds that there is no necessary link between using siwak and eliminating the khuluf. Even if it does, the Prophet commanded siwak generally, knowing that khuluf would still be present. This is supported by the hadith 'The best use of the fasting person is siwak' (Ibn Majah).
The passage quotes the story of Mu'adh ibn Jabal, who rejected the notion that siwak should be avoided in the evening.
4
Evaluate the Evidence
Compare the two sets of evidence. The Hanafis prioritize the general command for siwak and the practice of the Companions, while the Shafi'is prioritize the khuluf hadith. The Hanafi approach uses the principle that a general command is not restricted by a general virtue unless there is a direct prohibition.
There is no explicit prohibition of siwak after noon in the primary sources.
5
Determine the Thamarah al-Khilaf
The practical difference: Hanafis can use siwak at any time during fasting, while Shafi'is avoid it after noon. This affects daily practice, especially after 'Asr prayer.
The mufta bihi (relied-upon) position is the Hanafi view.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لا الكحل، ودهن الشارب، والسواك ولو عَشِياً) احترازاً عن قول الشَّافِعِي) إذ عنده يكرَهُ عَشِياً ) ؛ لأنه يزيلُ الخُلوف . [١]قوله : لا الكحل ودهن الشارب ؛ هما بفتح أول الحرف، بمعنى استعمال الكحل بالضم واستعمال الدهن بالضم ، وهما اسمان وجه عدم الكراهة : أنه ليس فيه شيء مناف للصوم ، ولا فيه ما يخوفُ به إلى فساده. (۲) قوله : والسواك ؛ أي استعمال المسواك سواء كان رطباً أو يابساً. [٣] قوله : ولو عشيّاً ؛ بفتح العين، وكسر الشين المعجمة، وتشديد الياء : يُطلقُ على ما بعد الزوال. (٤) قوله : لأنه يزيل الخلوف ؛ هو بفتح الخاء المعجمة، وبضمها : الرائحة الكريهة التي توجد في فم الصائم، وقد ورد في «الصحاح»: «إِنَّ خلوف فم الصائم أطيب عند الله من المسك، فلما كان السواك مصفي للفم، مزيلاً له ، كرهه الشافعي . ولنا إطلاق حديث: «خيرُ خلال الصائم السواك)، أخرجه ابن ماجة، ولا وملا زمة بين استعمال السواك وزوال الخلوف. (۱) ينظر: «التنبيه» (ص ٤٦ ) ، و (تحفة المحتاج (۳) : ٤٣٥) ، و(أسنى المطالب(١ : ٤٢٣)، وغيرهما. (۲) العَشِي : ما بينَ الزَّوَالِ إلى الغرُوبِ ومنهُ يُقَالُ للظهر والعصر صلاتا العشبي، ينظر: «المصباح المنير»(ص٤١٣). (۳) الخلوف : تغير رائحة فم الصائم ينظر: «الصحاح (١ : ٣٦٥). (٤) في (صحيح مسلم (۲) : ۸۰۷) ، و(صحيح البخاري»(٢ : ٦٧٠)، وغيرهما. (٥) وعن عامر بن ربيعة الله قال : رأيت النبي الله يستاك وهو صائم ما لا أحصي أو أعد» في (صحيح البخاري (٢) : ٦٨٢) معلقا ، و سنن أبي داود»(۱ : ۷۲۱)، وغيرها. وعن عبد الرحمن بن غنم له قال : سألت معاذ بن جبل أتتسوك وأنت صائم؟ قال : نعم. قلت : أي النهار أتسوك ؟ قال : أي النهار شئت إن شئت غدوة، وإن شئت عشية . قلت : فإن الناس يكرهونه عشية. قال : ولم ؟ قلت يقولون : إن رسول الله له قال : «لخلوف فم الصائم أطيب من ريح المسك»، فقال : سبحان الله ، لقد أمرهم رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم بالسواك حين أمرهم ، وهو يعلم أنه لا بد أن يكون بفم الصائم خلوف وإن استاك، وما كان بالذي يأمرهم أن ينتنوا أفواههم عمداً ، ما في ذلك من الخير شيء، بل فيه شر. في «المعجم الكبير»(۲۰ : ۷۰)، قال ابن حجر في تلخيص الحبير (۲) : (۲۰۲) : «إسناده جيد».
It says: No dislike in kohl, oil for mustache, and siwak even in the evening, as a precaution against the statement of al-Shafi'i, for according to him it is disliked in the evening because it removes the khuluf. [1] His statement: 'no dislike in kohl and oil for mustache': both are with fatha on the first letter, meaning the use of kohl (with damm) and the use of oil (with damm), both nouns. The reason for no dislike is that there is nothing contradictory to fasting in them, nor is there anything that endangers its nullification. (2) His statement: 'and siwak': meaning the use of the siwak, whether fresh or dry. [3] His statement: 'even in the evening': with fatha on the 'ayn, kasra on the shin, and tashdid on the ya; it applies to after the time of zawal (noon). (4) His statement: 'because it removes the khuluf': it is with fatha on the kha and damm: the unpleasant smell that occurs in the mouth of the fasting person. It has been reported in al-Sihah: 'Indeed, the khuluf of the mouth of the fasting person is more fragrant to Allah than musk.' Since siwak cleans the mouth and removes it, al-Shafi'i disliked it. We have the absolute hadith: 'The best of what the fasting person uses is siwak,' narrated by Ibn Majah. And there is no necessary connection between using siwak and eliminating the khuluf. (1) See: al-Tanbih (p. 46), Tuhfat al-Muhtaj (3:435), Asna al-Matalib (1:423), and others. (2) al-Ashi: from zawal to sunset; hence zuhr and asr are called the two evening prayers. See: al-Misbah al-Munir (p. 413). (3) al-Khuluf: change in the smell of the fasting person's mouth. See: al-Sihah (1:365). (4) In Sahih Muslim (2:807) and Sahih al-Bukhari (2:670) and others. (5) From Amir ibn Rabi'ah (may Allah be pleased with him) who said: I saw the Prophet (peace be upon him) using siwak while fasting countless times.' In Sahih al-Bukhari (2:682) as a mu'allaq, and Sunan Abi Dawud (1:721) and others. From Abd al-Rahman ibn Ghanam (may Allah be pleased with him) who said: I asked Mu'adh ibn Jabal: Do you use siwak while fasting? He said: Yes. I said: At what time of day do you use siwak? He said: Any time of day you wish; if you wish in the morning, and if you wish in the evening. I said: Indeed, people dislike it in the evening. He said: Why? I said: They say that the Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) said: 'The khuluf of the mouth of the fasting person is more fragrant than musk.' He said: Subhan Allah, the Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) commanded them to use siwak when he commanded them, and he knew that there must be khuluf in the mouth of the fasting person even if he uses siwak, and he would not command them to intentionally make their mouths stink; there is no good in that, rather it is evil. In al-Mu'jam al-Kabir (20:70), Ibn Hajar said in Talkhis al-Habir (2:202): 'Its chain is good.'
Source pages p. 516

Commentary
This passage is an inter-madhhab dispute between the Hanafi and Shafi'i schools regarding the use of siwak, kohl, and oil for a fasting person. The Hanafi position is that there is no dislike (karahah) in using any of these, whether during the day or in the evening (after noon). The author explains that the words 'kohl' and 'oil' refer to their application, and there is no harm because they do not break the fast nor endanger it. Regarding siwak, the Hanafis hold that it is permissible at any time, even in the evening, based on the general evidence that the Prophet (peace be upon him) encouraged siwak for the fasting person (hadith: 'The best use of the fasting person is siwak') and the story of Mu'adh ibn Jabal who saw no reason to dislike it. The Shafi'i school dislikes siwak in the evening because it removes the khuluf (the smell from the mouth of the fasting person), which is considered fragrant to Allah. The Hanafi response is that there is no necessary connection between using siwak and removing the khuluf; even if it removes it, it does not detract from the reward. The passage also cites a hadith that the Prophet (peace be upon him) used siwak while fasting, and the companions did not restrict it to a particular time. The conclusion is that the Hanafi view is stronger, as supported by authentic narrations and the practice of the righteous predecessors.

Key Terms
السواك (al-siwaak): Use of a stick or other material to clean the teeth and mouth.
الخلوف (al-khuluuf): The changed, pleasant smell in the mouth of a fasting person.
العَشِي (al-ashiyy): The time from noon (zawal) until sunset.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what is the ruling on using siwak in the evening (after noon) while fasting?
A) Makruh (disliked) because it removes the khuluf.
B) Sunnah (recommended) and not disliked.
C) Haram (forbidden) because it invalidates the fast.
D) Only permissible if the siwak is dry.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school permits and encourages siwak for the fasting person at all times, rejecting the Shafi'i dislike based on the removal of khuluf, as there is no necessary link between the two.

Traveler's Fasting: Recommended When No Hardship
Commentary on traveler's fasting preference

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on a traveler's fasting in Ramadan and apply the condition of harm to determine whether fasting or breaking is preferred.

Core Idea
A traveler's fast is recommended if no hardship; breaking is better if hardship exists.

This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah clarifies the Hanafi position on fasting while traveling. It distinguishes between a traveler who is not harmed by fasting and one who faces hardship. The commentator reconciles the prophetic hadith discouraging travel fasting with the practice of the Prophet and companions fasting on journeys.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its default ruling
The matn states 'fast of a traveler who is not harmed by it is beloved (أحب)', indicating it is recommended (mustahabb). The ruling applies specifically when no harm (ضرر) comes from fasting.
'Harm' here is interpreted as hardship (مشقة), not bodily injury.
2
Understand the qualifier 'not harmed'
The qualifier is a condition. If fasting causes hardship, the traveler is like a sick person, and breaking the fast becomes better (الأفضل الفطر). This is based on the hadith 'ليس من البر الصوم في السفر' (It is not from piety to fast in travel).
The hadith is a deterrent when hardship is present.
3
Reconcile conflicting evidence
The commentator notes that the Prophet and many companions fasted during the conquest of Mecca (Bukhari), showing that fasting is not always disliked. The preference depends on the presence of hardship.
Thus, fasting can be better when there is no hardship, especially if it helps maintain group worship.
4
Apply to practical scenarios
If a traveler can fast without undue difficulty, it is recommended to fast; if fasting causes significant hardship, breaking is better. The ruling is flexible based on the individual's condition.
This differs from the Shafi'i view that breaking is always better.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وصوم مسافر لا يضره أحب، ولا قضاء إن مات في سفره، أو مرضيه، وإن صح، أو أقام، ثم مات، فدى عنه وليه [٢] (٤) (وصوم مسافر لا يضره أحب، ولا قضاء إن مات في سفره أو مرضه): أي لا تجب الفدية، وإن صح، أو أقام، ثم مات، فدى عنه وليه [١] قوله: لا يضره؛ صفة للمسافر، وقيد به لأنه لو كان الصوم يصره فهو في حكم المريض ونحوه، فالأفضل الفطر. والمراد بالضرر المشقة، لا ضرر البدن بخصوصه، وهو محمل حديث: «ليس من البر الصوم في السفر»، أخرجه مسلم والنسائي وغيرهما، فقد ثبت: «أن رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم رأى رجلاً تحت ظل شجرة يرش عليه الماء، فقال: ما بال صاحبكم؟، فقالوا: إنه صائم»، فقال ذلك القول زجراً عن صوم المسافر، وعدم قبول الرخصة مع وجود المشقة. [٢) قوله: أحب؛ فإن الرخصة في الصوم للمسافر للترفيه والتيسير، وقد يكون ذلك في الصوم مع الجماعة، لا سيما عند عدم المضرة، ويشهد له: «أن النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم وكثيراً من أصحابه صاموا في رمضان في سفر غزوة فتح مكة»، رواه البخاري، ولو كان الأفضل الترك مطلقاً لما وقع ذلك. (۳) قوله: ولا قضاء... الخ؛ هذا الحكم وإن ذكره أكثرهم في المسافر والمريض لكنه ليس بمختص بهما، بل هو شامل للحامل والمرضع أيضاً. وبالجملة؛ المعذور بالعذر المبيح للفطر إذا مات في أيام عذره ولم يدرك عدة من أيام أخر لا تجب عليه الفدية عوض قضائه؛ لأن وجوب الفدية مبني على وجوب القضاء، ووجوبه مبني على تحقق القدرة عليه، كذا حققه في «البحر»، وغيره. [٤]قوله: فدى عنه؛ لقول ابن عمر: «من مات وعليه صيام فليطعم عنه بقدر ما فات عنه إن عاش بعده بقدره، وإلا فبقدرهما وشرط لها الإيصاء، ويصح من الثلث، وفدية كل صلاة كصوم يوم هو الصحيح [۱] [٢] بقدر ما فات عنه إن عاش بعده بقدره ", وإلا فبقدرهما): أي بقدر الصحة والإقامة، فإنه إذا فاتت عشرة أيام فأقام بعد رمضان خمسة أيام، ثم مات، أو صح بعد رمضان خمسة أيام ثم مات فعليه فدية خمسة أيام، وشرط لها الإيصاء، ويصح من الثلث.. وفدية كل صلاة كصوم يوم هو الصحيح) (1) مكان كل يوم مسكين)، أخرجه البيهقي والدارقطني، ورواه الترمذي وابن ماجة بسند ضعيف مرفوعاً إلى النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم. [۱]قوله: بعده؛ أي بعد الإقامة والصحة أو بعد رمضان. [٢]قوله: بقدره؛ أي بقدر أيام فات صومها عنه. [۳]قوله: وشرط؛ بصيغة المجهول، وضميرها إلى الفدية، والغرض وجوب الفدية، يعني يشترط لوجوب الفدية على الولي الإيصاء، وهو أن يوصي به قبل موته، فإن أصل وجوبها عليه، وأداؤه على الولي نيابة فلا يجب عليه إلا عند أمره به، فإن لم يوص به وأداه الولي من عند نفسه فهو تبرع منه يرجى قبوله بفضل الله ورحمته. [٤]قوله: ويصح من الثلث؛ أي من ثلث ماله بعد تجهيزه وتكفينه وإيفاء ديون العباد؛ لتقدم حق العبد على حق الله في أدائه؛ لاحتياجه واستغنائه، وقد ثبت في موضعه بأن تنفيذ الوصايا إنما يكون من الثلث، ولو زادت على الثلث لا تنفذ إلا بإجازة الوارث، فلو لم يكن له وارث تنفذ من كل المال، وتفصيله في «كتاب الوصايا». [0]قوله: كصوم يوم؛ قال في «البحر»: الحاصل أن ما كان عبادة بدنية فإن الوصي يطعم عنه بعد موته عن كل واجب كالفطرة، والمالية كالزكاة يخرج عنه القدر الواجب، والمركب كالحج يحج عنه رجلاً من مال الميت.
And the fast of a traveler who is not harmed by it is beloved (preferable), and there is no making up (fidyah) if he dies during his travel or sickness. But if he becomes healthy or resides, then dies, his guardian pays fidyah on his behalf. [2] (4) (And the fast of a traveler who is not harmed by it is beloved, and there is no making up if he dies during his travel or sickness): That is, fidyah is not obligatory. But if he becomes healthy or resides, then dies, his guardian pays fidyah on his behalf. [1] His saying: 'does not harm him' is a description of the traveler, and it is a qualification because if fasting harmed him, he would be in the ruling of the sick and the like, so breaking the fast is better. What is meant by harm is hardship, not harm to the body specifically. This is the interpretation of the hadith: 'It is not from piety to fast while traveling,' narrated by Muslim, al-Nasa'i, and others. It is confirmed that the Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) saw a man under the shade of a tree water being sprinkled on him, and said: 'What is the matter with your companion?' They said: 'He is fasting.' He said that statement as a deterrent against a traveler fasting and not accepting the concession when hardship is present. [2] His saying: 'beloved'; Indeed the concession in fasting for the traveler is for ease and leniency, and that may be in fasting with the congregation, especially when there is no harm. This is supported by: 'The Prophet (peace be upon him) and many of his companions fasted in Ramadan during the journey of the conquest of Mecca,' narrated by al-Bukhari. If breaking the fast was absolutely better, that would not have occurred. (3) His saying: 'And no making up...' etc.; This ruling, although most of them mentioned it for the traveler and the sick, is not specific to them, but includes the pregnant and breastfeeding woman as well. In summary: the one excused by an excuse that permits breaking the fast, if he dies during the days of his excuse and does not reach a number of other days, fidyah is not obligatory on him in place of making up; because the obligation of fidyah is based on the obligation of making up, and its obligation is based on the verification of ability upon it. This is what was verified in 'Al-Bahr' and others. [4] His saying: 'pays fidyah on his behalf'; due to the saying of Ibn 'Umar: 'Whoever dies and has fasting upon him, let someone feed on his behalf according to what he missed, if he lived after it according to its amount, otherwise according to both [i.e., the amount of health and residence], and bequest is a condition for it, and it is valid from one-third, and the fidyah of every prayer is like the fast of a day is the correct view.' [1] [2] 'according to what he missed if he lived after it according to its amount, otherwise according to both': i.e., according to health and residence. For if ten days were missed and he resided after Ramadan for five days, then died, or became healthy after Ramadan for five days then died, upon him is the fidyah of five days. Bequest is a condition for it, and it is valid from one-third. And the fidyah of every prayer is like the fast of a day is the correct view. (1) 'a poor person for every day' narrated by al-Bayhaqi and al-Daraqutni, and al-Tirmidhi and Ibn Majah narrated it with a weak chain marfu' to the Prophet (peace be upon him). [1] His saying: 'after it'; i.e., after residence and health, or after Ramadan. [2] His saying: 'according to its amount'; i.e., according to the amount of days whose fast he missed. [3] His saying: 'condition'; in the passive voice, and its pronoun refers to fidyah, and the purpose is the obligation of fidyah. That is, it is a condition for the obligation of fidyah upon the guardian that there be a bequest; that the deceased bequeath it before his death. For the origin of its obligation is upon him, and its performance by the guardian is on his behalf (by delegation), so it is not obligatory upon him except when he commands it. If he did not bequeath it and the guardian performs it from himself, it is a donation from him, and it is hoped that it will be accepted by Allah's grace and mercy. [4] His saying: 'and it is valid from one-third'; i.e., from one-third of his wealth after preparing him, shrouding him, and paying the debts of the people; because the right of the slave takes precedence over the right of Allah in its payment due to his need and Allah's self-sufficiency. It has been established in its place that the execution of bequests is only from one-third, and if it exceeds one-third, it is not executed except with the permission of the heir. If he has no heir, it is executed from all the wealth. Its details are in the 'Book of Bequests.' [0] His saying: 'like the fast of a day'; He said in 'Al-Bahr': The conclusion is that whatever is a bodily worship, the executor feeds on his behalf after his death for every obligatory act like fitrah; and financial (worship) like zakat, the obligatory amount is taken out; and composite (worship) like hajj, one man is sent from the deceased's wealth.
Source pages p. 520

Commentary
This passage is a commentary (sharh) on a matn from Al-Wiqayah, dealing with two rulings: (1) the preference for a traveler to fast when not harmed, and (2) the fidyah obligation upon the guardian if the deceased had missed fasts and later became able but died. The commentator begins by clarifying the matn's first statement: 'And the fast of a traveler who is not harmed by it is beloved (preferable).' The phrase 'not harmed' is a qualifier: if fasting causes harm, the traveler is like a sick person, and breaking the fast is better. 'Harm' here means hardship (mashaqqah), not bodily injury. This interpretation is based on the hadith from Muslim: 'It is not from piety to fast while traveling,' and the incident where the Prophet (peace be upon him) saw a man being sprinkled with water due to fasting in hardship. The commentator explains that 'beloved' indicates that the concession to break fast is for ease, but fasting may be better when there is no hardship, especially with the congregation. This is supported by the hadith of Bukhari that the Prophet and many companions fasted during the conquest of Mecca. If breaking fast were always better, this would not have occurred. Next, the commentator addresses forgiveness of fidyah if the person dies while still excused (traveling/sick). The matn states 'no making up' meaning no fidyah. This is general: whoever dies while the excuse persists, there is no fidyah because the obligation to make up never arose due to inability. If the person later becomes able (healthy/resident) and then dies, fidyah is due. The fidyah amount is based on the number of days he could have fasted after becoming able. For example, if 10 days were missed and he was able for 5 days after Ramadan, then dies, fidyah is for 5 days. The fidyah is supported by the narration of Ibn 'Umar: 'Whoever dies and has fasting upon him, let someone feed on his behalf...' This narration is a marfu' hadith but with a weak chain; however, it is acted upon in the madhhab. The commentator adds that this ruling is not limited to traveler and sick but includes pregnant and breastfeeding women. The fidyah obligation on the guardian is conditional upon the deceased having made a bequest (wasiyyah). If the deceased did not bequeath, the guardian's payment is voluntary. The fidyah is paid from one-third of the deceased's estate, after expenses of burial and debts. If there is no heir, it can be from the entire estate. The commentator concludes by generalizing: for bodily acts of worship (like missed fasts), the executor feeds for each missed day; for financial acts (like zakat), the amount is taken; and for composite acts (like hajj), a person is sent from the estate.

Key Terms
مسافر (musafir): A traveler undertaking a journey of at least 3 days (Hanafi) for permissible purposes.
ضرر (darar): Harm or hardship (مشقة) that makes fasting difficult, not mere discomfort.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, when should a traveler break his fast?
A) Always, because travel is a concession.
B) Only if fasting causes severe hardship.
C) Only if the journey is longer than 3 days.
D) Only if he is not fasting with congregation.

Key Takeaway
A traveler's fast is recommended when no hardship; breaking is better only when hardship exists.

intermediate

Fidyah for Deceased with Missed Fasts
Commentary on fidyah after death

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine the obligation and calculation of fidyah for a deceased person who missed Ramadan fasts and had an opportunity to make them up.

Core Idea
Fidyah is due only if the deceased had a period of ability to make up fasts; it requires a bequest and is limited to one-third of the estate.

This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah explains the scenarios where fidyah (compensatory feeding) becomes obligatory on behalf of a deceased Muslim. It addresses conditions such as death during the excuse versus after recovery, the role of a bequest, and the limitation of payment to one-third of the estate. The commentary also expands the ruling to pregnant and breastfeeding women.

Guided Path
1
Identify the deceased's status at death
Determine whether the person died while still excused (traveling, sick, pregnant, etc.) or after the excuse ended (became healthy, resident, etc.). If death occurred during the excuse, no fidyah is due because the obligation to make up never fully matured.
The obligation of fidyah is built on the obligation of qada, which requires ability.
2
If death occurred after ability, calculate the days with ability
Count the number of days between the end of the excuse and death during which the person could have fasted. Fidyah is due only for those days.
Example: missed 10 days, able for 5 days after Ramadan, then dies → fidyah for 5 days.
3
Check for a bequest (إيصاء)
The guardian's obligation to pay fidyah is conditional on the deceased having made a bequest instructing payment. Without a bequest, any payment by the guardian is voluntary (donation).
The bequest must be valid and executed from one-third of the estate.
4
Apply the one-third limit
The fidyah is paid from one-third of the deceased's estate, after funeral expenses and debts. If the fidyah exceeds one-third, it requires heirs' consent unless there is no heir (then full payment).
This follows general Hanafi law of bequests.
5
Generalize the ruling
The same rule applies to any bodily worship (missed fasts, prayers) and financial/combined acts (zakat, hajj). The guardian feeds for each missed fast, pays zakat, and arranges hajj from the estate.
The commentator cites Al-Bahr for this generalization.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وصوم مسافر لا يضره أحب، ولا قضاء إن مات في سفره، أو مرضيه، وإن صح، أو أقام، ثم مات، فدى عنه وليه [٢] (٤) (وصوم مسافر لا يضره أحب، ولا قضاء إن مات في سفره أو مرضه): أي لا تجب الفدية، وإن صح، أو أقام، ثم مات، فدى عنه وليه [١] قوله: لا يضره؛ صفة للمسافر، وقيد به لأنه لو كان الصوم يصره فهو في حكم المريض ونحوه، فالأفضل الفطر. والمراد بالضرر المشقة، لا ضرر البدن بخصوصه، وهو محمل حديث: «ليس من البر الصوم في السفر»، أخرجه مسلم والنسائي وغيرهما، فقد ثبت: «أن رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم رأى رجلاً تحت ظل شجرة يرش عليه الماء، فقال: ما بال صاحبكم؟، فقالوا: إنه صائم»، فقال ذلك القول زجراً عن صوم المسافر، وعدم قبول الرخصة مع وجود المشقة. [٢) قوله: أحب؛ فإن الرخصة في الصوم للمسافر للترفيه والتيسير، وقد يكون ذلك في الصوم مع الجماعة، لا سيما عند عدم المضرة، ويشهد له: «أن النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم وكثيراً من أصحابه صاموا في رمضان في سفر غزوة فتح مكة»، رواه البخاري، ولو كان الأفضل الترك مطلقاً لما وقع ذلك. (۳) قوله: ولا قضاء... الخ؛ هذا الحكم وإن ذكره أكثرهم في المسافر والمريض لكنه ليس بمختص بهما، بل هو شامل للحامل والمرضع أيضاً. وبالجملة؛ المعذور بالعذر المبيح للفطر إذا مات في أيام عذره ولم يدرك عدة من أيام أخر لا تجب عليه الفدية عوض قضائه؛ لأن وجوب الفدية مبني على وجوب القضاء، ووجوبه مبني على تحقق القدرة عليه، كذا حققه في «البحر»، وغيره. [٤]قوله: فدى عنه؛ لقول ابن عمر: «من مات وعليه صيام فليطعم عنه بقدر ما فات عنه إن عاش بعده بقدره، وإلا فبقدرهما وشرط لها الإيصاء، ويصح من الثلث، وفدية كل صلاة كصوم يوم هو الصحيح [۱] [٢] بقدر ما فات عنه إن عاش بعده بقدره ", وإلا فبقدرهما): أي بقدر الصحة والإقامة، فإنه إذا فاتت عشرة أيام فأقام بعد رمضان خمسة أيام، ثم مات، أو صح بعد رمضان خمسة أيام ثم مات فعليه فدية خمسة أيام، وشرط لها الإيصاء، ويصح من الثلث.. وفدية كل صلاة كصوم يوم هو الصحيح) (1) مكان كل يوم مسكين)، أخرجه البيهقي والدارقطني، ورواه الترمذي وابن ماجة بسند ضعيف مرفوعاً إلى النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم. [۱]قوله: بعده؛ أي بعد الإقامة والصحة أو بعد رمضان. [٢]قوله: بقدره؛ أي بقدر أيام فات صومها عنه. [۳]قوله: وشرط؛ بصيغة المجهول، وضميرها إلى الفدية، والغرض وجوب الفدية، يعني يشترط لوجوب الفدية على الولي الإيصاء، وهو أن يوصي به قبل موته، فإن أصل وجوبها عليه، وأداؤه على الولي نيابة فلا يجب عليه إلا عند أمره به، فإن لم يوص به وأداه الولي من عند نفسه فهو تبرع منه يرجى قبوله بفضل الله ورحمته. [٤]قوله: ويصح من الثلث؛ أي من ثلث ماله بعد تجهيزه وتكفينه وإيفاء ديون العباد؛ لتقدم حق العبد على حق الله في أدائه؛ لاحتياجه واستغنائه، وقد ثبت في موضعه بأن تنفيذ الوصايا إنما يكون من الثلث، ولو زادت على الثلث لا تنفذ إلا بإجازة الوارث، فلو لم يكن له وارث تنفذ من كل المال، وتفصيله في «كتاب الوصايا». [0]قوله: كصوم يوم؛ قال في «البحر»: الحاصل أن ما كان عبادة بدنية فإن الوصي يطعم عنه بعد موته عن كل واجب كالفطرة، والمالية كالزكاة يخرج عنه القدر الواجب، والمركب كالحج يحج عنه رجلاً من مال الميت.
And the fast of a traveler who is not harmed by it is beloved (preferable), and there is no making up (fidyah) if he dies during his travel or sickness. But if he becomes healthy or resides, then dies, his guardian pays fidyah on his behalf. [2] (4) (And the fast of a traveler who is not harmed by it is beloved, and there is no making up if he dies during his travel or sickness): That is, fidyah is not obligatory. But if he becomes healthy or resides, then dies, his guardian pays fidyah on his behalf. [1] His saying: 'does not harm him' is a description of the traveler, and it is a qualification because if fasting harmed him, he would be in the ruling of the sick and the like, so breaking the fast is better. What is meant by harm is hardship, not harm to the body specifically. This is the interpretation of the hadith: 'It is not from piety to fast while traveling,' narrated by Muslim, al-Nasa'i, and others. It is confirmed that the Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) saw a man under the shade of a tree water being sprinkled on him, and said: 'What is the matter with your companion?' They said: 'He is fasting.' He said that statement as a deterrent against a traveler fasting and not accepting the concession when hardship is present. [2] His saying: 'beloved'; Indeed the concession in fasting for the traveler is for ease and leniency, and that may be in fasting with the congregation, especially when there is no harm. This is supported by: 'The Prophet (peace be upon him) and many of his companions fasted in Ramadan during the journey of the conquest of Mecca,' narrated by al-Bukhari. If breaking the fast was absolutely better, that would not have occurred. (3) His saying: 'And no making up...' etc.; This ruling, although most of them mentioned it for the traveler and the sick, is not specific to them, but includes the pregnant and breastfeeding woman as well. In summary: the one excused by an excuse that permits breaking the fast, if he dies during the days of his excuse and does not reach a number of other days, fidyah is not obligatory on him in place of making up; because the obligation of fidyah is based on the obligation of making up, and its obligation is based on the verification of ability upon it. This is what was verified in 'Al-Bahr' and others. [4] His saying: 'pays fidyah on his behalf'; due to the saying of Ibn 'Umar: 'Whoever dies and has fasting upon him, let someone feed on his behalf according to what he missed, if he lived after it according to its amount, otherwise according to both [i.e., the amount of health and residence], and bequest is a condition for it, and it is valid from one-third, and the fidyah of every prayer is like the fast of a day is the correct view.' [1] [2] 'according to what he missed if he lived after it according to its amount, otherwise according to both': i.e., according to health and residence. For if ten days were missed and he resided after Ramadan for five days, then died, or became healthy after Ramadan for five days then died, upon him is the fidyah of five days. Bequest is a condition for it, and it is valid from one-third. And the fidyah of every prayer is like the fast of a day is the correct view. (1) 'a poor person for every day' narrated by al-Bayhaqi and al-Daraqutni, and al-Tirmidhi and Ibn Majah narrated it with a weak chain marfu' to the Prophet (peace be upon him). [1] His saying: 'after it'; i.e., after residence and health, or after Ramadan. [2] His saying: 'according to its amount'; i.e., according to the amount of days whose fast he missed. [3] His saying: 'condition'; in the passive voice, and its pronoun refers to fidyah, and the purpose is the obligation of fidyah. That is, it is a condition for the obligation of fidyah upon the guardian that there be a bequest; that the deceased bequeath it before his death. For the origin of its obligation is upon him, and its performance by the guardian is on his behalf (by delegation), so it is not obligatory upon him except when he commands it. If he did not bequeath it and the guardian performs it from himself, it is a donation from him, and it is hoped that it will be accepted by Allah's grace and mercy. [4] His saying: 'and it is valid from one-third'; i.e., from one-third of his wealth after preparing him, shrouding him, and paying the debts of the people; because the right of the slave takes precedence over the right of Allah in its payment due to his need and Allah's self-sufficiency. It has been established in its place that the execution of bequests is only from one-third, and if it exceeds one-third, it is not executed except with the permission of the heir. If he has no heir, it is executed from all the wealth. Its details are in the 'Book of Bequests.' [0] His saying: 'like the fast of a day'; He said in 'Al-Bahr': The conclusion is that whatever is a bodily worship, the executor feeds on his behalf after his death for every obligatory act like fitrah; and financial (worship) like zakat, the obligatory amount is taken out; and composite (worship) like hajj, one man is sent from the deceased's wealth.
Source pages p. 520

Commentary
This passage is a commentary (sharh) on a matn from Al-Wiqayah, dealing with two rulings: (1) the preference for a traveler to fast when not harmed, and (2) the fidyah obligation upon the guardian if the deceased had missed fasts and later became able but died. The commentator begins by clarifying the matn's first statement: 'And the fast of a traveler who is not harmed by it is beloved (preferable).' The phrase 'not harmed' is a qualifier: if fasting causes harm, the traveler is like a sick person, and breaking the fast is better. 'Harm' here means hardship (mashaqqah), not bodily injury. This interpretation is based on the hadith from Muslim: 'It is not from piety to fast while traveling,' and the incident where the Prophet (peace be upon him) saw a man being sprinkled with water due to fasting in hardship. The commentator explains that 'beloved' indicates that the concession to break fast is for ease, but fasting may be better when there is no hardship, especially with the congregation. This is supported by the hadith of Bukhari that the Prophet and many companions fasted during the conquest of Mecca. If breaking fast were always better, this would not have occurred. Next, the commentator addresses forgiveness of fidyah if the person dies while still excused (traveling/sick). The matn states 'no making up' meaning no fidyah. This is general: whoever dies while the excuse persists, there is no fidyah because the obligation to make up never arose due to inability. If the person later becomes able (healthy/resident) and then dies, fidyah is due. The fidyah amount is based on the number of days he could have fasted after becoming able. For example, if 10 days were missed and he was able for 5 days after Ramadan, then dies, fidyah is for 5 days. The fidyah is supported by the narration of Ibn 'Umar: 'Whoever dies and has fasting upon him, let someone feed on his behalf...' This narration is a marfu' hadith but with a weak chain; however, it is acted upon in the madhhab. The commentator adds that this ruling is not limited to traveler and sick but includes pregnant and breastfeeding women. The fidyah obligation on the guardian is conditional upon the deceased having made a bequest (wasiyyah). If the deceased did not bequeath, the guardian's payment is voluntary. The fidyah is paid from one-third of the deceased's estate, after expenses of burial and debts. If there is no heir, it can be from the entire estate. The commentator concludes by generalizing: for bodily acts of worship (like missed fasts), the executor feeds for each missed day; for financial acts (like zakat), the amount is taken; and for composite acts (like hajj), a person is sent from the estate.

Key Terms
فدية (fidyah): Compensatory feeding of a poor person for each missed fast, paid from the deceased's estate upon bequest.
إيصاء (isa'): Bequest, the deceased's instruction for fidyah to be paid.
الثلث (al-thuluth): One-third of the estate available for bequests after debts and funeral.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A person missed 10 days of Ramadan due to sickness, recovered and was healthy for 3 days, then died. He made a bequest for fidyah. How many days of fidyah are due?
A) 10 days
B) 3 days
C) 0 days
D) 7 days

Key Takeaway
Fidyah on behalf of a deceased is only due for the days they had the ability to fast after the excuse ended, and only if a bequest was made.

Completion of Fast for a Resident Who Travels or a Traveler Who Arrives
Explains completion of fast and no kaffara for breaking

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on completing a fast when a resident travels during Ramadan and explain why no kaffara is due if it is broken.

Core Idea
Once a fast is begun, it must be completed; travel permits not starting, not breaking.

This passage from the commentary on al-Wiqaya addresses two common scenarios: a resident who begins fasting then travels during the day, and a traveler who returns home before mid-morning. In both cases, the fast must be completed. However, if they intentionally break it, no expiation (kaffara) is required because of the doubt (shubha) created by travel. Understanding this distinction helps clarify how Hanafi fiqh balances obligation and leniency.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two scenarios
The passage covers (1) a resident who travels during a Ramadan day and (2) a traveler who arrives home before the major forenoon (dahwa kubra). The ruling is identical for both.
2
Extract the core obligation
In both scenarios, the fast must be completed (itmam). The reason: travel does not permit breaking a fast already started; it only permits not starting in the first place.
This is a rational proof (dalil aqli).
3
Examine the exception to kaffara
If the fast is intentionally broken, no kaffara is required due to the shubha (doubt) created by travel. Shubha is a key concept in Hanafi fiqh for mitigating punishments.
The presence of a legitimate license (even if not fully applicable) creates doubt.
4
Note the temporal boundary for the traveler
The traveler must arrive before dahwa kubra (mid-morning). If after, the license to break ends and the ruling may differ—possibly kaffara applies.
Dahwa kubra is when the sun is high; used to determine when a traveler's license expires.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كما يَجِبُ الإتمام على مقيم سافر في يوم منه ، لكن لو أفطر لا كفارة فيهما
("كما يجب الإتمام على مقيم سافر في يوم منه ، لكن لو أفطر لا كفارة" فيهما) : أي في قدوم المسافر وسفرِ المقيم .
[١]قوله : كما يجب... الخ ; يعني إن سافر مقيم في يوم من رمضان يجب عليه إتمام صوم ذلك اليوم ؛ لأنَّ السَّفَرَ لا يبيحُ الفطر، وإنما يبيحُ عدم الشروع، فإذا شَرَعَ فيه حال الإقامة، ثم سافر لَزِمَ عليه إتمامه.
[٢] قوله : لا كفارة ; أي إن أفطر المسافرُ الذي قَدِمَ وطنه قبل الضحوة الكبرى، والمقيم الذي سافر في نهار رمضانَ لا تجب الكفّارة، وإن كان الفطر عمداً لوجودِ الشبهة، وهي دارئة لها.
As completion is obligatory upon a resident who traveled during a day of it, but if he breaks it there is no expiation in both. ("As completion is obligatory upon a resident who traveled during a day of it, but if he breaks it there is no expiation" in both): meaning in the arrival of the traveler and the travel of the resident. [1] His saying: as it is obligatory... etc.; meaning if a resident traveled during a day of Ramadan, he must complete the fast of that day because travel does not permit breaking the fast, but rather permits not beginning; so if he began while resident, then traveled, completing it becomes incumbent upon him. [2] His saying: no expiation; meaning if the traveler who arrived at his home before the major forenoon (al-dahwa al-kubra) breaks his fast, or the resident who traveled during the day of Ramadan, expiation is not obligatory, even if the breaking was intentional, due to the presence of doubt (shubha) which is a preventive (dar'ia).
Source pages p. 527

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof from the commentary on al-Wiqaya. It states a legal rule: a person who is a resident and begins fasting in Ramadan, then travels during the day, must complete that fast. Similarly, a traveler who arrives at his home before mid-morning (the time when travel ends the license to break fast) must also complete. The key point is that travel does not permit breaking a fast once started; it only permits not starting. The proof is that the fast was validly commenced, so it cannot be nullified mid-day. If they intentionally break the fast, no expiation (kaffara) is required because there is a doubt (shubha) about the prohibition—the travel creates a legitimate excuse, even though breaking is sinful. The term 'dahwa kubra' refers to the time when travel ends the license. This ruling is from the Zahir al-Riwayah and is relied upon in the school.

Key Terms
إتمام (itmām): The obligation to complete the fast once begun, not break it.
كفارة (kaffāra): Expiation for intentional breaking of Ramadan fast without excuse.
شبهة (shubha): A doubt or ambiguity that prevents the application of a harsh ruling (like kaffara or hadd).
ضحوة كبرى (ḍaḥwa kubrā): The major forenoon, approximately mid-morning, when travel license ends for a traveler arriving home.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a resident begins fasting in Ramadan, then travels during the day and intentionally breaks his fast, what is the ruling?
A) He must make up the day and pay kaffara.
B) He must make up the day only, no kaffara.
C) He does not need to make up because the fast was invalid.
D) He must pay kaffara but no qada'.

Key Takeaway
When a resident travels after starting a fast, or a traveler arrives home before mid-morning, the fast must be completed; breaking it requires qada but no kaffara because of shubha.

Ruling on Qada' for Unconscious Days in Ramadan
Explains qada' for unconscious days except day of recovery

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on qada' for unconscious days in Ramadan and identify the exception for the day of recovery, citing the proof of analogy to illness.

Core Idea
Qada' is wajib for all unconscious fasting days except the day one regains consciousness, because intention is present on that day.

What happens if a person is unconscious for several days during Ramadan? The key is intention (niyya), which is required every night. The commentator explains that unconscious days – except the day the person comes to – must be made up, because intention was absent. The proof is that unconsciousness is like illness, which excuses the fast but requires makeup. This lesson unpacks the ruling and its logical basis.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling and its exception
From the passage, note that qada' is required for all unconscious days, except the day the person regains consciousness (or its night).
The exception is key: if one came to during the day, intention is presumed.
2
Locate the proof (dalil)
The commentator proofs by analogy: unconsciousness is a type of illness (marad), and illness makes the fast invalid but requires qada'.
Qiyas: the illa (effective cause) is the inability to form intention at the proper time.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
Intention (niyya) is a condition for fasting. Unconsciousness prevents intention, so the fast is invalid unless intention was made before unconsciousness. The first day is assumed valid because intention could have been made.
The exception relies on the existence of intention on the recovery day.
4
Apply the ruling to a scenario
Suppose someone is unconscious from night of 1st Ramadan until midday of 3rd Ramadan. The first two days require qada', but the third day (when they came to) does not, because they had intention at the start of the day.
This shows the practical effect of the rule.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقضى أياماً أغمي عليه فيها إلا يوماً حَدَّتَ فيه ، أو في ليلته
("وقضي أياماً " أغمي عليه فيها إلا يوماً حَدَثَ فيه ، أو في ليلته) ؛ لأنه إذا أغمي عليه أياماً لم توجد منه النِّيَّةُ فيما عدا اليومَ الأَوَّل، أما اليوم الأَوَّلُ فَالظَّاهِرُ أنه قد نوى الصوم فيه ، أقول : هذا إذا لم يذكر أنه نوى، أم لا ، أما إذا عَلِمَ أَنه نوى فلا شك في الصحة، وإن عَلِمَ أنَّه لم ينو فلا شك في عدم الصحة.
[٣] قوله : أياماً ؛ يعني إذا أغمي عليه في أيام رمضان يجب عليه قضاء تلك الأيام، وإن كان جميع الشهر ؛ لأنه نوع مرض.
[٤] قوله : يوماً ; لوجود الإمساك المقرون بالنية، فيصح صومه، ولا يجب قضاؤه.
And he makes up days in which he became unconscious except a day in which he came to during it or during its night. ("And he makes up days in which he became unconscious except a day in which he came to during it or during its night") because if he became unconscious for days, intention (niyya) is not present from him except the first day. As for the first day, it is apparent that he intended fasting in it. I say: this is if he does not mention whether he intended or not. But if he knows that he intended, there is no doubt about validity; and if he knows that he did not intend, there is no doubt about invalidity. [3] His saying: days; meaning if he became unconscious during days of Ramadan, he must make up those days, even if it is the entire month, because it is a type of illness. [4] His saying: a day; due to the existence of abstention (imsak) coupled with intention, so his fast is valid and its qada' is not obligatory.
Source pages p. 527

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof from the commentary. It addresses a person who becomes unconscious (mughma 'alayh) for some days of Ramadan. The ruling: he must make up (qada') all the days he was unconscious, except the day on which he regained consciousness (infiqa) during the day or during the preceding night. The reason is that for unconscious days, he did not have valid intention (niyya) at the proper time (from night or before dawn). However, on the day he came to, he likely had intention at the beginning of the day, so the fast is valid. The commentator adds a note: if he knows he intended (before unconsciousness) then the fast is valid; if he knows he did not intend, it is invalid. The proof is analogy to illness (marad): unconsciousness is a type of illness that prevents intention, so qada' is wajib. The exemption of the recovery day is because the combination of abstention (imsak) and intention makes the fast sound.

Key Terms
إغماء (ighmā'): Unconsciousness that prevents intention; treated as illness.
نية (niyya): Intention to fast, required for validity.
قضاء (qaḍā'): Making up the missed fast after Ramadan.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a person is unconscious for three days in Ramadan and regains consciousness during the day of the third day, which days must he make up?
A) All three days.
B) The first two days only.
C) Only the first day.
D) None, because unconsciousness is a complete excuse.

Key Takeaway
Unconsciousness is treated as an illness that prevents intention, thus requiring qada' for all days except the day one recovers consciousness during the day.

Ruling on Insanity and Fasting in Ramadan
Distinguishes between full insanity (no qada') and partial (qada')

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rule for insanity and fasting and distinguish between full and partial insanity in determining qada' obligation.

Core Idea
Full insanity removes fasting obligation; partial insanity requires qada' – dispute turns on strength of insanity as preventer vs. remover.

This passage addresses the legal effect of insanity (junun) on the obligation to fast in Ramadan. It introduces an intra-Hanafi dispute about whether a person who reaches puberty already insane must make up missed days if he recovers partially. The ruling hinges on whether the insanity is a 'preventer' (mani') or 'remover' (rafi') of obligation, which determines the required strength of insanity.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two scenarios
Note that the passage distinguishes between insanity that covers the entire month (full) and insanity that does not (partial). For full insanity, no qada' is required; for partial, qada' is obligatory.
This is the baseline ruling agreed upon.
2
Locate the intra-Hanafi dispute
The dispute arises when a person reaches puberty already insane. According to Imam Muhammad, no fast is obligatory even if the insanity is partial. According to the apparent transmission (zahir al-riwaya) of Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf, qada' is required.
The disagreement is about the onset of insanity relative to puberty.
3
Understand the conceptual distinction
The passage explains that insanity before puberty is a 'preventer' (mani') – it blocks the obligation from arising. Insanity after puberty is a 'remover' (rafi') – it removes an already established obligation. A preventer can be weak (non-covering), but a remover must be strong (covering the entire month).
This is the key legal reasoning (ta'lil).
4
Apply the distinction to the disputed scenario
For one who reaches puberty insane, the insanity is a preventer (since it was present before puberty). Therefore, even weak insanity suffices to block the obligation – this is Muhammad's view. However, the majority holds that if the person ever regains sanity after puberty even briefly, the obligation is triggered, and only full insanity can remove it.
The thamarah al-khilaf: whether qada' is required.
5
Determine the mufta bihi
The relied-upon position (mufta bihi) is the majority view: qada' is wajib for all missed days whenever insanity is partial, regardless of when it began. This is confirmed by later authorities like Ibn 'Abidin.
The source notes another weak riwaya, but the mu'tamad is clear.

And if he became insane entirely, he does not make up. [0] His saying: and if he became insane; with dammah on the jim and tashdid on the nun in the passive form from junun; meaning if he became insane before sunset from the beginning of the night, because if he was sane at the beginning of the night, then became insane for the entire Ramadan, he makes up the fast of the entire month by agreement, except the day of that night. Thus in al-Binaya. (1) (al-Binaya 3:374). And if he recovered for part of it, he makes up what passed, whether he reached puberty insane or sane, then became insane, in the apparent narration (zahir al-riwaya). (1) Insanity, if it covers the month of Ramadan, the fast drops; if it does not cover, then not, but rather qada' is obligatory. And there is no difference in this between whether he reached puberty insane or reached puberty sane then became insane. According to Muhammad: if he reaches puberty, fasting is not obligatory upon him even though it is not covering, because insanity if connected to childhood makes fasting not obligatory; so this insanity is a preventive (mani'), so a weak insanity (non-covering) suffices for prevention. But if an adult becomes insane, then it is a remover (rafi') of the obligatory fast, so it must be a strong insanity (covering). [1] His saying: part of it; meaning in some days of Ramadan. [2] His saying: the fast dropped; due to manifest hardship (haraj bayyin) in obligation. [3] His saying: no; because the cause has occurred, which is the month, and there is no hardship in obligation, as in al-Musta'ib. [4] His saying: this; meaning the obligation when not covering. [0] His saying: indeed insanity... etc. Summation: Fasting is not obligatory in childhood, so if insanity is connected to it, it is a preventive from obligation, so a weak insanity (non-covering) suffices. But if he becomes insane after puberty, then the insanity is a remover (dafi') of the obligation, because the obligation was established after puberty, then drops due to insanity, so the removing insanity must be strong, i.e., covering; so non-covering is not a remover. [6] His saying: insanity; because removing something after its establishment requires a strong effect. (1) And in another narration (riwaya ukhra): if he recovers during night or day after the time of intention has passed, qada' is not required. Ibn 'Abidin said in Radd al-Muhtar (2:82) after mentioning who authenticated each narration: the sum is that they are two authenticated positions, and the relied-upon (mu'tamad) is the obligation of qada', because it is the zahir al-riwaya and the texts are upon it.
Source pages p. 527

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute about insanity (junun) and fasting. The ruling: if someone is insane for the entire month of Ramadan, no qada' is required because the obligation falls away entirely. If he recovers for part of the month, he must make up the missed days. The dispute concerns the scenario where a person reaches puberty while insane (i.e., insane from childhood into Ramadan) — according to Imam Muhammad, no fast is obligatory even if the insanity does not cover the entire month, because the insanity is a preventative (mani') that blocks the obligation from arising. In contrast, the apparent narration (zahir al-riwaya) of Imam Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf holds that if he is not insane for the entire month, qada' is obligatory regardless of when he became insane. The reasoning: a preventative (mani') prevents the obligation and can be weak (non-covering), while a remover (rafi') removes an already established obligation and must be strong (covering the whole month). For one who is insane from childhood, the obligation never arose, so even a weak insanity suffices. For one who was sane at the start of puberty, the obligation existed, so only full insanity removes it. The commentary also mentions another riwaya where if the insane person recovers after the time for intention (night), no qada' is needed, but Ibn 'Abidin confirms that the relied-upon position is that qada' is required.

Key Terms
جنون (junūn): Insanity that prevents valid intention and excuse from fasting.
مانع (māni'): A preventive that stops the obligation from arising; here insanity from childhood.
رافع (rāfi'): A remover that cancels an already established obligation; required to be strong (covering).
ظاهر الرواية (ẓāhir al-riwāya): The apparent/established transmission from the imams, relied upon in the school.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the relied-upon position (zahir al-riwaya), a person who reaches puberty insane (from childhood) and then recovers for one day of Ramadan must:
A) Fast only that one day, no qada' for the rest.
B) Fast that one day and make up all missed days.
C) Not fast at all because he was insane at puberty.
D) Only make up the days he was insane, not the recovery day.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, the legal effect of insanity on fasting depends on whether it prevents the obligation (mani') or removes an existing obligation (rafi'), determining the required duration of insanity.

The Ruling on Saying 'للّٰه عليّ صوم' as Vow and Oath
Intra-Hanafi dispute on vow vs. oath

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi dispute on whether the phrase 'للّٰه عليّ صوم' constitutes both a vow and an oath, and identify the practical difference (thamarah al-khilaf).

Core Idea
A vow necessarily implies an oath; the literal meaning includes its necessary consequent without combining literal and figurative.

When someone says 'للّٰه عليّ صوم', does it count as a vow (nadhr), an oath (yamin), or both? This question sparked an intra-Hanafi dispute between Abu Hanifa and Muhammad on one side, and Abu Yusuf on the other. The answer hinges on whether the oath is a metaphorical meaning or a necessary consequent of the vow. This lesson unpacks the reasoning and its practical implications.

Guided Path
1
Identify the dispute
Recognize that the passage discusses an intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf: what is the legal effect of saying 'للّٰه عليّ صوم' when the speaker intends both or just an oath?
2
State each position
Abu Hanifa and Muhammad hold that both the vow and the oath are operative; Abu Yusuf holds that only one applies.
3
Analyze the key argument
The central issue is whether combining literal (vow) and metaphorical (oath) is allowed. The majority argues that the oath is a necessary consequent (lazim), not a separately intended figurative meaning, so combining is permissible.
The example of buying a relative shows that a necessary consequent (emancipation) does not require separate intention.
4
Identify the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical difference: If both are operative, the speaker must both fulfill the fast (vow) and pay expiation if the oath is broken. If only one, only one set of obligations applies.
This affects the mufti's fatwa.
5
Evaluate the tarjih
Though the text does not state a tarjih, the majority view (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad) is the relied-upon position in the school.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإذا كان اليمين معنى مجازياً يَرِدُ عليه أنه يلزم الجمع بين الحقيقة والمجاز، فلدفع هذا قيل في كتب أصولنا: ليس اليمين معنى مجازياً. فإذا قال: لله عليَّ صوم كذا، ونوى النذر واليمين أو نوى اليمين فقط، ينبغي أن لا يحمل إلا على أحدهما كما ذهب إليه أبو يوسف، فإنّه إذا أخذ كل منهما، وحكم بترتب أحكامهما كما قاله أبو حنيفة ومحمد، يلزم الجمع بين المجاز والحقيقة وهو غير جائز. وقوله: ليس معنى مجازياً؛ فيه بحث؛ فإنّه لا مخلص من كونه معنى مجازياً؛ فإن لازم الشيء يكون معنى مجازياً له. وجوابه: إنّ المراد نفي كونه معنى مجازيّاً مراد بالصيغة، وحاصله على ما في التلويح: إنَّ قوله: لله عليّ نذر بصيغته؛ لكونها موضوعة له يمين بموجبه؛ أي بل هذا الكلام نذر بصيغته يمين عُيّن بموجبه والمراد بالموجب: اللازم، كما أن شراء القريب شراء بصيغته، إعتاق بموجبه. فيخطر ببالي أن اليمين لو كانت موجبة لثبت بلا نية، كشراء القريب، بل هي معنى مجازي. لازمه المتأخر عنه، ودلالة اللفظ هي لازم معناه، لا يكون بطريق المجاز ما لم يستعمل في اللازم، ولم يرد به اللازم مع قرينة مانعة عن إرادة ما وضع له. فإنّ الحقيقة تدلّ على جزاء المعنى بالتضمّن، وعلى لازمه بالالتزام، ففهم الجزء ولازمه قد يكون من حيث أنه نفس المراد، فاللفظ حينئذ مجاز، وقد يكون من حيث أنه جزء المراد أو لازمه، فاللفظ حقيقة كما إذا فُهِمَ الجدارُ من لفظ البيت المستعمل في معناه، وفهم الشجاعة من لفظ: الأسد؛ المستعمل في السبع. قوله: إنّ شراء القريب... الخ؛ يعني إذا اشترى رجل قريبه ممن يكون ذا رحم محرم منه كالأب والابن يعتق عليه، فهذا اشتراء بالصيغة، ويلزمه إعتاقه، ولا يلزم الجمع بين الحقيقة والمجاز؛ لعدم إرادته الإعتاق، وبالجملة ثبوت اللازم لا يتوقف على الإرادة. قوله: فيخطر ببالي؛ أي بقلبي؛ أي في ردّ ذلك الجواب، وظاهره أنه من مخترعاته، وهكذا ذكره في التوضيح، قال التفتازاني في التلويح: الإشكال إنما وقع في خاطر المصنف له على التوارد، وإلا فقد نقله صاحب الكشف عن الإمام السرخسي مع الجواب عنه بوجهين: الأول: إنما لَمّا استعملت الصيغة في محلّ آخر خرجت اليمين من أن تكون مرادة، فصارت كالحقيقة المهجورة، فلا يثبت من غير نية.
If the oath (yamin) is a metaphorical meaning, then the objection arises that combining the literal and figurative is necessary; to repel this, it is said in our books of usul: the oath is not a metaphorical meaning. So if one says 'For Allah upon me is such-and-such a fast', and intends both a vow and an oath, or only an oath, then it should not be carried except upon one of them, as Abu Yusuf went; because if both are taken and the rulings of both are applied, as Abu Hanifa and Muhammad said, then combining the figurative and the literal is necessary, and that is not permissible. And his statement 'it is not a metaphorical meaning' is debatable; because there is no escape from it being a metaphorical meaning, for the necessary consequent of a thing is a metaphorical meaning of it. The answer is that what is meant is to negate that it is a metaphorical meaning intended by the expression; its summary as in al-Talwih: indeed his statement 'for Allah upon me is a vow' in its wording, since it is placed for it, is an oath by its necessary consequent; i.e., rather this speech is a vow in its wording, an oath specified by its necessary consequent, and what is meant by the necessary consequent is the necessary implication; just as the purchase of a relative is a purchase in wording but an emancipation by its necessary consequent. It occurs to my mind that if the oath were a necessary consequent, it would be established without intention, like the purchase of a relative; rather, it is a metaphorical meaning. Its necessary consequent is subsequent to it, and the indication of the wording is the necessary consequent of its meaning; it is not by way of metaphor unless it is used in the necessary consequent, and the necessary consequent is not intended due to a preventing context from intending what it was originally placed for. For the literal meaning indicates the part of the meaning by inclusion, and its necessary consequent by implication; so understanding the part and its necessary consequent may be insofar as it is the same as what is intended, in which case the wording is metaphorical; and it may be insofar as it is part of what is intended or its necessary consequent, in which case the wording is literal, just as when the wall is understood from the word 'house' used in its meaning, and when bravery is understood from the word 'lion' used for the beast. His statement: 'the purchase of a relative...' etc.; it means if a man buys his relative from someone who is a mahram such as the father or son, he is emancipated upon him; this is a purchase in wording, and emancipation is necessitated by it, and there is no necessity to combine literal and metaphorical because he did not intend emancipation; in sum, the establishment of the necessary consequent does not depend on intention. His statement: 'it occurs to my mind' means in my heart; i.e., in refuting that answer; apparently it is one of his innovations; thus it is mentioned in al-Tawdih; al-Taftazani said in al-Talwih: the problem only occurred to the mind of the author due to coincidence, otherwise the author of al-Kashf transmitted it from Imam al-Sarakhsi with an answer in two ways: the first: because when the formula was used in another context, the oath left being intended, so it became like an abandoned literal meaning, thus it is not established without intention.
Source pages p. 533

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the legal effect of the phrase 'للّٰه عليّ صوم' when the speaker intends both a nadhr (vow) and a yamin (oath) or only a yamin. The discussion is set within the context of the Qur'anic verse on expiation of oaths. The commentator begins by noting a potential objection: if 'yamin' is a metaphorical meaning of the phrase, then applying both the literal (nadhr) and figurative (yamin) meanings would be a combination of literal and figurative, which is disallowed in usul al-fiqh. To avoid this, some Hanafi usul texts assert that 'yamin' is not a metaphorical meaning. Abu Yusuf holds that the phrase should be interpreted as only one of them (i.e., either nadhr or yamin), while Abu Hanifa and Muhammad al-Shaybani hold that both meanings can be operative. The commentator then examines the claim that 'yamin' is not metaphorical, pointing out that a necessary consequent (lazim) of a literal meaning is itself a metaphorical meaning. The answer is that the negation is about the intention: the phrase may have both meanings but only one is intended by the speaker's wording. The example of buying a relative is given: the purchase is literal, but the emancipation (the necessary consequent) is established without being intended as a separate meaning. Similarly, the necessary consequent of the vow (the yamin) should be established without requiring a separate intention. The commentator then raises an objection: if yamin were merely a necessary consequent, it would be established without intention, like emancipation in the purchase of a relative; but in reality, yamin requires intention (niyyah), showing it is a metaphorical meaning. The resolution is that the necessary consequent is not intended as a separate meaning; the wording is used literally for the vow, and the yamin is its necessary implication, but it is not intended by the speaker unless indicated by context. The passage concludes with a quotation from al-Talwih explaining that the problem noted by the author was already addressed by Imam al-Sarakhsi with two answers: one being that when the phrase is used in another context (like 'for Allah upon me is a fast' without explicit 'nadhara'), it becomes like an abandoned literal meaning, so it does not give the yamin ruling without intention. This discussion illustrates the Hanafi school's nuanced approach to combining literal and metaphorical meanings in vow formulations.

Key Terms
يمين (yamin): An oath, a legally binding utterance that invokes Allah's name or attributes to affirm or deny something, incurring expiation if broken.
نذر (nadhr): A vow, where one obligates upon oneself an act of worship that was not originally obligatory, such as fasting or charity.
لازم (lazim): Necessary consequent; something that necessarily follows from a concept or utterance, e.g., emancipation necessarily follows from buying a relative.
حقيقة (haqiqah): Literal meaning; the original, direct meaning for which a word was coined.
مجاز (majaz): Figurative meaning; a meaning derived from the literal meaning by way of similarity or necessity, but not the original meaning.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the position of Abu Hanifa and Muhammad regarding the phrase 'للّٰه عليّ صوم' when the speaker intends both a vow and an oath?
A) The phrase counts as only a vow
B) The phrase counts as only an oath
C) The phrase counts as both a vow and an oath
D) The phrase is void

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi majority view treats the oath as a necessary consequent of the vow, so both rulings apply when saying 'للّٰه عليّ صوم', regardless of the speaker's individual intention.

Definition and Ruling of I'tikaf
Defining i'tikaf and its ruling with conditions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the definition of i'tikaf according to the Hanafi school and identify its ruling as sunnah muakkadah in the last ten days of Ramadan, along with its conditions.

Core Idea
I'tikaf is staying in a mosque, fasting, with intention—a confirmed sunnah for the last ten days of Ramadan.

I'tikaf is a spiritual retreat in the mosque, especially during Ramadan. The Hanafi school defines it precisely and grades its ruling based on time and intention. This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah explains the definition, the conditions, and the strength of the practice.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definition
Read the definition of i'tikaf: it is the staying (lubth) of a fasting person in a mosque of congregation (masjid jama'ah). Note the three key elements: staying, fasting, and a congregational mosque.
The attribution to the fasting person hints at the condition of fasting.
2
Determine the ruling (hukm) in different contexts
The passage states i'tikaf is sunnah muakkadah in the last ten days of Ramadan, mustahabb at other times, and becomes wajib by vow or commencement. Understand the gradation.
The detailed view is preferred over the mere 'mustahabb' position.
3
List the conditions for validity
From the definition and the text, extract the conditions: being Muslim, sane, intention, fasting, and performing i'tikaf in a mosque where congregational prayer is held (masjid jama'ah).
Fasting is explicitly mentioned as a condition; the other conditions are implied by the nature of the act.
4
Analyze the evidence (dalil) and its strength
The primary evidence is the Prophet's continuous practice (muwazabah) in the last ten days, and the hadith 'La i'tikafa illa bi sawm'. Note that the chain is weak but supported by Aisha's statement.
The Hanafi school considers the collective evidence sufficient to establish the condition of fasting.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب الاعتكاف
الاعتكاف سنّةٌ مؤكدة : وهو لَبْتُ صائم في مسجد جماعة
قال: الاعتكاف سنة مؤكدة. هذا هو الصحيح، لمّا ثبت في الصحاح والسنن أن النبي واظب عليه في العشر الأواخر من رمضان.
وقيل: مستحب، وأحسنُ الأقوال التفصيل : وهو أنه سنة مؤكدة في العشر الأواخر من رمضان، ومستحب في غيره، ويكون واجباً بالنذر وبالشروع، كذا ذكره العيني والزيلعي في شرحي «الكنز».
ثم السنية في رمضان ليست على سبيل سنن العين، بل هو سنة مؤكدة كفاية، إذا قام بها البعض سقط عن الآخرين، وقد فصلت هذا البحث بما لا مزيد عليه في رسالتي : «الإنصاف في حكم الاعتكاف».
وقوله : وهو؛ هذا تفسير المعتبر شرعاً، واللُّبْتُ بفتح اللام وتضم؛ أي المكث، وفي إضافته إلى الصائم إشارة إلى أن الصومَ شرط لتحققه؛ لحديث: «لا اعتكاف إلا بصوم»، أخرجه الدارقطني والبيهقي بسند ضعيف، وتأيد ذلك بقول عائشة: «لا اعتكاف إلا بصوم» أخرجه أبو داود والبيهقي.
Chapter of I'tikaf. I'tikaf is a confirmed sunnah: it is the staying of a fasting person in a mosque of congregation. He said: I'tikaf is a confirmed sunnah. This is the correct view, because it is established in the authentic books and sunan that the Prophet consistently performed it in the last ten days of Ramadan. And it was said: it is mustahabb, and the best of the statements is the detail: that it is a confirmed sunnah in the last ten days of Ramadan, and mustahabb at other times, and it becomes obligatory by vow and by commencement. Thus mentioned by al-Ayni and al-Zayla'i in their commentaries on al-Kanz. Then the sunniyyah (recommended status) in Ramadan is not in the way of individual sunan, rather it is a confirmed sunnah of sufficiency; if some perform it, it falls from others. And I have elaborated on this research with no further addition in my treatise 'al-Insaf fi Hukm al-I'tikaf'. And his saying 'huwa' (it is) is an interpretation of what is legally considered. Al-lubth (staying) is with fathah or dammah on the lam, meaning remaining. And in its attribution to the fasting person there is an indication that fasting is a condition for its realization, due to the hadith: 'No i'tikaf except with fasting', narrated by al-Daraqutni and al-Bayhaqi with a weak chain, and it is supported by the statement of Aisha: 'No i'tikaf except with fasting', narrated by Abu Dawud and al-Bayhaqi.
Source pages p. 537

Commentary
This passage introduces the chapter on i'tikaf. It defines i'tikaf legally as the staying (lubth) of a fasting person in a mosque where congregational prayer is held (masjid jama'ah). The ruling is stated as sunnah mu'akkadah (confirmed sunnah), based on the Prophet's continuous practice in the last ten nights of Ramadan. The commentator notes a difference of opinion: some say mustahabb, but the preferred view is the detailed one: sunnah mu'akkadah specifically in the last ten days of Ramadan, mustahabb at other times, and it becomes wajib by vow (nadhr) or by commencing the i'tikaf (shuru'). This is the view of al-Ayni and al-Zayla'i. Furthermore, the sunniyyah (recommended nature) in Ramadan is a communal sunnah (sunnah kifayah), meaning if some members of the community perform it, the obligation is lifted from the rest. The commentator then clarifies the definition: 'huwa' refers to the legally valid i'tikaf; the word 'lubth' means remaining. The condition of fasting is indicated by the attribution to the fasting person, and is supported by the hadith 'No i'tikaf except with fasting' (though with a weak chain) and the statement of Aisha. Thus, the Hanafi position is that fasting is a condition for the validity of i'tikaf.

Key Terms
اعتكاف (iʿtikāf): Legally: staying (lubth) in a mosque with the intention of worship, specifically by a fasting person in a congregational mosque.
سنة مؤكدة (sunnah muʾakkadah): A confirmed sunnah; an act that the Prophet consistently performed with slight omission, and is highly recommended.
مسجد جماعة (masjid jamāʿah): A mosque where the five daily prayers are performed in congregation.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which of the following is a condition for the validity of i'tikaf according to this passage?
A) I'tikaf must be performed in any mosque, even if no congregational prayer is held.
B) The person must be fasting.
C) The person must be in a state of ihram.
D) I'tikaf is only valid in the last ten days of Ramadan.

Key Takeaway
I'tikaf is a confirmed sunnah during the last ten days of Ramadan, requiring fasting, intention, and a congregational mosque for validity.
