Minimum Duration and Mosque Condition for I'tikaf
Explains minimum duration of i'tikaf and requirement of a congregational mosque

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the intra-Hanafi dispute on the minimum duration of i'tikaf, state the mufta-bihi view (an hour suffices), and identify the requirement that i'tikaf be in a congregational mosque.

Core Idea
The mufta-bihi view allows an hour for nafl i'tikaf, overturning the apparent matn of one day.

This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'aya explains two key rulings of i'tikaf: the minimum duration and the type of mosque required. While the main text (matn) states one day as the minimum, the commentator reveals an internal Hanafi disagreement, with Imam Muhammad allowing as little as an hour. Similarly, the mosque condition—whether a congregational mosque is required—is disputed between Abu Hanifa and his two students. Understanding these ikhtilafat and their resolution is essential for applying the school's relied-upon position.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two contested rulings
The passage addresses (a) the minimum duration of i'tikaf (one day vs. an hour) and (b) the requirement of a congregational mosque (whether it must host the five daily prayers).
These are two distinct ikhtilafat within the Hanafi school.
2
Examine the proof for the one-day minimum
The hadith of 'Umar's vow: the Prophet commanded him to fast and perform i'tikaf, implying that fasting is a condition and fasting is a day-long act. This supports Abu Hanifa's view.
This is a zanni dalil; it can be overridden by istihsan.
3
Analyze Imam Muhammad's counter-argument
Nafl acts are based on leniency and expansion (tasamuḥ wa tawsi‘a). An hour—meaning any part of time—suffices. This is reported as the apparent narration from Abu Hanifa himself.
Muhammad's view is considered a stronger transmission (ẓāhir al-riwāya).
4
Determine the mufta-bihi view
Later Hanafi authorities like al-Durr al-Mukhtar adopt Muhammad's view; i'tikaf can be as short as an hour. This is the basis for fatwa.
The fatwa is based on the more flexible opinion, making the act easier.
5
Assess the mosque condition dispute
Abu Hanifa requires a masjid jamā‘a (with imam, mu'adhdhin, and five daily prayers). Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (Sāḥibān) allow any mosque. Al-Ṭaḥāwī and others chose the latter, citing the absolute wording of the Quran.
The chosen view is that any mosque is sufficient.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بنيته. وأقله يوم، فيقضى من قطعه فيه بعد الشروع فيه يوماً بنيته". وأقله يوم" ، فيقضى من قطعه فيه بعد الشروع فيه يوماً ) : أي إذا شرع في الاعتكاف فقطعه قبل تمام يوم وليلة فعليه القضاء خلافا لمحمد له، فإِنَّ أَقلَّهُ ساعة عنده، وقد حصلت. وفي سنن أبي داود والنسائي: «إنّ عمر الله جعل عليه أن يعتكف ليلة أو يوماً في المسجد الحرام في أيام الجاهلية ، فسأل النبي الله عنه ، فقال : اعتكف وصم). ويشترط أيضاً أن يكون في المسجد ؛ ليدلّ عليه قوله : وَلَا تُبَشِرُوهُنَّ وَأَنتُمْ عَكِفُونَ في المسجد ) (۲) ، وكونه مسجد جماعة؛ أي مسجداً له إمام ومؤذن أديت فيه الصلوات الخمس أولاً مروي عن أبي حنيفة ، وعنه اشتراط أداء الخمس، وقالا : يصح في كلِّ ،مسجد واختاره الطحاوي والسروجي والإتقاني؛ لكونه موافقاً لإطلاق القرآن.
[١] قوله : بنيته ؛ أي بنية الاعتكاف وهذا شرط في سائر العبادات، فإنما الأعمال بالنيات. [٢] قوله : يوم ؛ لأنه ثبت أنّ الصومَ شرط فيه، وأقله يوم، واختار محمد له أنّ أقله ساعة ؛ لأن مبنى النفل على المسامحة والتوسعة، والمراد بالساعة جزء من الزمان لا الساعة المعروفة، وهذا هو ظاهرُ الرواية عن الإمام ، وبه يفتى. كذا في «الدر المختار»(۳).
He said: with his intention. And its minimum is a day; so if one cuts it after starting it, he makes up one day with intention. "And its minimum is a day" meaning: if he started i'tikaf and cut it before completion of a day and night, he must make it up, contrary to Muhammad, for its minimum with him is an hour, and it has occurred. In Sunan Abi Dawud and Nasa'i: 'Umar had vowed to perform i'tikaf for a night or a day in the Sacred Mosque during Jahiliyyah; he asked the Prophet about it, and he said: Perform i'tikaf and fast.' It is also required that it be in a mosque, as indicated by His saying: 'And do not approach them while you are secluded in the mosques.' And it must be a congregational mosque, i.e., a mosque with an imam and a mu'adhdhin where the five prayers are performed. This is first narrated from Abu Hanifa, and from him the condition of performing the five prayers. The two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) said: it is valid in every mosque, and al-Tahawi, al-Saruji, and al-Itqani chose this because it conforms to the absolute wording of the Quran. [1] His saying: with his intention, i.e., with the intention of i'tikaf, and this is a condition in all acts of worship, for actions are by intentions. [2] His saying: a day, because it is established that fasting is a condition in it, and its minimum is a day. Muhammad chose that its minimum is an hour because supererogatory acts are based on leniency and expansion, and what is meant by hour is a part of time, not the well-known hour. This is the apparent narration from the Imam, and it is the basis for fatwa. Thus in al-Durr al-Mukhtar.
Source pages p. 538

Commentary
This passage addresses two key rulings of i'tikaf: (1) the minimum duration, and (2) the requirement of the mosque. The main text (matn) states that the minimum of i'tikaf is one day, and if one breaks it after starting, he must make up one day. The commentator explains that this is the position of Abu Hanifa and the majority, but Imam Muhammad holds that an hour is sufficient. The proof for the one-day minimum is the hadith of 'Umar, where the Prophet instructed him to fast and perform i'tikaf, implying that i'tikaf is connected to fasting, which is of a day's duration. Muhammad's view is based on the principle that supererogatory acts (nafl) are flexible; thus a short period suffices. The commentator notes that the fatwa is given according to Muhammad's view as it is the apparent narration from Abu Hanifa. Secondly, the text requires that i'tikaf be performed in a mosque, citing the Quranic verse 'while you are secluded in the mosques'. Abu Hanifa further conditions that it be a congregational mosque (masjid jama'ah) where the five daily prayers are held, while Abu Yusuf and Muhammad allow any mosque. The commentator mentions that later scholars like al-Tahawi chose the more lenient view as it matches the absolute wording of the Quran.

Key Terms
اعتكاف (i'tikaf): Seclusion in a mosque with the intention of worship, specifically staying in the mosque for a period with niyyah.
مسجد جماعة (masjid jama'ah): A mosque where congregational prayers are held with an imam and mu'adhdhin, and preferably where the five daily prayers are performed.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the relied-upon (mufta bihi) view in the Hanafi school, what is the minimum duration of a nafl i'tikaf?
A) One day and one night
B) One day
C) An hour
D) No minimum

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school's mufta-bihi view permits a short i'tikaf of any duration, and any mosque suffices for its validity.

Permissible Exits from I'tikaf: Human Need and Friday Prayer
Rulings on permissible exits from i'tikaf

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the permissible reasons for a mu'takif to leave the mosque and apply the conditions for leaving for Friday prayer.

Core Idea
A mu'takif may leave only for unavoidable needs; Friday prayer is a legitimate exit at zawal.

I'tikaf requires staying in the mosque, but certain needs necessitate leaving. This lesson explores the hadith of Aisha defining permissible exits: natural and religious needs. Understanding what qualifies as a legitimate exit is crucial to preserving the validity of i'tikaf.

Guided Path
1
Identify the base ruling from the hadith
Read the hadith of Aisha: the mu'takif should not leave except for unavoidable needs. This establishes the general prohibition and its exception.
The hadith is narrated by the six authentic compilers.
2
Define 'human need' (hajat al-insan)
The commentary explains that need includes both natural needs (urine, stool, ghusl after wet dream) and religious needs (Eid, Friday). Distinguish these categories.
Natural needs cannot be performed in the mosque; religious needs like Friday are communal obligations.
3
Apply the ruling for Friday prayer
The mu'takif may leave at zawal (sun's decline) to attend Friday. If his mosque is far, he may leave earlier to catch the prayer.
Zawal marks the start of Friday's time in the Hanafi school.
4
Distinguish valid exits from invalid ones
Any departure without a recognized excuse invalidates i'tikaf, even if forgetful. This reinforces the importance of adhering to the conditions.
This rule is further detailed in the next lesson.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
عائشة وعن رضي الله عنها قالت: السنة على المعتكف أن لا يعود مريضاً، ولا يشهد جنازة، ولا يمس ،امرأة، ولا يباشرها، ولا يخرج لحاجة إلا لما لا بد منه، ولا اعتكاف إلا بصوم، ولا اعتكاف إلا في مسجد جامع»
(ولا يخرج منه إلا لحاجة الإنسان (۱۷۱) ، أو الجمعة وقت الزوال ، ومَن بَعُدَ مَنْزِلُهُ عنه فوقتاً يدركها (۳) ، ويصلي السنن على الخلاف)، وهو أن يصلي قبلها أربعاً، وفي رواية (۳) : سنّاً : ركعتين تحية مسجد، وأربعاً سنة ، وبعدها أربعاً عند أبي حنيفة ه ، وستا عندهما ، ولا يفسد بمكثه أكثر منه فلو خرج منه ساعة بلا عذر فسد (٤)
[E]
[ 1 ] قوله : إلا لحاجة الإنسان ؛ لحديث عائشة : كان رسولُ الله إذا اعتكف لا يدخل البيت إلا لحاجة الإنسان)، أخرجه الأئمة الستة. والمراد بالحاجة أعم من أن يكون طبعية : كبول و غائط، وغسل لو احتلم، ولا يمكنه الاغتسال في المسجد، أو تكون شرعيّة : كعيد وجمعة. كذا في «النهر» [٢]قوله : ومن بعد؛ أي إن كان منزله ؛ أي معتكفه بعيدا عن مسجد الجامع وغيره. يخرج في وقت يدرك الجمعة ويُصلِّي السنن. [٣]قوله : ولا يفسد ؛ أي لو مكث في المسجدِ الجامع أكثر من مقدارِ يُصلِّي فيه الجمعة والسنن لا يفسد اعتكافه ؛ لأنّ المسجد ؛ أي مسجد كان محل له، إلا أنه لا يستحب ؛ لأنه التزم أداءه في مسجد واحد ، فلا يتمه في مسجدين من غير ضرورة. [٤]قوله : فلو خرج ؛ أي من معتكفه بلا عذر ، وهو الحاجة الطبعية أو الشرعية يفسد اعتكافه، وإن كان ناسياً، وإن خرج لإنهدام مسجد أو لانجاء غريق يفسد أيضاً، لكن لا يأثم. كذا في «الفتح».
Aisha (may Allah be pleased with her) said: The sunnah for the mu'takif is that he does not visit the sick, nor attend a funeral, nor touch a woman, nor have physical contact with her, nor go out for any need except that which is unavoidable; there is no i'tikaf except with fasting; there is no i'tikaf except in a congregational mosque. (He should not leave it except for human need, or for Friday at the time of zawal, and one whose home is far should leave at a time to catch it, and he prays the sunan according to the difference) – which is that he prays four before it, and in one narration: two rak'ahs as tahiyyat al-masjid and four as sunnah, and after it four according to Abu Hanifa, and six according to the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad). And his staying longer does not invalidate it. So if he leaves it for an hour without excuse, it becomes invalid. [1] His saying: except for human need – due to the hadith of Aisha: 'When the Messenger of Allah performed i'tikaf, he would not enter the house except for human need.' Recorded by the six authentic compilers. What is meant by need is general, whether natural (like urine, stool, or ghusl if he had a wet dream and cannot bathe in the mosque) or shar'i (like Eid or Friday). Thus in al-Nahr. [2] His saying: and one whose home is far – i.e., if his home or his i'tikaf place is far from the congregational mosque, he goes out at a time to catch Friday and pray the sunan. [3] His saying: and his staying longer does not invalidate it – i.e., if he stays in the congregational mosque longer than the time needed to pray Friday and the sunan, his i'tikaf does not become invalid, because the mosque (any mosque) is his place. However, it is not recommended because he committed to performing it in one mosque and should not complete it in two mosques without necessity. [4] His saying: so if he leaves – i.e., from his i'tikaf place without excuse, whether natural or shar'i need, his i'tikaf becomes invalid, even if he forgot. And if he leaves due to the collapse of the mosque or to save a drowning person, it also becomes invalid but he does not sin. Thus in al-Fath.
Source pages p. 538

Commentary
This passage elaborates on the permissible exits from i'tikaf and the consequences of unauthorized departure. It begins by citing the hadith of Aisha outlining the sunnah for the mu'takif: he should avoid worldly engagements and only leave for unavoidable needs. The main text then specifies that the mu'takif may leave for 'human need' (natural or religious) and for Friday prayer at zawal (when the sun declines). For one whose place of i'tikaf is far from the Friday mosque, he may leave early enough to attend Friday. The text then discusses the supererogatory prayers (sunan) to be prayed on Friday, noting a difference: Abu Hanifa says four before and four after, while the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) say six after. Staying longer in the Friday mosque does not invalidate i'tikaf because any mosque is valid for i'tikaf, though it is not recommended to switch mosques without necessity. Finally, the passage states that if a mu'takif leaves without any legitimate excuse, his i'tikaf is invalidated, even if he left forgetfully. However, if he leaves for a genuine emergency like a collapsing mosque or saving a drowning person, it is invalid but he is not sinful. This shows the importance of adherence to the conditions of i'tikaf while acknowledging exceptional circumstances.

Key Terms
حاجة الإنسان (hajat al-insan): Essential human need, both natural (urine, stool, ghusl) and religious (Eid, Friday).
الزوال (zawal): The time when the sun crosses the meridian (zenith), i.e., just before Dhuhr, when the Friday khutbah begins.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the ruling if a mu'takif leaves the mosque to attend Friday prayer before zawal?
A) Permissible if he returns after prayer.
B) Not permissible; he must leave exactly at zawal.
C) Permissible only if his home is far.
D) Invalidates i'tikaf.

Key Takeaway
A mu'takif may only leave for unavoidable human needs or Friday prayer at the prescribed time; any other departure invalidates i'tikaf.

Intra-Hanafi Dispute on Friday Sunan and Invalidation of I'tikaf
Intra-Hanafi dispute on Friday sunan and invalidation rules

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the Hanafi positions on the number of Friday sunan during i'tikaf and determine when i'tikaf is invalidated by departure.

Core Idea
Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn differ on Friday sunan numbers; any unauthorized departure invalidates i'tikaf even if forgetful.

This passage continues the discussion on Friday during i'tikaf, now focusing on the supererogatory prayers (sunan) and the conditions for invalidation. The Hanafi school has an internal difference on the number of sunan after Friday, and the text clarifies that staying longer in the Friday mosque does not invalidate i'tikaf, but leaving without excuse does.

Guided Path
1
State the two views on Friday sunan
Abu Hanifa holds 4 rak'ahs before Friday and 4 after; Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (Sahibayn) hold 2 tahiyyat, 4 sunnah before, and 6 after. Identify each view.
The text mentions a narration from Hasan ibn Ziyad for the Sahibayn's view.
2
Apply the ruling for staying longer
If the mu'takif stays in the Friday mosque longer than needed for prayer and sunan, his i'tikaf remains valid because any mosque is valid for i'tikaf.
However, it is not recommended to change mosques without necessity.
3
Determine when departure invalidates
Any exit without a legitimate excuse (natural or religious need) invalidates i'tikaf, even if done forgetfully.
For emergencies like mosque collapse or saving a drowning person, it invalidates but no sin.
4
Analyze the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical difference between the two views on sunan numbers affects the number of rak'ahs the mu'takif prays. Both are valid within the school.
No explicit tarjih is given in this passage.

This passage elaborates on the permissible exits from i'tikaf and the consequences of unauthorized departure. It begins by citing the hadith of Aisha outlining the sunnah for the mu'takif: he should avoid worldly engagements and only leave for unavoidable needs. The main text then specifies that the mu'takif may leave for 'human need' (natural or religious) and for Friday prayer at zawal (when the sun declines). For one whose place of i'tikaf is far from the Friday mosque, he may leave early enough to attend Friday. The text then discusses the supererogatory prayers (sunan) to be prayed on Friday, noting a difference: Abu Hanifa says four before and four after, while the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) say six after. Staying longer in the Friday mosque does not invalidate i'tikaf because any mosque is valid for i'tikaf, though it is not recommended to switch mosques without necessity. Finally, the passage states that if a mu'takif leaves without any legitimate excuse, his i'tikaf is invalidated, even if he left forgetfully. However, if he leaves for a genuine emergency like a collapsing mosque or saving a drowning person, it is invalid but he is not sinful. This shows the importance of adherence to the conditions of i'tikaf while acknowledging exceptional circumstances.

Key Terms
السنن (sunan): Supererogatory prayers performed before and after Friday, with differences in number among Hanafi imams.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the ruling if a mu'takif leaves the mosque to save a drowning person?
A) His i'tikaf remains valid because it's a good deed.
B) His i'tikaf becomes invalid but he does not sin.
C) He is excused and his i'tikaf remains valid.
D) His i'tikaf is invalid and he is sinful.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school allows flexibility in the number of Friday sunan within i'tikaf, but any unauthorized departure invalidates the i'tikaf, even if forgetful.

Exiting I'tikaf: Permitted Exits and the Intra-Hanafi Dispute on Jumu'ah Sunnah
Rulings on exiting for need and Jumu'ah with intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the two categories of need that permit exiting i'tikaf and identify the difference between Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn regarding the number of sunnah rak'ahs before and after Jumu'ah.

Core Idea
A mu'takif may exit only for human or legal need; the sunnah rak'ahs for Jumu'ah are disputed within the Hanafi school.

This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah details the conditions under which a person in i'tikaf may leave the mosque. It establishes that only essential needs—both natural (like urinating) and legal (like Friday prayer)—permit exit, grounding this in a hadith of Aisha. It then presents an intra-Hanafi disagreement over how many sunnah rak'ahs the mu'takif should pray when attending Friday prayer. Understanding these rulings clarifies the balance between devotion and necessity in i'tikaf.

Guided Path
1
Identify the default rule
Start with the base ruling: the mu'takif is prohibited from leaving the mosque. The passage states 'He does not exit from it except for...' indicating the default is non-exit.
The phrase 'لا يخرج' implies a prohibition unless an exception applies.
2
Recognize the exceptions: human need and legal need
The text lists two valid reasons: (1) 'human need' (natural bodily needs, washing after wet dream if impossible in mosque) and (2) Jumu'ah prayer at the time of zawal. The commentary expands 'need' to include Eid prayers. List these categories.
Natural needs are those that cannot be performed in the mosque (e.g., using toilet). Legal needs are religious obligations like Friday prayer.
3
Analyze the evidence (dalil)
The proof is the hadith of Aisha: the Prophet would not enter his house during i'tikaf except for human need. This shows that exit is only for necessity. Identify the words 'except for human need' as the key phrase permitting exit.
Hadith is recorded by the six major hadith collectors (al-Sitta).
4
Explore the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf on sunnah prayers
When the mu'takif goes to Jumu'ah, he prays sunnah prayers. The matn says 'on the difference' (على الخلاف). The commentary details: Abu Hanifa says 4 before and 4 after; Sahibayn say 6 before (2 tahiyyah + 4 sunnah) and 6 after. Understand that this is a ikhtilaf within the school.
The tarjih (preferred view) is not stated here, but later Hanafi authorities often follow the Sahibayn or Abu Hanifa depending on the region.
5
Apply rulings on staying longer and invalidating exit
If the mu'takif stays longer in the Jumu'ah mosque than needed, his i'tikaf remains valid (since any mosque is a valid place). However, exiting without any excuse, even briefly, invalidates the i'tikaf. Emergency exits (e.g., saving a drowning person) also invalidate but without sin.
The invalidation applies even if he forgets (nasin). The text distinguishes between sin and invalidation: emergency exit invalidates but no sin.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
عنه ولا يخرج منه إلا لحاجة الإنسان (۱۷۱) ، أو الجمعة وقت الزوال ، ومَن بَعُدَ مَنْزِلُه عنه فوقتاً يدركها (۳) ، ويصلي السنن على الخلاف)، وهو أن يصلي قبلها أربعاً، وفي رواية (۳) : سنّاً : ركعتين تحية مسجد، وأربعاً سنة ، وبعدها أربعاً عند أبي حنيفة ه ، وستا عندهما ، ولا يفسد بمكثه أكثر منه فلو خرج منه ساعة بلا عذر فسد (٤)
[ 1 ] قوله : إلا لحاجة الإنسان ؛ لحديث عائشة : كان رسولُ الله إذا اعتكف لا يدخل البيت إلا لحاجة الإنسان)، أخرجه الأئمة الستة. والمراد بالحاجة أعم من أن يكون طبعية : كبول و غائط، وغسل لو احتلم، ولا يمكنه الاغتسال في المسجد، أو تكون شرعيّة : كعيد وجمعة. كذا في «النهر» [٢]قوله : ومن بعد؛ أي إن كان منزله ؛ أي معتكفه بعيدا عن مسجد الجامع وغيره. يخرج في وقت يدرك الجمعة ويُصلِّي السنن. [٣]قوله : ولا يفسد ؛ أي لو مكث في المسجدِ الجامع أكثر من مقدارِ يُصلِّي فيه الجمعة والسنن لا يفسد اعتكافه ؛ لأنّ المسجد ؛ أي مسجد كان محل له، إلا أنه لا يستحب ؛ لأنه التزم أداءه في مسجد واحد ، فلا يتمه في مسجدين من غير ضرورة. [٤]قوله : فلو خرج ؛ أي من معتكفه بلا عذر، وهو الحاجة الطبعية أو الشرعية يفسد اعتكافه، وإن كان ناسياً، وإن خرج لإنهدام مسجد أو لانجاء غريق يفسد أيضاً، لكن لا يأثم. كذا في «الفتح».
From him: He does not exit from it except for human need, or for Friday prayer at the time of zawal, and whoever's residence is far from it, he exits at a time to reach it, and he prays the sunnah prayers according to the difference: he prays before it four, and in a narration: sunnah: two rak'ahs of tahiyyat al-masjid and four sunnah, and after it four according to Abu Hanifa, and six according to them (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad). And it is not invalidated by his staying longer than that, for if he exited from it for an hour without excuse, it is invalidated. [Commentary 1] His statement: except for human need; due to the hadith of Aishah: When the Messenger of Allah performed i'tikaf, he would not enter the house except for human need. Recorded by the six imams. And by 'need' is meant general, whether natural: like urine and stool, and washing if he had a wet dream and cannot bathe in the mosque, or legal: like Eid and Friday. So in al-Nahr. [2] His statement: and whoever's residence is far; that is if his residence, i.e. his place of i'tikaf, is far from the congregational mosque and others, he exits at a time to reach Friday and prays the sunnah. [3] His statement: and it is not invalidated; that is if he stays in the congregational mosque more than the amount in which he prays Friday and the sunnah, his i'tikaf is not invalidated because the mosque, any mosque, is a place for him, except that it is not recommended because he committed to performing it in one mosque, so he does not complete it in two mosques without necessity. [4] His statement: for if he exited; that is from his place of i'tikaf without an excuse, which is natural or legal need, his i'tikaf is invalidated even if he was forgetful, and if he exited due to a mosque collapsing or saving a drowning person, it is also invalidated but he does not sin. So in al-Fath.
Source pages p. 539

Commentary
This passage presents the rulings on exiting i'tikaf. The commentator begins by stating the principle: the mu'takif may only leave the mosque for two reasons: natural human needs (urination, defecation, ritual bath after wet dream if not possible in the mosque) or legal needs (Jumu'ah prayer, Eid prayer). The proof is the hadith of Aisha where the Prophet would not enter his house during i'tikaf except for such needs. For Jumu'ah, if the mu'takif's mosque is far from the Friday mosque, he should leave early enough to attend the prayer. Then a significant intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf is explained: regarding the sunnah prayers before and after Jumu'ah. The matn states 'according to the difference'. The commentator clarifies: Abu Hanifa holds four rak'ahs before Jumu'ah (after the first call) as sunnah, while the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) hold six rak'ahs (two for tahiyyat al-masjid and four sunnah). After Jumu'ah, Abu Hanifa says four rak'ahs, the two say six. The relied-upon position is mentioned in other sources. Then the ruling on staying longer: if the mu'takif remains in the Jumu'ah mosque longer than necessary, his i'tikaf is still valid (not invalidated) because any mosque is a place for i'tikaf, but it is not recommended to change mosques without need. Finally, intentional exit without a valid excuse, even for a short time, invalidates i'tikaf, even if forgetful. Exits for emergencies like a collapsing mosque or saving a drowning person also invalidate but without sin, as the act is obligatory despite causing invalidation.

Key Terms
حاجة الإنسان (hajat al-insan): Need that permits exit from i'tikaf; includes natural needs (urine, stool, washing after wet dream) and legal needs (Eid, Friday prayers).
السنن (al-sunan): Non-obligatory prayers prayed before and after Friday prayer; their number is disputed among Hanafi imams.
الخلاف (al-khilaf): The intra-school disagreement between Abu Hanifa and his students (Sahibayn) regarding the sunnah prayers.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which of the following best describes the ruling on a mu'takif who exits the mosque to save a drowning person?
A) The i'tikaf is invalidated and he commits a sin.
B) The i'tikaf is invalidated but he does not sin.
C) The i'tikaf remains valid because the act is virtuous.
D) The i'tikaf is invalidated only if he intended to break it.

Key Takeaway
A mu'takif may exit only for necessity; the intra-Hanafi dispute over Jumu'ah sunnah rak'ahs is a legitimate ikhtilaf without a definitive tarjih in this passage.

Permissible Acts in the Mosque During I'tikaf
Ruling on permissible worldly acts for the mu'takif in the mosque.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on eating, drinking, sleeping, and buying/selling for a mu'takif, and distinguish between permissible and disliked transactions.

Core Idea
A mu'takif may perform essential worldly acts in the mosque only to the extent of necessity; commercial transactions are disliked.

During i'tikaf, a person retreats to the mosque for worship but still has basic human needs. This lesson explains which everyday activities are permitted and under what conditions, based on the Hanafi school's nuanced approach to necessity and mosque etiquette.

Guided Path
1
Identify the acts mentioned
The passage lists eating, drinking, sleeping, buying, and selling. Note that these are normally mubah (permissible) acts, but they become restricted in the mosque context.
The mu'takif is not expected to fast or abstain from all worldly activities.
2
Apply the condition of necessity
The commentator states that permission for these acts is granted only to the extent of need. The mu'takif should not bring his merchandise into the mosque—only the contract may be made if necessary.
The principle: 'ما جوز للضرورة يتقدر بقدرها' (what is permitted due to necessity is limited to its extent).
3
Differentiate between need and trade
If the mu'takif buys or sells for personal or family needs, it is permissible. However, if he does so for profit (li tijarah), it becomes makruh (disliked).
This distinction is unique to the Hanafi school.
4
Distinguish mu'takif from non-mu'takif
For a non-mu'takif, engaging in these acts without necessity is disliked, as indicated by the hadith. The mu'takif's need creates a special dispensation.
The hadith: 'Keep your children, insane, buying, selling... away from your mosques.'
5
Evaluate the hadith evidence
The hadith from Ibn Majah is ahad (zanni al-thubut) and its dalalah is general. Hanafis use istihsan to allow the mu'takif's necessity to override the general prohibition.
This illustrates how a secondary principle (istihsan) can tailor a general rule.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويأكل ويشرب وينام ويبيع ويشتري فيه بلا إحضار مبيع لا غيره
[١] قوله : ويأكل ... الخ ؛ الحاصل أنه يجوز للمعتكف الأفعال المباحةُ في المسجدِ كالأكل والشرب والنوم والبيع والشراء، فإنّه قد يحتاج إلى ذلك، لكن ينبغي أن لا يحضر متاعه فيه، فإنّ ما جوّز للضرورة يتقدّر بقدرها. [٢]قوله : يبيع ؛ وكذا كل عقد احتاج إليه لنفسه أو عياله، فلو لتجارة كره. كذا في «الدر المختار»(۲). [٣]قوله : لا غيره ؛ فإنّه يُكره لغير المعتكف ارتكاب هذه الأفعال ونحوها بلا ضرورة، يدلّ عليه حديث : «جنبوا مساجدكم صبيانكم ومجانينكم، وشراءكم وبيعكم وخصوماتكم ورفع أصواتكم وإقامة حدودكم وسلّ سيوفكم»، أخرجه ابن ماجة.
And he eats, drinks, sleeps, buys, and sells therein without bringing merchandise; not others. [Commentary 1] His statement: and he eats... etc.; the summary is that it is permissible for the mu'takif to perform permissible actions in the mosque like eating, drinking, sleeping, buying and selling, for he may need that, but he should not bring his belongings therein, for what is permitted due to necessity is restricted to its extent. [2] His statement: he sells; and likewise every contract he needs for himself or his dependents, but if for trade, it is disliked. So in al-Durr al-Mukhtar. [3] His statement: not others; for it is disliked for non-mu'takif to commit these actions and similar without necessity, as indicated by the hadith: 'Keep your children, insane, buying, selling, arguments, raising voices, establishing penalties, and drawing swords away from your mosques,' recorded by Ibn Majah.
Source pages p. 540

Commentary
This passage expands on the permissible worldly acts a mu'takif may perform in the mosque. The commentator begins by summarizing the ruling: the mu'takif is permitted to engage in normally mubah (permissible) actions such as eating, drinking, sleeping, buying, and selling, because he may genuinely need these during his retreat. However, there is a condition: he should not bring his merchandise (for sale) into the mosque; permission is limited to necessity. If he buys or sells for personal or family needs, it is allowed, but if he does it for trade and profit, it becomes makruh (disliked). The commentator then notes that for a non-mu'takif, these actions are disliked without necessity, citing the hadith from Ibn Majah which commands keeping mosques free from commerce, children, arguments, etc. This establishes general etiquette for mosques, but the mu'takif's need grants special dispensation.

Key Terms
بلا إحضار مبيع (bila ihdar mabi'): Without bringing the merchandise into the mosque; i.e., the mu'takif may contract a sale but should not physically bring goods for sale into the mosque.
لتجارة (li tijarah): For the purpose of trade and profit; this makes the otherwise permissible sale makruh for the mu'takif.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

D) Invalidates the i'tikaf.

Key Takeaway
Essential worldly acts are permissible for the mu'takif only as needed; commercial profit-making is disliked.

The Ruling on Prolonged Silence and Speaking Good in I'tikaf
Ruling on silence and speech in i'tikaf

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on prolonged silence in i'tikaf and identify the conditions that make it disliked.

Core Idea
Prolonged silence intended as worship is disliked; speak only good.

In i'tikaf, a mu'takif is expected to engage in worship, but not all forms of silence are virtuous. This lesson clarifies that prolonged silence observed as an act of worship is contrary to the Sunnah and is disliked (makruh tanzih). Instead, the mu'takif should speak what is good—speech that contains no sin, including permissible talk when needed.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The text prohibits prolonged silence (al-samt al-tawil) and commands speaking only good. Determine the legal classification (hukm) of each.
The ruling on silence is disliked (makruh tanzih) if intended as worship.
2
Locate the dalil
The commentator cites the hadith: 'No silence for a day until night' (رواه أبو داود). Identify the type of evidence and its strength.
This is a hadith ahad (zanni al-thubut), hence the ruling is not haram but makruh.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
Understand how the negation in the hadith ('لا صمات') indicates prohibition of silence as a religious practice.
The negation is for the pre-Islamic practice of vowing silence.
4
Define 'good' (khayr)
The text explains that 'good' means speech without sin, including mubah talk when there is need, not idle chatter.
Source: al-Nahr (a later Hanafi reference).
5
Apply to i'tikaf scenerio
A mu'takif should avoid prolonged silence as worship and instead engage in beneficial speech like dhikr, Qur'an, or necessary conversation.
Silence for rest or without worship intention is permissible.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولا يصمت، ولا يتكلم إلا بخير
[٤] قوله : ولا يصمت ؛ بضم الميم ، وهو السكوتُ الطويل، وكراهته إذا اعتقده قربة، فإنّه ليس في شرعنا ؛ لحديث : لا صمات يوم إلى الليل»، رواه أبو داود. [0]قوله : إلا بخير؛ وهو ما لا إثم فيه، ومنه المباح عند الحاجة إليه لا عند عدمها. كذا في «النهر»
And he does not remain silent, and he does not speak except with good. [Commentary 4] His statement: and he does not remain silent; with damma on the mim, it is prolonged silence, and its dislikeness if he considers it an act of worship, for it is not in our Shariah; due to the hadith: 'No silence for a day until night,' narrated by Abu Dawood. [0] His statement: except with good; that is what has no sin in it, including permissible speech when there is need, not when there is no need. So in al-Nahr.
Source pages p. 540

Commentary
This passage addresses two rulings regarding speech during i'tikaf. First, the mu'takif should not observe prolonged silence (samt) especially if he intends it as a form of worship. The commentator explains that 'as-samt' with a damma on the mim means extended silence. Such silence is makruh (disliked) when the person believes it to be a religious act of worship, because this practice is not part of Islamic law. The proof is the hadith reported by Abu Dawood: 'There is no silence for a day until night,' meaning the pre-Islamic practice of observing silence as a vow is prohibited. Second, the mu'takif should not speak except that which is good (khayr). The commentator defines 'good' as speech that contains no sin; this includes permissible (mubah) talk when there is a genuine need, but not idle speech without need. The source is al-Nahr. Thus, the mu'takif should engage in beneficial speech like dhikr, knowledge, or necessary conversation, and avoid sinful, useless, or overly silent behavior.

Key Terms
صمت (samt): Prolonged, intentional silence, especially if considered an act of worship.
بخير (bi khayr): Speech that contains no sin, including permissible talk when needed.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) It is a commendable practice.
B) It is disliked (makruh).
C) It is forbidden (haram).
D) It is permissible but not recommended.

Key Takeaway
Prolonged silence intended as worship is disliked; a mu'takif must speak only good and avoid innovation.

Invalidators of I'tikaf: Intercourse, Kissing, and Touching
Ruling on what invalidates i'tikaf with Quranic proof

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify which acts invalidate i'tikaf, state the condition of emission for kissing and touching, and apply the Quranic proof from Surah al-Baqarah.

Core Idea
Intercourse invalidates i'tikaf absolutely; kissing/touching only if they lead to emission.

This lesson draws from the Hanafi legal tradition to clarify which acts break the i'tikaf. While intercourse is an absolute invalidator, lesser physical contacts are treated more subtly: they only nullify the seclusion if accompanied by emission. Understanding this distinction prevents unwarranted invalidation and keeps the i'tikaf sound.

Guided Path
1
1. Identify the primary invalidator
The text states that intercourse (al-waṭʾ) invalidates i'tikaf absolutely, whether at night, forgetfully, or even if performed outside the vagina. This is the core ruling.
No exceptions are mentioned for time or forgetfulness.
2
2. Examine lesser acts: kissing, touching, thigh-fondling
These acts are considered incitements (dawāʿī) to intercourse. They only invalidate i'tikaf if they lead to emission (inzāl). Without emission, they are impermissible but do not break the i'tikaf.
The Hanafi condition of emission is strict; other schools may rule differently.
3
3. Note what does NOT invalidate
Emission through mere thought or looking (without physical contact) does not invalidate i'tikaf, even though it is prohibited. The text explicitly excludes this.
This preserves the validity of i'tikaf for unintended mental stimulation.
4
4. Apply the Quranic proof
The verse 'Do not have relations with them while you are in seclusion in the mosques' (2:187) prohibits mubāsharah (sexual intimacy). According to Hanafi uṣūl, the imperative of prohibition (nahy) indicates that the act is ḥarām and accordingly invalidates the i'tikaf.
The verse is used to prohibit intercourse and, by qiyās, its incitements.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويبطله الوطء ولو ليلاً، أو ناسياً، ووطؤه في غير فرج، أو قبلة، أو لمس إن أَنْزَلَ" وإلا فلا ، وإِن حَرم " والمرأة تعتكف في بيتها. قوله : الوطء ؛ وإن لم يكن فيه إنزال، سواءً كان في المسجد أو خارجه، دلّ عليه قوله : ( وَلَا تُبْشِرُوهُنَّ وَأَنتُمْ عَنْكِفُونَ فِي الْمَسجِدِ (١) ، وهو يفيد أنه محظور في الاعتكاف، ويلتحق به ما هو من دواعيه كالقبلة واللمس والتفخيذ إن أنزل ؛ فإنّه في معنى الجماع، فإن لم ينزل لا يفسد، لكن يحرم فعله. قوله : إن أنزل ؛ ولا يفسد بإنزاله بتفكر ونظر. قوله : وإن حرم؛ أي وإن كان ارتكابُ كلّ هذا مكروهاً تحريماً حالة الاعتكاف. قوله : تعتكف في بيتها ؛ أي يستحب لها أن تعتكف في مسجدِ بيتها ؛ لأنه أبعد الفتنة، ومبنى حالها على الستر، فلو اعتكفت في مسجد جماعة في خباء ضرب لها فيه لا بأس به لثبوتِ ذلك عن أزواج النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم في عهده، كما ثبت في «صحيح البخاري»، وغيره.
And invalidates it: intercourse even at night or forgetfully, and his intercourse in other than the vagina, or kissing, or touching if he emitted, otherwise not, even if it is impermissible. And the woman performs i'tikaf in her house. His statement: intercourse; even if there is no emission, whether in the mosque or outside it, indicated by His statement: 'And do not have relations with them while you are in seclusion in the mosques' (2:187), and it indicates that it is prohibited in i'tikaf, and attached to it are its incitements like kissing, touching, and fondling thighs if he emitted; for it is in the meaning of intercourse. If he did not emit, it does not invalidate, but its doing is impermissible. His statement: if he emitted; and it does not invalidate by emission through thought or looking. His statement: even if it is impermissible; meaning even if committing all this is severely disliked during i'tikaf. His statement: she performs i'tikaf in her house; meaning it is recommended for her to perform i'tikaf in the mosque of her house because it is farther from temptation, and her condition is based on modesty. So if she performs i'tikaf in a congregational mosque in a tent pitched for her therein, it is permissible due to its confirmation from the wives of the Prophet ﷺ during his time, as confirmed in Sahih al-Bukhari and others.
Source pages p. 541

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof on the invalidators of i'tikaf (seclusion in the mosque). The author states that intercourse invalidates i'tikaf regardless of time or forgetfulness, and even if it occurs outside the vagina. Kissing, touching, and thigh-to-thigh contact only invalidate if accompanied by emission; otherwise, they are impermissible (makruh tahrimi) but do not break the i'tikaf. The proof is the Quranic verse 'Do not have relations with them while you are in seclusion in the mosques' (2:187), which prohibits intercourse and anything leading to it. Emission through mere thought or looking does not invalidate i'tikaf. For women, it is recommended to perform i'tikaf in her house's prayer area (musalla) to avoid fitna, but she may also do so in a mosque within a tent, as the Prophet's wives did.

Key Terms
الوطء (al-waṭʾ): Sexual intercourse, specifically penetration, which invalidates i'tikaf absolutely.
المباشرة (al-mubāsharah): Physical contact between spouses during i'tikaf, prohibited by the Quranic verse.
إنزال (inzāl): Emission of seminal fluid; a condition for invalidating i'tikaf through kissing or touching.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Does a kiss during i'tikaf invalidate the i'tikaf?
A) Yes, always.
B) Yes, only if accompanied by emission.
C) No, never.
D) Only if done intentionally.

Key Takeaway
Intercourse invalidates i'tikaf unconditionally; lesser acts invalidate only when they result in emission, otherwise they are sinful but do not break the seclusion.

Vow of I'tikaf: Inclusion of Nights and Consecutiveness
Ruling on a vow to fast days of i'tikaf entails nights consecutively.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on a vow of i'tikaf for days regarding automatic inclusion of nights and required consecutiveness, and apply it to examples.

Core Idea
A vow of i'tikaf for days automatically includes nights and requires consecutiveness.

When someone vows to observe i'tikaf for a certain number of days, the Hanafi school rules that the nights are automatically included and the days must be consecutive—even if not explicitly stated. This lesson unpacks that ruling, explains the role of intention, and contrasts it with the Shafi'i position.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and ruling
The act is a vow (nadhr) to perform i'tikaf for a specified number of days. The ruling: the vow becomes wajib (obligatory) upon the person.
2
Understand automatic inclusion of nights
The phrase 'with its nights' means that when days are vowed, the nights are automatically part of the obligation, even if not mentioned. For two days, the night between them is included.
This is because i'tikaf is a continuous act; a day cannot be separated from its preceding or following night.
3
Recognize the requirement of consecutiveness
The vow is binding consecutively (walāʾ) without needing to stipulate it. Days naturally follow one another; therefore, the i'tikaf must be performed on consecutive days.
If the vower later performs them separately, the vow is not fulfilled.
4
Note the validity of limiting intention
If the vower intends only the daytime (i.e., he does not intend to include nights), the intention is valid because he intended what is the reality of i'tikaf. However, the obligation still includes nights; the intention does not change the default requirement.
The commentary clarifies that the reality of i'tikaf is daytime activity, but the night is a necessary accompaniment.
5
Compare with Shafi'i position
The Hanafi position is that days automatically include nights. The Shafi'is hold that nights are included only if explicitly stipulated. This difference has practical implications for fulfilling the vow.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
نذر اعتكاف أَيَّامٍ لزمه بلياليها ولاءً بلا شرطه، وفي يومين بليلتهما، وصح نيَّةُ النَّهار خاصة. قوله : نذر ... الخ ؛ يعني إذا نذر اعتكاف يوم فقط من دون ذكر الليل، كأن يقول: الله عليّ أن اعتكف ثلاثة أيام لزمه بلياليها على سبيل التتابع والولاء، وإن لم يشترط ، وكذا في اليومين، فإن نوى الأيام خاصة صح؛ لإرادته ما هو الحقيقة.
He vowed i'tikaf of days: it becomes binding upon him with its nights consecutively without his condition, and in two days with their night, and the intention of the day alone is valid. His statement: 'nasara... etc.' meaning: if he vowed i'tikaf of only a day without mentioning the night, like saying: 'By Allah upon me to observe i'tikaf for three days', it becomes binding upon him with its nights in the manner of consecutiveness and sequence, even if he did not stipulate, and similarly in two days. So if he intended the days only, it is valid because he intended what is the reality.
Source pages p. 541

Commentary
This passage is a ruling statement on vows of i'tikaf. The matn says: If someone vows to perform i'tikaf for days (e.g., three days), it becomes obligatory to include the nights as well, and they must be consecutive—even if he did not explicitly stipulate consecutiveness. The same applies to a vow of two days: the nights between them are included. However, if he intends only the daytime, his intention is valid because the reality of i'tikaf is daytime activity; the nights are a necessary accompaniment but not the essence. The commentary clarifies that the phrase 'without his condition' means even if he did not mention continuity, it is still required because days naturally follow nights. The mention of 'two days' is an example: two days with their intervening night.

Key Terms
نذر (nadhr): A vow obligating an act of worship; here vows of i'tikaf.
ولاء (walāʾ): Consecutiveness; required for vowed i'tikaf of days even if not stipulated.
نية (niyyah): Intention; here, intending only daytime i'tikaf is valid.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If someone vows to perform i'tikaf for 'two days' without mentioning nights, what is obligatory?
A) Only the two days, separately.
B) Two days and one night consecutively.
C) Two days and two nights consecutively.
D) Only if he intends nights.

Key Takeaway
A vow of i'tikaf for days automatically obligates the inclusion of nights and requires consecutiveness, regardless of explicit stipulation.

The Ruling on 'Umrah: Sunnah Mu'akkadah, Its Pillars, and Disliked Times
States the status, components, timing, and disliked days of 'Umrah

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on 'Umrah, identify its two pillars and required conditions, and explain why it is prohibitively disliked on the Day of 'Arafah and the four days after.

Core Idea
'Umrah is a confirmed sunnah (not fard) consisting of tawaf and sa'i, and is prohibitively disliked during the days of Hajj.

This lesson examines the Hanafi position on 'Umrah as presented in the matn of al-Wiqayah and its commentary 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah. We will explore why the Hanafi school classifies 'Umrah as sunnah mu'akkadah, distinguish its pillars from other acts, and understand the reasoning behind the disliked times. The passage also provides a proof from the hadith of 'Aisha, demonstrating the legal reasoning (istidlal) used to derive these rulings.

Guided Path
1
Identify the legal classification
What does the text say about the ruling of 'Umrah? It states 'al-'Umrah sunnah'—meaning confirmed sunnah (sunnah mu'akkadah). Note the distinction from fard and wajib.
In Hanafi usul, sunnah mu'akkadah is less binding than wajib but still recommended; omission without excuse is blameworthy but not sinful.
2
List the pillars and required acts
The passage mentions tawaf and sa'i as the two pillars. It also states that ihram is required before them, and shaving (halq) or trimming (taqsir) is required after them.
Ihram is a condition (shart) for the validity of these acts; halq/taqsir is a wajib (obligatory component) in the Hanafi school.
3
Determine the permissible time and its exceptions
'Umrah is allowed all year, but it is makruh tahrimi on the day of 'Arafah (9th Dhul-Hijjah) and on the 10th, 11th, 12th, and 13th of Dhul-Hijjah. The reason is that these are the days of the actions of Hajj.
The commentator in al-Fath specifies that the dislike is tahrimi (prohibitively disliked), not tanzihi (lightly disliked).
4
Analyze the proof (dalil)
The hadith of 'Aisha (رضي الله عنها) is cited: the Prophet (صلى الله عليه وسلم) ordered her to perform 'Umrah after completing Hajj. This indicates that 'Umrah should not be done during the Hajj days, as it can be done afterwards.
The hadith is authentic (muttafaqun 'alayh). The command is for permission, not obligation, as shown by the consensus that 'Umrah is not fard.
5
Understand the legal reason (ta'lil)
The dislike is because performing 'Umrah during the Hajj days would mix 'Umrah acts with the specific acts of Hajj (wuquf, ramy, etc.) and also it resembles assuming ihram for Hajj before its prescribed time, which is itself disliked.
The Quranic verse (2:197) about refraining from obscenity during Hajj is cited to emphasize the sanctity of those days.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والعمرة سنة وهي طواف وسعي، ولا وقوف لها ، وجازت في كل السنة، وكرهت في يوم عرفة وأربعة بعدها. قوله : وهي طواف وسعي؛ اقتصر على ذكر الركنين، ويشترط لهما الإحرام قبلهما، والحلق أو التقصير بعدهما. قوله : ولا وقوف لها؛ أي ليس فيها وقوف بعرفة ولا مزدلفة، ولا رواح إلى منى. قوله : وكرهت؛ أي تحريماً، صرح به في «الفتح». قوله : وأربعة بعدها؛ يعني يوم العاشر والحادي عشر، والثاني عشر، والثالث عشر، وذلك لأنها أيام أفعال الحج، فلا تدخل فيها أفعال العمرة؛ ولذا أمر رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم عائشة رضي الله عنها بأن تعتمر بعد الفراغ من الحج على ما ثبت في «الصحيحين» وغيرهما. فمن فرض فيهن الحج فلا رفث ولا فسوق ولا جدال في الحج. ولهذا يكره الإحرام للحج قبلها، لشبهه بالركن.
And 'Umrah is sunnah: it is tawaf and sa'i, and there is no wuquf for it, and it is permissible in all of the year, and it is disliked on the day of 'Arafah and four days after it. He said: 'it is tawaf and sa'i'; he limited himself to mentioning the two pillars, and for them ihram is required before them, and shaving or trimming after them. He said: 'and there is no wuquf for it'; meaning there is no standing at 'Arafah or Muzdalifah, nor going to Mina. He said: 'and it is disliked'; i.e. prohibitively disliked, as clarified in al-Fath. He said: 'and four days after it'; meaning the tenth, eleventh, twelfth, and thirteenth, because they are days of the actions of Hajj, so the actions of 'Umrah are not included in them; hence the Messenger of Allah صلى الله عليه وسلم ordered 'Aisha رضي الله عنها to perform 'Umrah after completing Hajj, as established in al-Sahihayn and others. 'So whoever undertakes the Hajj therein, let there be no obscenity, nor wickedness, nor wrangling in the Hajj' (2:197). And for this reason, ihram for Hajj before its time is disliked, due to its resemblance to the pillar (i.e., the prescribed time).
Source pages p. 551

Commentary
This passage begins with the ruling on 'Umrah. The matn states that 'Umrah is sunnah—meaning it is a confirmed sunnah (sunnah mu'akkadah) in the Hanafi school, not obligatory. Its two essential components (arkan) are tawaf and sa'i. The commentator notes that the author only mentioned these two pillars; in addition, ihram must precede them, and shaving (halq) or trimming (taqsir) of the hair must follow. 'Umrah has no wuquf (standing) at 'Arafah or Muzdalifah, nor any going to Mina; these are exclusive to Hajj. It is permissible to perform 'Umrah at any time of the year, but it is prohibitively disliked (makruh tahrimi) on the day of 'Arafah and the four days that follow—that is, the 10th, 11th, 12th, and 13th of Dhul-Hijjah. The reason is that these are the days of the actions of Hajj, so engaging in 'Umrah during them would conflict with the Hajj rituals. The commentator supports this with the hadith of 'Aisha (may Allah be pleased with her), who was told by the Prophet (صلى الله عليه وسلم) to perform 'Umrah after completing Hajj. The passage also includes a Quranic verse (2:197) about conduct during Hajj, and from this the commentator derives that it is disliked to assume ihram for Hajj before its prescribed time, as it resembles a premature assumption. Overall, this section clarifies the nature, timing, and restrictions of 'Umrah.

Key Terms
العمرة (al-'Umrah): A pilgrimage to the Ka'bah consisting of tawaf and sa'i, with ihram and halq/taqsir, performed at any time.
سنة (sunnah): A recommended act; in the context of 'Umrah, it is a confirmed sunnah (sunnah mu'akkadah).
طواف (tawaf): Circumambulating the Ka'bah seven times; a pillar of 'Umrah and Hajj.
سعي (sa'i): Walking between Safa and Marwah seven times; a pillar of 'Umrah and Hajj.
الإحرام (ihram): The state of ritual consecration for Hajj or 'Umrah, including wearing specific attire and observing prohibitions.
الحلق (halq): Shaving the head, an obligatory act upon completion of 'Umrah or Hajj for men.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the Hanafi school, what is the ruling on performing 'Umrah on the 11th of Dhul-Hijjah?
A) Sunnah mu'akkadah
B) Mubah
C) Makruh tahrimi
D) Haram

Key Takeaway
'Umrah is a confirmed sunnah (sunnah mu'akkadah) performed anytime except the five days of Hajj (9th-13th Dhul-Hijjah) when it is prohibitively disliked (makruh tahrimi).

Mawaqit and Prohibition of Delaying Ihram
Ruling on miqat boundaries and prohibition of delaying ihram

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the specific miqat for three regions and explain why delaying ihram beyond them is haram, citing the hadith proof and Hanafi usul principle.

Core Idea
Crossing the miqat without ihram is haram — the Prophet's command is a binding prohibition.

This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'aya establishes the fixed boundaries (mawaqit) for assuming ihram and clarifies that passing them without ihram is a serious prohibition. Understanding these boundaries is essential for every Muslim undertaking Hajj or Umrah, as ignorance does not excuse the sin.

Guided Path
1
Identify the mawaqit
The text names three mawaqit: Juhfa for Syrians, Qarn for Najdis, and Yalamlam for Yemenis. Note the geographical descriptions.
2
Determine the ruling
The phrase 'wa haruma' clearly indicates that delaying ihram beyond these points is prohibited (haram).
3
Examine the proof
The hadith 'La tujawizu al-miqaata illa bi ihram' is cited. In Hanafi usul, a command in the negative (nahy) indicates tahrim.
4
Note the exception
The text adds 'la al-taqdim', meaning it is not prohibited to assume ihram before the miqat; doing so is permissible and even recommended for precaution.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

[٢) قوله : والنجدي ؛ أي من يأتي من بلادِ نجد ميقاته قرن، وهو بالفتح : اسم موضع بينه وبين مكة اثنان وأربعون ميلاً، وقيل : غير ذلك. And the Syrian [miqat] is Juhfa, and the Najdi is Qarn, and the Yemeni is Yalamlam. And it is prohibited to delay ihram beyond them for one who intends to enter Makkah, not to precede [ihram before them]. [1] He said: Juhfa: with damma on the jim, a place between it and Makkah three stages or more. [2] He said: and the Najdi: meaning whoever comes from the land of Najd, his miqat is Qarn, and it is with fath: a place between it and Makkah forty-two miles, and it is said otherwise. [3] He said: and the Yemeni: with two fatha; meaning whoever comes from the land of Yemen, his miqat is Yalamlam, with fath of the ya, and fath of the two lams, between them a silent mim: a mountain between it and Makkah two stages. [4] He said: and it is prohibited: meaning strongly prohibited, due to the hadith: 'Do not pass the miqat except with ihram,' reported by Ibn Abi Shayba. [5] He said: not to precede: meaning preceding ihram before the mawaqit, and there are reports and traditions from the companions about that, which we have expanded in 'al-Ta'liq al-mumajjad 'ala Muwatta' Muhammad'. (1) Juhfa: a village about 220 km from Makkah. Pilgrims perform ihram from Rabigh, which is before Juhfa towards the sea, so one who does ihram from Rabigh does so before the miqat. It is said that performing ihram from it is more precautionary due to uncertainty of Juhfa's location. See: al-Durar al-Hissan (p. 20), al-Mawsu'a al-Kuwaitiyya (2:146). (2) Qarn: Qarn al-Manazil: a mountain overlooking Arafat, today called al-Sayl, about 94 km from Makkah. See: al-Durar al-Hissan (p. 20), al-Mawsu'a al-Kuwaitiyya (2:146). (3) Yalamlam: a mountain about 94 km from Makkah. See: al-Durar al-Hissan (p. 21). (4) i.e., about the mawaqit of ihram. (5) In Musannaf Ibn Abi Shayba (4:509) without mentioning Ibn Abbas, quoted by Zayla'i in Nasb al-Raya; mention of Ibn Abbas after Sa'id, similarly Suyuti in al-Jami' al-Saghir (6:390) and he declared it hasan. (6) From Umm Hakim from Umm Salama, may Allah be pleased with her, said: 'Whoever makes talbiya from al-Aqsa Mosque for Umrah, his previous sins are forgiven, so Umm Hakim rode to Jerusalem and made talbiya from there for Umrah.' In Sahih Ibn Hibban (9:14), Sunan al-Bayhaqi al-Kabir (5:30), Musnad Ahmad (9:299), Musnad Abi Ya'la (12:441), al-Mu'jam al-Kabir (17:54), and others.
Source pages p. 552

Commentary
This passage is a ruling on the mawaqit (boundaries for ihram) for those entering Makkah for Hajj or Umrah. The author states the specific miqat for three regions: for the Syrian (Sham) it is Juhfa; for the Najdi it is Qarn; and for the Yemeni it is Yalamlam. The commentary elaborates on the locations, distances, and names of these places. Then the ruling is given: it is haram (prohibited) to delay ihram beyond these points for anyone intending to enter Makkah. However, it is not prohibited to assume ihram before reaching these mawaqit (the author says 'not to precede', i.e., there is no harm in doing ihram earlier). The proof for the prohibition is the hadith 'Do not pass the miqat except with ihram', reported by Ibn Abi Shayba. The author notes that the hadith is a clear prohibition, making the act haram. The commentary also discusses the possibility of assuming ihram before the miqat, which is permissible based on reports from the Companions. The footnotes provide additional geographical details and references to the hadith literature.

Key Terms
ميقات (mīqāt): In this text, it refers to the specific boundary or place where one must assume ihram for Hajj or Umrah, set by the Prophet for different regions.
إحرام (iḥrām): The state of consecration for Hajj or Umrah, signified by specific rituals and prohibitions, assumed at the miqat.
جحفة (Juḥfa): A place about three stages from Makkah, the miqat for people of Sham (Syria).
قرن (Qarn): A mountain about 42 miles from Makkah, the miqat for people of Najd.
يلملم (Yalamlam): A mountain about two stages from Makkah, the miqat for people of Yemen.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Sunnah mu'akkadah
B) Mubah
C) Makruh tanzihi
D) Haram

Key Takeaway
The miqat boundaries are fixed by the Prophet; passing them without ihram is a major sin (haram), but one may assume ihram early for caution.

Miqat Rulings for Those Inside the Miqat and Makkah Residents
Rulings on miqat for internal residents and Makkans with proofs

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on where a resident of Makkah assumes ihram for Hajj and Umrah, and explain the concession for those inside the miqat to enter Makkah without ihram.

Core Idea
Inside the miqat, enter Makkah without ihram; for Hajj, residents start from the Haram; for Umrah, from the hill.

This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah addresses two distinct groups: those who live within the miqat boundaries but outside the Haram (ahl dakhiliha), and residents of Makkah. The rulings on where and when to assume ihram differ based on one's location and the pilgrimage being performed. The text provides the evidence and rationale behind these rules, including the practice of the Companions and the directive of the Prophet (peace be upon him) to Aisha for Umrah from al-Tan'im.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two categories of people
The passage distinguishes between 'ahl dakhiliha' (those inside the miqat but outside the Haram) and residents of Makkah (including non-Makkans who reside there). Determine which ruling applies to each.
The text uses 'ahl dakhiliha' for the first group and 'man sakana bi Makka' for the second.
2
State the ruling for ahl dakhiliha
For those inside the miqat, they may enter Makkah without ihram. Their miqat for Hajj/Umrah is the hill (outside the Haram). This is a concession due to frequent entry.
The phrase 'wa hill li ahl dakhiliha' indicates permissibility (mubah).
3
State the ruling for Makkah residents
For Hajj, the miqat is the Haram itself (they can assume ihram from within Makkah). For Umrah, the miqat is the hill (outside the Haram). The reason given is to achieve a type of travel (li yatahaqqaq naw' safar).
Residents include both original Makkans and non-Makkans who have taken up residence.
4
Identify the evidence
The proof for ahl dakhiliha is the practice of Ibn Umar (narrated by Malik). For Makkah residents, the evidence is the Prophet's command to his companions in the Farewell Pilgrimage and to Aisha to go to al-Tan'im for Umrah.
Also cited: hadith from Abu Huraira and Ibn Abbas about completing Hajj by assuming ihram from home.
5
Understand the rationale
For ahl dakhiliha, requiring ihram every time they enter Makkah would cause hardship. For Makkan residents, Hajj requires going to Arafat (in the hill), so starting from the Haram is appropriate; Umrah is performed inside the Haram, so starting from the hill creates a journey (safar) into the sacred precinct.
The principle of avoiding hardship (raf' al-haraj) is key.

And it is permissible for the people inside it [the miqat] to enter Makkah not in the state of ihram, so his miqat is the hill; meaning whoever is inside the mawaqit but outside Makkah, his miqat is the hill, i.e., outside the Haram. [4] [0] And for one who resides in Makkah, for Hajj [his miqat is] the Haram, and for Umrah the hill; because Hajj is at Arafat, and it is in the hill, so his ihram from the Haram, and Umrah is in the Haram, so his ihram from the hill, to achieve a type of travel. [1] He said: for the people inside it: meaning whoever is between him and Makkah. [2] He said: muhrim; that is established from Ibn Umar, narrated by Malik in al-Muwatta. (3) He said: so his miqat; meaning if he intends ihram for Hajj or Umrah, whoever is between Makkah and the miqat. [4] He said: and for one; whether a Makkan or a non-Makkan who resides there. [5] He said: the Haram; so he assumes ihram from the Haram, and goes out to Mina and Arafat; by this the Messenger of Allah commanded his companions during the Farewell Pilgrimage, and he commanded Aisha, may Allah be pleased with her, to assume ihram for Umrah from al-Tan'im, which is outside the Haram; all this is established in al-Sahihayn and al-Sunan. (2) And from Abu Huraira, he said: regarding His statement: 'And complete the Hajj and Umrah for Allah' (Quran 2:196), he said: from the completion of Hajj is that you assume ihram from the house of your family. In Sunan al-Bayhaqi al-Kabir (5:30), Shu'ab al-Iman (3:447), and al-Suyuti declared it hasan in al-Jami' al-Saghir (2:537); al-Tahanawi said in I'la' al-Sunan (10:26): perhaps his declaring it hasan is due to supporting evidence. And similarly, [Ibn Abbas] regarding the statement of Allah: 'And complete the Hajj and Umrah for Allah', he said: that you assume ihram from the house of your family. In al-Mustadrak (2:303), and he declared it sahih, and Musannaf Ibn Abi Shayba (3:125), Sharh Ma'ani al-Athar (2:159), al-Athar (1:101), al-Ahadith al-Mukhtara (2:221), and said: its chain is sahih. (1) Because he enters it frequently for need, and requiring ihram every time would be burdensome. See: Sharh Ibn Malik (q 66/a). (2) From Aisha, may Allah be pleased with her: I arrived in Makkah while menstruating, and I did not perform tawaf around the House nor between Safa and Marwa, so I complained about that to the Messenger of Allah, and he said: 'Undo your hair, comb, and make talbiya for Hajj and leave the Umrah,' she said: so I did, and when we completed the Hajj, the Messenger of Allah sent me with Abd al-Rahman ibn Abi Bakr to al-Tan'im, and I performed Umrah.' In Sahih Muslim (2:570), Sahih al-Bukhari (2:563), and others.
Source pages p. 553

Commentary
This passage discusses the rulings for two categories of people regarding where they assume ihram. First, for those who are already within the miqat boundaries but outside Makkah (called 'ahl dakhiliha'), it is permissible for them to enter Makkah without being in the state of ihram. Their miqat is the 'hill' (the area outside the Haram). The proof for this is the practice of Ibn Umar, narrated by Malik in al-Muwatta. The reason for this concession is that such persons frequently enter Makkah for various needs, and requiring ihram every time would cause hardship. Second, for a person who resides in Makkah (whether originally Makkan or an outsider), the miqat for Hajj is the Haram itself; that is, he can don ihram from within the Haram because Hajj involves going to Arafat which is outside the Haram, and starting from the Haram gives him the sacred start. For Umrah, however, the miqat is the hill (outside the Haram), because Umrah is performed inside the Haram, and to complete the journey, he should go out to the hill and then re-enter with ihram. The text provides supporting evidence from the Prophet's command to his companions in the Farewell Pilgrimage and to 'Aisha to assume ihram for Umrah from al-Tan'im (a place in the hill). Additionally, the hadith from Abu Huraira and Ibn Abbas mentioning 'ihram from the house of your family' is cited, indicating that it is recommended to assume ihram from one's home, but the specific rulings for residents of Makkah are adjusted as per the Sunna.

Key Terms
حل (ḥill): In this text, it means the area outside the Haram of Makkah, i.e., the non-sacred territory; the miqat for those inside the miqat and for Makkans performing Umrah.
حرم (ḥaram): The sacred territory of Makkah within which certain prohibitions apply; in this context, it is the miqat for Makkans for Hajj.
أهل داخلها (ahl dākhilihā): Those who live inside the miqat boundaries but outside the Haram, i.e., between the miqat and the Haram.
تنعيم (al-Tanʿīm): A place just outside the Haram near Makkah, from which the Prophet commanded Aisha to assume ihram for Umrah.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, where should a resident of Makkah assume ihram for Umrah?
A) From the same miqat as his homeland
B) From the Haram
C) From the hill (outside the Haram)
D) From Arafat

Key Takeaway
Inside the miqat, enter without ihram; Makkan residents: Hajj from Haram, Umrah from the hill.

Permissibility of Seeking Shade, Wearing a Money Belt, and Increasing Talbiyah in Ihram
Commentary on three permissive actions during iḥrām.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rulings on seeking shade and wearing a stitched money belt during ihram, and identify the recommended times for talbiyah, citing the evidence from the Sunnah and practices of the Companions.

Core Idea
Necessity permits what is generally prohibited: shade and stitched garments are allowed during ihram when needed; talbiyah is recommended at specific times.

During ihram, many normal actions become prohibited to maintain the state of sanctity. However, the Hanafi school, drawing on the practice of the Prophet (peace be upon him) and his Companions, makes allowances for necessity. This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah clarifies three such rulings: seeking shade, wearing a stitched money belt, and increasing talbiyah at certain moments. Understanding these concessions helps the pilgrim balance devotion with practical needs.

Guided Path
1
Identify each act and its ruling
Determine the three acts: istiẓlāl (seeking shade), shadd himyān (tying a money belt), and ikthār al-talbiyah (increasing talbiyah). The ruling for shade and money belt is mubāḥ (permissible) due to necessity; talbiyah is mustaḥabb (recommended) at specific times.
2
Locate the evidence for permissibility
For shade, cite the hadith of Umm al-Ḥuṣayn in Ṣaḥīḥ Muslim showing the Prophet allowed covering to protect from heat, and the practice of 'Uthmān having a tent pitched. For the money belt, the permission is established from a group of Companions and Successors. For talbiyah, the practice of the Prophet and the Salaf at certain moments.
These are aḥādīth āḥād, but accepted in practice.
3
Understand the basis of concession (ḍarūrah)
The general prohibition of covering the head or wearing stitched garments is lifted when there is genuine need, such as extreme heat or the need to carry money safely. This is based on the principle that necessity overrides restrictions.
The concession is tied to actual need; without it, the original prohibition applies.
4
Specify the recommended times for talbiyah
The passage mentions five occasions: after every fard ṣalāh, when ascending a high place, when descending into a valley, when meeting riders, and at dawn. These times are when the pilgrim's devotion is heightened.
Talbiyah is a form of dhikr associated with the state of iḥrām.
5
Note the cross-madhhab difference
Some schools (e.g., Mālikī) may restrict shade more strictly, but the Ḥanafī view is supported by sound narrations and the principle of necessity.
Ḥanafīs prioritize ease and practical need.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

( وأكثر التلبية متى صلى ، أو علا شرفاً ، أو هَبَطَ وادياً، أو لَقِيَ رُكْباناً ، أو أسحر 11 ] قوله : والاستظلال ؛ أي الدخولُ في ظل سقف البيت أو جداره أو الهودج أو الفسطاط أو نحو ذلك (٣) ، فقد كان لعثمان الله فسطاط يضرب له في إحرامه ، أخرجه (٤) ابن أبي شيبة. (۲) حقوه : الخصر، ومشد الإزار من الجنب . ينظر : «اللسان (٢ : ٩٤٨). (۳) فعن أم الحصين ، قالت : حججت مع رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم حجة الوداع فرأيت أسامة وبلالاً ، وأحدهما آخذ بخطام ناقة النبي ، والآخر رافع ثوبه، يستره من الحر، حتى رمى جمرة العقبة» في «صحیح مسلم (٢ : ٩٤٤). وعن جابر : «فلما كان يوم التروية توجهوا إلى منى، فأهلوا بالحج وركب رسول الله فصلی بها الظهر والعصر والمغرب والعشاء والفجر، ثم مكث قليلاً حتى طلعت الشمس وأمر بقبة من شعر عملا له بنمرة ، ... فوجد القبة قد ضربت له بنمرة فنزل بها» في «صحيح مسلم (۲ : ۸۸۹)، وغيره. (٤) فعن عقبة بن صهبان له قال : رأيت عثمان الله بالأبطح وإن فسطاطه مضروب وإن سيفه معلق بالفسطاط» في مصنف ابن أبي شيبة (۳: ۲۹۹)، وغيره.
And seeking shade under a house and a howdah, and tying a money belt around his waist, and increasing the talbiyah whenever he prays, or ascends a high place, or descends into a valley, or meets riders, or at dawn. Commentary: And seeking shade under a house and a howdah: the howdah (mahmil) is the large litter. And tying a money belt around his waist: meaning the money belt, even though it is stitched, there is no harm in tying it on his hip. And increasing the talbiyah: whenever he prays, or ascends a high place, or descends into a valley, or meets riders, or at dawn. Explanatory note [1]: The statement 'and seeking shade' means entering into the shade of the roof of a house or its wall, or the howdah, or the tent, or the like. Indeed, 'Uthman (may Allah be pleased with him) had a tent pitched for him during his ihram, as recorded by Ibn Abi Shaybah. [2] The statement 'and tying a money belt': it is a thing resembling a waistband, tied around the middle, and dirhams are placed in it. The permission for this has been established from a group of Companions and Successors due to necessity, and we have expanded upon this topic in 'al-Ta'liq al-Mumajjad'. [3] The statement 'and increase': it is the past tense of ikthar (to increase); i.e., he recites the talbiyah after every salah, when ascending a high place—which is a word with two fathahs: a high place—and when descending: i.e., going down into the valley, meaning the low place, when meeting riders—with a dammah on the ra: i.e., traveling riders—and at the onset of dawn. Ibn Abi Shaybah narrated that the Salaf used to recommend talbiyah in these situations.
Source pages p. 562

Commentary
This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah is a critical commentary on the matn of al-Wiqayah. It addresses three permissive actions during ihram. First, seeking shade under a permanent structure or a portable canopy like a howdah is allowed, citing the practice of 'Uthman ibn 'Affan who had a tent pitched for him while in ihram. This demonstrates that the general prohibition of covering or seeking shade is relaxed due to need. Second, wearing a money belt (himyan) around the waist, even though it is stitched, is permissible. Stitched garments are normally prohibited, but necessity (like carrying money) provides a license, as upheld by many Companions and Successors. The commentator refers to his larger work 'al-Ta'liq al-Mumajjad' for further detail. Third, increasing talbiyah at specific times is recommended: after every obligatory prayer, upon ascending a high place, descending into a valley, meeting travelers, and at dawn. This is based on the practice of the Prophet (peace be upon him) and the Companions. The passage thus clarifies that the strict rules of ihram are tempered by practical needs and recommended acts of devotion.

Key Terms
استظلال (istiẓlāl): Seeking shade under a roof, wall, tent, or similar covering.
هميان (himyān): A money belt, often stitched, worn around the waist to carry money.
تلبية (talbiyah): The recitation of 'Labbayk Allahumma Labbayk...' during Hajj and Umrah, especially at specified times.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what is the ruling on wearing a stitched money belt (himyan) during ihram?
A) It is strictly prohibited because stitching is not allowed.
B) It is permissible due to necessity for carrying money.
C) It is recommended but not required.
D) It is only allowed for women.

Key Takeaway
In ihram, necessity permits what is normally prohibited; the Sunnah also encourages increased devotion through talbiyah at specific moments.
